 Project development objective Ref. PAD B.2, TechnicalAnnex 3
 The developmental objective of the Program is to contribute to the sustainable development of a
 plural, decentralized, rural-agrarian system of technological innovation, guided by demand, and
 led and disseminatedby the private sector, with the purpose of increasingthe productivity and
 competitivenessof the agricultural sector through the generation and adoption of
 environmentally sound technologies.

 Project description [one-sentence summary of each component] Ref. PAD B.3.a, Technical
 Annex 4
 The Project would finance the following four components over a four year period.
   Component 1. Strengthening the Market for Innovation Services
   The aimof this component is to advancethe development of a decentralized marketfor
 professional services for agricultural innovation. It will strengthenproducer organizations as
 beneficiaries of quality services, and improve entrepreneurial capacity of private service
 providers.
   Component 2. Strengthening the National Agricultural Researchand Development System
   The aim of this component is to strengthen agricultural researchandtechnological development
 for innovation instrategic areas of national importance, and to strengtheninstitutional and
  professional competence.
  Component 3. Monitoringand Evaluation (M&E), InformationSharing, and Policy for
  Innovation
   The aimof this component is to develop the institutional capacity of the public sector to
  formulate and implement agricultural innovation policy inconjunction with the private sector,
  and regulate the quality of the NAIS.
  Component 4. Project Implementation
   The aim of Component 4 is to support the present INCAGRO-Project Management Unit (PMU)
  which would continue to be the implementing agency for Phase 11.

A. STRATEGICCONTEXTAND RATIONALE
1. CountryandSector Issues
Overview
Peru is a lower-middle-income country in the Latin America and Caribbean (LAC) region, with a per
capita gross national income (GNI) of US$2,150 (income category 111), making it eligible for a World
Bank loan. Despite experiencing significant positive growth in recent years, and having the highest
growth potential in the subregion, per capita GDP and poverty rates show little progress due to lack of
employment creation. Deep inequalities of wealth and well-being persist. The national poverty rate,
according to 2003 estimates, was 49 percent (Annex 14), an increase from 42.7 percent in 1997. Thirty
percent of Peru's population lives in rural areas, three-quarters of which are categorized as poor' (living
on less than US$2 a day), and one-half of which is categorized as extremely poor (living on less than
US$1 a day). This poverty i s most pronounced in the highlands (sierra) and the jungle (selva), where
poverty rates are nearly double that of the coast. Agricultural research capacity i s an important factor in
reducingpoverty by building food security and economic stability while a well-developed agricultural
innovation system with sound policies for efficient and effective functioning are important prerequisites
for dissemination and adoption of new innovations.

Government Strategy
According to the 2002 Country Assistance Strategy (CAS), the current Government of Peru's (GOP's)
development strategy to address the issue of poverty has three main objectives: (a) increased
competitiveness of the private sector and employment generation; (b) equity, and social justice - to
provide access to quality health, education, cultural, and basic services; and (c) institutionalization of
public sector democracy, and creating an efficient, transparent, decentralized state. Due to the high
monetary indebtedness of the Peruvian public and private sectors, the GOP, through World Bank
assistance, is attempting to strengthen the fiscal balances through controls on expenditure, improved debt
management, increased tax collection, and initiation of a national competitiveness program to address
barriers to private sector growth and increasing exports. The Bank's assistance i s directed at moderating
the overall decline in public sector funding of agricultural research, stimulating private sector funding of
the transfer of agricultural technology, and strengthening institutions. The presence of a strong economic
program and institutional infrastructure i s essential to reduce the detrimental effects of periodic economic
shocks on poverty reduction gains due to tactical investments on programs and projects related to
agricultural productivity.
As most recently articulated in the revised "Policy Letter" (Annex 15), the strategy describes the
following issues: (a) the role of the State in institutional modernization of science, technology, and
innovation inthe agrarian sector; (b) the challenges for improved competitiveness of Peruvian agriculture
inglobalization; (c) the long-termobjectives of the programand Government's commitments with respect
to institutional reforms, policies, and financing; (d) the achievements of the Program so far and the
proposal for the Phase I1Project; and (e) The Phase I1project proposal and triggers for Phase 111.

Agricultural Sector Issues
Agriculture plays a dominant role in the Peruvian economy. About 80 percent of the rural poor are
employed in agriculture and fishing. Rural poverty i s closely associated with low agncultural
productivity. Over the past several years, government policies have led to an opening of the economy,
thereby increasing pressure on the agricultural sector to be more competitive. The need to improve
competitiveness will be further increased with the finalization of trade agreements with the United States,


IIncomparison, a householdi s also definedas poor when per capita consumptionis lower than the cost of a minimumbasket of
goods and services and extremely poor when per capita consumption is lower than the cost of a minimum basket of food
necessary to maintainadequate caloric intake.


                                                           1

the European Commission, and MERCOSUR. These agreements will have immediate implications for
the development and strengthening of science, technology, and innovation policies, the level of public
investment required, research orientation, the intellectual property environment that would influence
incentives for the private sector in innovation, and the rules and regulations that affect commerce. Such
new demands on the technological competitiveness of the country pose new challenges that must be
addressedby the program (described in detail inAnnex 1).
In this regard, the public sector has a clear role in enabling and supporting the development of an
innovation system that i s characterized by institutional diversity, and that would generate the required
scientific knowledge and information appropriate to the demands of the sector. The centralization and
lack of competency to implement technology transfer and quality extension services and to identify the
bottlenecks in information systems for innovation are the leading challenges to technological
development, especially at the regional level. The institutional changes that have taken place in the last
few years are driven by an increasing awareness of a demand structured around several dimensions
requiring a more open, decentralized, and demand-oriented National Agricultural Innovation System
(NAIS). The supply system also needs to be multidimensional, specialized, and well coordinated to
ensure appropriate allocation of scarce public resources to high-priority issues and areas. This calls for
better orchestration of the NAIS to facilitate the involvement of a large number of actors, and the efficient
implementation of policies, financing, and implementation of research and extension by the various
specialized institutions.
The NAIS has had difficulties in adjusting to these new demands. Deficiencies still exist in the areas of
research, extension, and education capacities among public and private sector partners. This Adaptable
Program Loan (APL)addresses a range of issues in the NAIS. The Program i s justified since it aims to
create new opportunities and increaseagricultural competitiveness by improving innovations arisingfrom
new knowledge. Phase Iof the loan now in place has assisted the GOP in supporting reforms in this
subsector.    The institutional changes brought about through Phase I indicate the existence of a
multidimensional demand for innovation. The supply side i s also shown to be multidimensional. This
situation implies a larger window of opportunity for the reorganizationof the NAIS with the objective of
facilitating multi-actor participation and institutional specialization.   The scope of this framework,
consisting of policies and priorities, investments, and research and extension, thus requires different
disciplines, instruments, variety of inputs, and distinct mandates.
The Phase I1Project loan is expected to deepen these reforms led by the Peruvian private sector, and to
improve public sector management, while giving continued attention to social demands such as the
inclusion of indigenous populations and rural women. A substantial portion of the rural communities
living in geographically dispersed areas lacks appropriate knowledge, experience, and skills required to
become members of modernized and efficient farms and agribusinesses. To assure the participation of
traditionally excluded sectors of the population, such as indigenous populations (representing 15 percent
of the population of Peru) and rural women in general, special efforts have been made to target
investments and to ensure their active participation in the modernization efforts. For example, in Phase I,
a pilot study developed with rural communities in Huancavelica demonstrated the existence of the
demand for innovation services in certain population segments, and the need for assistance with specific
instruments. The competitive grants program, which was the instrument of choice and is successfully
being implemented in Peru, will have various subcomponents to address specific challenges posed by
various populations segments, and to integrate the urgent need to link the extension technology transfer
with adaptive and strategic research, education, mechanisms for improved information flow, and
agribusiness management skills.
The third phase of the program would consolidate systems for policy, regulation, operation and financing
of a pluralistic agricultural technology system. The system would be based on institutional pluralism both
inimplementation and financing. Farmers and other stakeholders would havean increasedparticipation in
the governance of the system. Strategic national research programs would be generating information and


                                                       2

technologies to support adaptive research and extension programs. Agricultural training institutions and
information systems would effectively support the sector technology programs. Competitive allocation of
resources would be central to financing strategies. Inthis way, Peru's agricultural researchand extension
system would evolve with a mix of public and private funding. A permanent Focal Point within the
Ministry of Agriculture would be responsible for policy, regulation, public financing and international
cooperation for agricultural technology programs.
The main results from Phase Ihave been the high degree of private group participation -- 86 private
groups participating in the implementation of subprojects, as well as the magnitude and plurality of
institutional involvement -- 200 institutions either as partners or associates are participating in the project.
Over 11,000 beneficiaries (clientes) have benefited from the Phase Iproject out of which 94% are small
producers who are organized in more than 300 associations. The project has leveraged additional
resources -- out of US$12.8 million committed for executing subprojects, 49.6% was co-financed, i.e.,
31.3% of the original target of 12% and the project has established its presence in three macro-regions
and is also operating intwo additionalregions.
With support of the project's decentralized offices, subprojects selection and preparation were conducted
with the full participation of beneficiaries and reflected the expressed demand of producers at local level.
One hundred demand-driven subprojects that were financed by INCAGRO are composed of adaptive
research (24), extension services (33), strategic research (28) and training (capacity building) or research
(16). The 100 subprojects were selected from 1017 proposals (an approval rate of about 10%) This large
number in the list of possible subprojects testifies to the fact that there i s no shortage of proposals both in
number and sub-sectoral composition.
There are also initiatives under implementation to strengthening the full chain of agricultural production
and marketing, especially export markets. The project i s supporting several agro-business initiatives --
some are now exporting their products directly to foreign markets. Other subprojects, such as organic
coffee, are in the process of certifying their products to export directly to foreign markets. There have
also been good results in the livestock area and these will be further assessed when impact evaluation i s
completed.
Reflecting the project's aim of establishing a pluralistic agricultural extension service and technology
generation system, the subprojects are being executed by producers, service providers, private sector
collaborators, and research institutions (including the National Institute for Agricultural Research and
Extension INEA, Research Institute for Peruvian Amazon--IIAP, among others). Some subprojects also
have established partnerships with internationalinstitutions and universities and research institutions from
other countries. This achievement bodes well towards establishing a pluralistic agricultural innovation
and service provision system.
Finally, the project has strengthened the idea that private sector led agricultural innovation system is
feasible. The project has also demonstrated that the public and private sector can collaboratively work
towards improving the agricultural innovation system. In some Regions there are strong collaborations
among all stakeholders in the agricultural sector. According to the extemal review conducted in July
2003, 90% of subprojects are assessed as satisfactory and, in general, the project is on its way to reaching
the triggers established for the initiation of the second phase of the project. The successful achievement
of the PDO augurs well for the second phase of the program aimed at increasing the productivity and
competitiveness of the Peruvian agricultural sector.
In response to the demands for greater local control and full participation, increased political,
administrative, and fiscal decentralization have been designed and implemented by the GOP. InPhase I,
a strategy of a gradual process of decentralization was adopted, and will be extended to the whole country
inPhase 11. The institutional sustainability of such regionalagricultural development activities is an issue
that requires certain preconditions by the regional authorities to ensure opportunities for greater
participation of civic society.     These preconditions include resource availability, current levels of

competence at the regional level, availability of communications facilities, and upgrading of essential
infrastructure.
2. Rationale for Bank Involvement
Relevant Policies and Commitment of the Government
The project, "Research and Agricultural Extension for the Innovation and Competitiveness of Peruvian
Agriculture," is Phase I1of an "APL", which was based on an agriculture sector strategy outlined in a
Policy Letter signed October 25, 1999 by the GOP that emphasized the central role of innovation in
fulfilling its sectoral objectives. That Policy Letter recognized the complementary roles of the public and
private sectors in achieving the stated objectives of the strategy. The Government's commitment to the
strategy outlined in the Policy Letter was illustrated by the successful implementation of Phase Iof the
program, begun in January 2001. The APL program received strong support from the Ministry of
Finance and the Ministry of Agriculture (MOA). Despite the substantial progress achieved in the APL
first Phase, there remains a significant unfinished APL agenda.              The Government's continued
commitment to the strategy i s outlined in the new Policy Letter (Annex 15). Public institutions would
play an important role in defining and implementing strategic and regulatory elements of the innovation
system related to the agricultural sector. The focus of this Project on private-sector-led innovation as a
means to enhance competitiveness and reduce rural poverty follows logically from the earlier stated
Government strategy.       Recognizing the need for high-quality agricultural services to improve the
competitiveness and sustainability of the rural agricultural sectoral capacity, the Government included
this project in the World Bank's Peru CAS for FY2003-07 (approved in August 2002 and updated in
2004), and a Program Human Resources Development (PHRD) grant was used for project preparation.
However, the Loan for Phase I1 has since been downsized to US$25m from the original amount of
US$SOmthat was proposed at the beginning of Phase I.       The reasons for such a reduction were the limited
fiscal space allotted in the national budget and the limitations of indebtedness allotted to the Ministry of
Agriculture.
Comparative Advantage of the Bank
Because of the Bank's involvement in the initial design phase of this APL, it i s seen as a long-term
strategic partner in the agricultural innovation system in Peru, and therefore has a comparative advantage
incontinuingits support of this endeavor. The Bank can capitalize on the lessonslearnedfrom Phase 1of
the project, and those from similar Bank-supported projects in other countries in the LAC region and
elsewhere. The Bank has considerable experience in the design and implementation of agricultural
researchand extension projects in Indiaand Kenya, and in other countries in the region such as Colombia,
Ecuador, Mexico, and Venezuela. The Bank has long been viewed as a world leader in financing and
policy support to agricultural research and development (R&D), at both the country level and
internationally (through its support to the Consultative Group on International Agricultural Research
[CGIAR]). The Bank's support to the agriculture sector i s partly responsible for the observed increase in
agricultural output in Peru, where the agricultural production index rose from 121 (1990-99) to 170
(2000-02), using 100 as the base in 1989-91. The Project follows new innovative approaches to lending
for agriculture by the Bank that are characterized by the increased role of the private sector and rural
communities, diversification of supply, diversification of funding, and institutional specialization.

Portfolio of Projects in the Rural Agricultural Sector
Inthe last two decades the Bank has assistedPeru with a number of projects relevant to the ruralagrarian
sector (Annex 2). The Bank programfor FY2002-06 has three maincomponents: (1) to create conditions
to increase economic competitiveness and productivity; (2) to improve social policy efficiency; and (3) to
create a modern, decentralized, efficient state at the service of the people. Currently, all loans in the Peru
portfolio are focused on poverty alleviation. Projects specifically targeted to rural sector poverty include:
(a) FY2003 -rural water and sanitation (US$50 million) and rural education and teacher development



                                                       4

APL (US$52.5 million); and (b) FY2004 -agricultural researchand extension APL Phase I1(US$25.0
million), and Sierra rural development (US$20 million pipeline project).
The portfolio of projects active in the rural sector of Peru and funded by various other donors (listed in
Annex 2) illustrates the active nature of the donor community in assisting the Peruvian economy. The
total investment (US$430.66 million from donors) in agriculture between 1996 and 2005, and for projects
implemented through the MOA, is US$578.48 million, of which US$147.82 million i s counterpart
funding.

3. Higher-LevelObjectivesto Whichthe ProjectContributes
Alignment with CAS
This Project strongly supports the World Bank Group's assistance strategy for Bank borrowers, and is
included in the Bank's lending program for Peru as stated in the CAS, while falling within agricultural
extension and research, the largest sector of lendingto agriculture by the Bank. It contributes to the three
central objectives of the CAS, and to the GOP's strategy o f (i)generating competitiveness and
employment, (iij equality and social justice, and (iii) creation of an efficient state that i s transparent and
decentralized, by improving the competitiveness of the agricultural sector and the creation of a
decentralized agricultural innovation system. Equity issues related to empowerment and inclusion of
marginalized groups would be dealt with in various ways, including the continuation of the special fund
for indigenous peoples and women in this project. The Project would also support the Government's
efforts to decentralize the agricultural service sector in the country, and ensure delivery of efficient
services in poverty-ridden rural areas. Overall, the Project would help Peru improve the productivity and
quality of agri-based food products, improve and modernize post-harvest processing, and increase the
efficiency of farm-to-market food chains, leading to increased income and sustainable development
within the framework of environmental and natural resource protection and other safeguards.
Alignment with Millennium Development Goals (MDGs)
The rural commitment of the APL program in general, and the Phase I1Project in particular, i s well
aligned with the Bank's MDGs for 2015, largely inthe area of eradication of extreme poverty and hunger,
and to a lesser extent to ensure environmental sustainability and to establish a global partnership for
development.      The Project would focus on improving economic opportunities for and removing
constraints on rural poor households, and address some of the issues related to agricultural employment
driven by innovations as a risk-diversification strategy and an escape from poverty.

B. PROJECTDESCRIPTION
1. LendingInstrument
Phase I1of the Adaptable Program Loan (APL)of US$25.0 million will finance the Project. This lending
instrument was selected based on the needs of the GOP as described earlier to invest in the agricultural
sector. At the initiation of the program, an APL was chosen over that of a specific investment loan (SIL)
for the following reasons: (a) institutional development of innovation systems, and agricultural R&D
itself, are long-term processes that require stable support over many years; (b) the institutions involved
require time to develop strategies, capacities, and alliances, and financing must adapt to institutional
needs as they evolve; and (c) the decentralized provision of demand-driven and co-financed services
represents a fundamental change in the Peruvianinnovation systems, and requires a period of learning and
of adjustment of program strategies as they are developed.             Phase Iexperience has shown that
implementation strategies and institutional needs do change over time, and financing has to be sufficiently
flexible to respond adequately to these changes; and (d) in light of the long period required to succeed
with reforms in the agricultural sector in general, and more specifically in innovations, it i s logical to
separate the loan credit period into three distinct phases, with identifiable triggers.



                                                       5

2. Project Objective and Phases
Program Development Objective
The developmental objective of the Program (up to 12 years and a total projected APL loan of US$
90.87million) is to contribute to the sustainable development of a plural, decentralized, rural-agrarian
system of technological innovation, guided by demand, and led and disseminated by the private sector,
with the purpose of increasing the productivity,and competitiveness of the agricultural sector through the
generation and adoption of environmentally soundtechnologies.
Inthedesignofthe program, consideration was given to supportingacentralized, public system, as inthe
past, or providing all support through the private sector. In line with stakeholder views in Peru at the
time, and consistent with evolving experiences, it was decided to support the development of a
decentralized and demand-driven system that would be co-financed by users and private partners
representing civic society.
The execution of the APL Program was designed to be implemented in three phases: (1) Phase I(IBRD
loan of 9.6 US$ million), of three-years' duration, for the establishment of a Technological Innovation
System, (2) Phase I1(proposed IBRD loan of 25.0 US$ million), of four years' duration, for the scaling
up of the system to cover all regions, and (3) Phase 111(projected IBRDloan of 56.1 US$ million), of five
years' duration, for the consolidation of the system. The total Loan amount for the three periods of the
APL will be reduced to US$9lmfrom the original estimated amount of US$116mbecauseof the present
limitations of fiscal space and the level of indebtedness allotted to the Ministry of Agriculture affecting
Phase I1of the project. The Phase I,            which was concentrated in three regions and expanded to five, has
shown that development of an innovation system with these characteristics i s feasible in Peru, and that
there i s sufficient supply and demand for the types of services beingfinanced by the Project. InPhase 11,
the Project would continue to pursue the objective of expanding the Program scope to all regions of Peru.
This broader scaling up is particularly timely within the context of the recent decentralization (in Peru).
The APL instrument implies the execution of triggers or measures of Project implementation progress,
and meeting its preset obligations in each of the phases before passing to the approval and execution of
the following phase. An External Evaluation Mission reviewed Phase Iadvances, with the specific
mission of verifying whether the triggers had beenreached.
Status of Completionof Triggersof Phase I
Triggers for the initiation of Phase I1were based on the results of an External Review (ER) after 30
months from the date of effectiveness, in July 2003. A team of four independent specialists, with
expertise in agricultural economics, agricultural research, agricultural extension, and project
administration carried out the review. The specific triggers2 for continuing with Phase I1 were: (a)
determination by the ER team that the competitive extension and adaptive research grants program is
operating effectively in three regions, and that at least 75 percent of ongoing subprojects3 had a
satisfactory rating; (b) evidence from the Project-monitoring data and confirmation by the ER team that
the private sector and other stakeholders are co-financing at least 15 percent of adaptive research and
extension subproject costs; (c) determination by the ER team that the Project has a comprehensive
monitoring and evaluation system that produces timely information necessary for program management
and evaluation; (d) presentation of priorities for a program of competitive grants to support program
development in strategic research, information, and extension service support programs; and (e) approval

Of the five triggers agreed on as stated above for initiation of Phase 11, three have been successfully
completed. A fourth (the development of plans for strategic research programs) was partially completed
(three research areas advanced and three others in progress). The reason for the partial completion of this
trigger i s that the amount of work and planning required in producing quality plans was beyond the
capacity of the small unit assigned to this task. However, the work has advanced sufficiently to ensure
that it will be continued into Phase 11. WIEA's Strategic Plan, the fifth trigger, has also been completed,
though it i s not of sufficient quality to merit approval. Nevertheless, since INIEA continues to be
financed by the Ministry of Agriculture, the Bank will maintain its dialogue with the Government over
this phase of the program to promote a reform of WIEA that will ensure its proper role as a publicly
financed institution in a modem and pluralistic innovation system. The Govemment has committed inthe
Policy Letter to support an INIEA that is specialized in the execution of researchand technology transfer,
and which would concentrate its resources in a number of strategic programs that are of a strong public
good nature. This would also require that the Ministry of Agriculture would support these strategic
programs. If these conditions are met, the Project through its Strategic Services Development Fund
(FDSE) could co-finance subprojects within these strategic programs.            Given the importance of
smallholder agriculture and its inherent problems, the Project has a special interest in financing such a
strategic program to address these problems for which WIEA i s well positioned to implement, given its
presencein various eco-regions of the country.
Specific Triggersfor Initiation of Phase 111
The specific triggers for the initiation of Phase 111 would be: (a) Establishment of an Agricultural
Technology Focal Point (ATFP) within the MOA, that would be responsible for regulation, priority
setting, and public financing of agricultural technology programs as well as a framework on intellectual
property rights; (b) Positive evaluation of the formation and implementation of action plans of the Macro
Regional Consultative Councils with the participation of the representatives of Regional Governments,
institutions of development, and producers organizations; (c) Positive evaluation of the implementation of
the competitive grants program for extension and adaptive research in all regions and in at least 75
percent of the subprojects; (d) Positive evaluation of strategic research programs in at least three strategic
areas including various product chains implemented in centers of excellence; (e) strategic alliances
formed by public and private sector agents and other stakeholders are co-financing (in monetary terms) at
least 30 percent of adaptive research and extension subproject costs and at least 10 percent in strategic
subprojects.
3. Project Development Objective and Key Indicators
Project Development Objective, Phase II (2005-2009)
The overall objective of Phase I1 i s to contribute to the expansion, strengthening, and institutional
development of the rural agricultural technology and innovation system so it is pluralistic, decentralized,
demand driven, and led by the private sector.          During Phase 11, the project will also establish a
Technological Innovation Policy Focal Point within the M O A that will be responsible for policy
formulation and coordination of public investments, which will contribute to the sustainability of the
agricultural innovation system.

The overall impact4 of the Phase I1 Project would be evaluated using the following seven key
performance indicators: (i)46,000 producers (direct participants, female and indigenous participants)
participating in extension and adaptive research subprojects with an average of 25% of co-financing

4. Project Components
The total APL Phase I1Project cost is expected to be US$43.11 million (Bank share: US$25.0 million;
GOP share: US$6.06 million; other stakeholders: US$l2.05 million) and be of four years' duration,
beginningJuly 1, 2005. The Project would finance the following four components, the details of which
are given in Annex 4.
Component 1. Strengthening the Marketfor Innovation Services
Expected cost: US$20.94 million (Bank share: US$14.49 million; GOP: US$0.72 million; others:
US$5.72 million).
The aim of Component 1 i s to advance the development of a decentralized market for professional
services for agricultural innovation. It will strengthen producer organizations as beneficiaries of quality
services, and improve entrepreneurial capacity of private service providers. The result will be a more
efficient market for services. Special emphasis will be given to the development of instruments that
would ensure equitable participation opportunities to vulnerable groups such as organizations of
indigenous and women's groups. To realize this objective the component will: (a) finance a Competitive
Fund that will co-finance subprojects in adaptive research and extension based on business plans that
reflect producer demands, including vulnerable groups such as indigenous populations and women's
organizations; and (b) co-finance local participant forums, internships of pre-professionals, and training
workshops, to improve the administrative capacity of organizations and the agribusiness of producers, and
to strengthen the capacity of the suppliers with a managerial orientation to provision of services.
Implementation of the competitive fund will be decentralized at the macro-region level. The participation
of technical representatives of Regional Governments of the macro-regions would be ensured in the
evaluation, priority setting, and negotiation of subproject proposals for the competitive fund. Local
capacity to formulate and implement innovative subprojects that contribute to regional agricultural
competitiveness will be strengthened.
Component 2. Strengthening the National Agricultural Research and Development System
Expected cost: US$14.00 million (Bank share: US$7.27 million; GOP: US$0.40 million; others: US$6.33
million).
The aim of Component 2 i s to strengthen agricultural research and technological development for
innovation in strategic areas of national importance, and to strengthen institutional and professional
competence.     The component would establish support mechanisms for "centers of excellence" in
emerging areas of science and technology, and for regional training programs in high-skill innovation
services that are critical to increasing the competitiveness of the agricultural sector. The component will
manage the Strategic Services Development Fund (FDSE). The FDSE would: (a) invest in the strategic
national and international alliances to co-finance research activities and capacity building, initially in
three high-priority areas-genetic     resources conservation and use, biotechnology, and integrated plant
and animal management-and           which would be gradually expanded to include natural resource



                                                       8

management (NRM);soils and land management, irrigation and water management, ago-forestry, post-
harvest handling and processing, and sustainable agriculture; (b) development of the capacity of public
research institutes to co-finance strategic research activities targeting public funds for the smallholder
agnculture; (c) finance the strengthening of regional training and information programs for extension
service providers and to co-finance cumculums with a focus on capacity building and institutional
development; and (d) co-finance training programs for researchers in specialties includedin high-priority
areas of strategic researchthat require a certain critical mass of professional talent.
This Fundwould also partially finance the development of the public sector capacity to generatescientific
and technological information, to develop new products and processes as a result of research and
development (R&D) in a program of strategic research for smallholder agriculture in Peru, in a manner
where a public research institute concentrate its efforts, technical capacity and financial resources in the
implementation of a number of priority and strategic subprojects of value and delivery of public goods.
Given the importance of smallholder agriculture and its inherent problems, the Project has a special
interest in financing a strategic programto address such problems and for which INIEA i s well positioned
to implement given its presence in various eco-regions of the country. This would require certain
conditions for the reform of INIEA as outlined in the Policy Letter, which accompanies this operation,
and ifmet, this strategic programcould be financed inINIEA.
Component 3. Monitoring and Evaluation (M&E), Information Sharing, and Policy for Innovation
Expected cost: US$5.53 million (Bank share: US$2.25million; GOP: US$3.28 million).
The aim of Component 3 i s to develop the institutional capacity of the public sector to formulate and
implement agricultural innovation policy in conjunction with the private sector, and regulate the quality
of the NAIS. The outcome would be the establishing of a Technological Innovation Policy Focal Point in
a modemized M O A that i s responsible for defining policies, and which would coordinate investments in
this sector. The outputs would be: (a) institutionalization of processes, priorities, and policies to promote
and protect innovations with consensus within the system and policy definition in critical areas such as
promotion of public investments in science and agricultural technology, intellectual property rights, and
public-private partnerships; (b) validation of an M&E system to generate performance measurement
standards to measure the results of modernization of the NAIS services; and (c) a modernized and
systematized information system composed of virtual networks that will increase the sector's capacity to
generate and use systematized and updated scientific and technological information. The activities to be
financed would be: (1) studies and consultations on prospective technologies and technological
forecasting, elaboration of a concerted agenda of the innovation system, identification of the constraints
on the development, adoption, and dissemination of innovations, and defining priorities and formulating
policies in potential strategic areas of innovations; (2) a M&E system for subprojects using measuring
instruments that permit comparison of designs, identify best practices of quality, submit information to
public scrutiny, reward the best subprojects, and ensure the achievement of maximum results and desired
impacts; and (3) an information system that i s in the public domain and enriched with data generated by
subprojects, operational plans, and programs in place, available to all stakeholders participating in
agriculturalinnovations.
Component 4. Project Implementation
Expected cost: US$2.51million (Bank share: US$0.86 million; GOP: US$1.65 million).
The aim of Component 4 is to finance the present INCAGRO-Project Management Unit (PMU), which
would continue to be the implementingagency for Phase 11. The main responsibilities of the PMUwould
be to: (a) implement and monitor the Project at the national level; (b) be the principal contact with the
World Bank in Project supervision and financial management of the Bank's and public investment,
including efficient administration of the organizational structure of the Project; (c) conduct annual
program audits; (d) ensure quality assessments of Project implementation in terms of social impacts and

5. LessonsLearnedand ReflectedinProject Design
Lessonsfrom PreviousBank-Assisted Projects
The Bank has a long history of supporting rural-development-related projects in Peru, including the
Programa Nacional para el Manejo de las Cuencas Hidrolo'gicas y Conservacidn de Suelos
(PRONAMACHCS), rural roads, irrigation, and community development (see Annex 2 for a list of Bank-
funded projects in the rural sector). The experience of these Bank lending programs has provided
valuable pointers to guide future investments inthe rural agricultural sector. The Project would also build
on the Bank's experience in agricultural extension and research projects in other countries in LAC and
other regions.
In addressing issues such as sustainability, commitment by the national government, and poor
implementation raised by the Operations Evaluation Department (OED) in connection with overall Bank
assistance to Peru (2002)5, this Project is ensuring sustainability by leveraging resources from co-
financers, mainstreaming a competitive grant scheme as part of the GOP strategy for financing of
agricultural researchand extension, increasing the role of producer organizations in the identification and
implementation of subprojects, and increasing institutional plurality on the supply side. Moreover, a
private business group has recognized the successful implementation of Phase Iof the Project by a special
award for creativity in 2003. The Phase IProject, through its two competitive funds (107 subprojects),
has leveraged US$1.04 from both public and private partners for every dollar invested by the Project.


  OED Peru CountryAssistance Evaluation2002.


                                                     10

Sixty-seven private groups or institutions are participating in this Project as demanders or suppliers of
services. A special funding window, Huchuy Ayni (Mutual Help), has been established to facilitate the
participation of indigenous peoples.
The lessons learned from Phase Iof the APL Project described below on research, extension, and
institutional framework i s invaluable in the design and implementation of the Phase I1Project. Phase 1,
which initially focused on three regions of the country, and later expanded to five, has shown that a
demand-driven, decentralized, and competitive system for the allocation of resources to research and
extension can be successfully implemented in Peru. This corroborates evidence from similar agricultural
technology and extension projects financed by the Bank in other L A C countries (Colombia, Ecuador,
Mexico, and Venezuela) and in the rest of the world (Cameroon, Georgia, India, and Kenya).

On research: Attention to excellence in research quality needs to be enhanced through: (a)
competitively awarding research grants based on explicit economic, eco-regional, social, and technical
criteria; (b) external review of research programs and subprojects at least once during the project life; (c)
active involvement of strong research institutions and universities; (d) effective linkages with
international, regional, and national research centers of excellence; and (e) needs-based training of
participants at all levels of the production chain. A bottom-up approach to determine the needs of the
beneficiaries (clientes) requires involvement of farmers/producers in setting the research agendas,
including special consideration of the needs of disadvantaged groups (that is, gender issues, indigenous
peoples issues, and vulnerable groups). An innovation systems concept that i s fully inclusive of all
players in the demand-supply chain is required to promote synergies among technology generation
(scientists and educators), other market stakeholders (industry, agnbusinesses, and so forth), and
extension service providers and producers. Regional and national level research priority setting and
planning i s required to address the differences in innovation system supply and demand, and to ensure
local ownership by all players, which in turn would lead to a more sustainable system. Rationalizing
existing resources and limiting factors for expansion of facilities are important to developing financially
sustainable research systems.       Policy and regulatory regimes should be favorable to private sector
research and technology spill-ins.
On extension: Extension systems must be adaptable to respond to rapidly changing economic, trade,
and sectoral conditions. Investment in countrywide extension systems may be inappropriate when
there i s a lack of resources or technology to support such systems. Both public and private sector
services should be considered in extension system design, and both traditional mass media and new web-
basedcommunication technologies are appropriate tools for extension use, that is, web-based networking.
Needs-based extension staff training should focus on the training of extension workers to encourage
producer organization participation in extension, in addition to providing technical information and skills
development.
On institutional framework including the role of the Government: A strong Government commitment
i s essential for a sustainable and effective research and extension system.         Developing in-house
socioeconomic capacity and setting up of effective monitoring and evaluation (M&E) systems are
essential to the success of the Project. An M&E system should assess the short- and medium-term
impacts of the Project that result from the development and adoption of new technological interventions.
Both public and private sector services must be involved in strategic plans for implementing rural
agricultural development activities.
Partner Activities in Relationto Bank Assistance
A number of other partners, such as the Inter-American Development Bank (IADB), the International
Fundfor Agricultural Development (FAD), and the UnitedStates Agency for International Development
(USAID), are active inPeru's development of human resources, infrastructure, and services inrural areas.
While these donor agencies are active in the rural areas, accordingto a country client survey conducted in




                                                     11

2002; the Bank i s recognized for bringing important expertise and intemational experience to Peru and
demonstrating strong leadership in the design and preparation of new and relevant projects.
The lessonsof other donor-funded projects in the sector (see Annex 2) also influenced the Project design,
particularly the need for (a) synergetic input on the role of the private sector in innovation by input from
medium-to-larger-sized agricultural entrepreneurs and multinationals; (b) greater focus on end users,
particularly the disadvantaged groups, by location, ethnicity, gender, age, or education; (c) encouraging
the participationof universities and other institutions of higher education as partners of the NAIS; and (d)
empowerment of regional bodies as knowledge-transfer channels, and integration with the broader
agricultural sector inclusive of the export and tourism sectors.

6. Alternatives Considered andReasonsfor Rejection
Inthedesign of the program, consideration was given to supporting acentralizedpublicsystem, as inthe
past, or providing all support through the private sector. In line with stakeholder views in Peru, and
consistent with evolving international experiences, it was decided to support the development of a
decentralized, demand-driven system that would be co-financed by users and private partners. Phase Iof
the Project, which was concentrated in three regions, and expanded to five, has shown that development
of an innovation system with these characteristics i s feasible in Peru, and that there is sufficient supply
and demand for the types of services beingfinanced by the Project. The proposed Phase I1Project would
continue to pursue the objective of the program, expanding its scope to all regions in Peru, and giving
greater attention to targeting disadvantaged groups, including indigenous people and women. This
expansion is particularly timely within the context of the recent decentralization process established in
Peru, and the implementationof the competitive funds for extension, and adaptive and strategic research,
Another tactical alternative considered and rejected was relying exclusively on the importation of
innovations into the sector. This was based on Phase IAPL experience and that of similar projects in
Colombia, Ecuador and Venezuela, where it has been clearly shown that developing local capacity i s
crucial to facilitate the importation and fitting of appropriate technologies to local conditions. In this
context, a strategy of developing local and decentralized institutional capacity and stronger and more
qualified human resources, and producing adapted and available technologies to adequately address
specific and localizedproblems i s considered to be a more adequate strategy.
The exclusive support for a single researchand extension institution (that is, INIEA) in Peru was rejected,
because one national institute cannot adequately meet the country's agricultural research needs. This i s
particularly important since a competitive sector demands that a range of options needs to be delivered to
the suppliers and demanders of services. A multi-partner NAIS that includes a strong and active private
sector also improves the sustainability of such a system that adapts to changing needs, andkeeps up with
the rapid advances in science.
The establishment of a GOP-implemented, public-sector-financed extension service would also have
similar disadvantages, in addition to being costly to the economy, whereas private sector participation in
agricultural investments i s seen as improving ownership and efficiency of resource use. The producer-co-
financed strategy was therefore judged the most appropriate, because it pennits the producers to express
their needs while sharing costs, thereby enhancing the financial sustainability of the services.

3. Monitoringand Evaluation(M&E) of Outcomes/Results
The M&E framework that was refined based on the Phase Iexperience and improved during Project
preparation will be fully operational by the Project effectiveness date. This framework takes into account
that a well-designed system is critical to ensure timely and successful implementation of the Project, and
to enhance its impact for the beneficiaries by the systematic and periodic analysis of lessons learned, and
their effective dissemination. Project monitoringand evaluation would be the responsibility of the Project
Management Unit (PMU). Performance indicators have been established for the Project and its four
components, and are presented in the Results Framework in Annex 3. The M&E system i s based on a
cascade of goals, purposes, results, and activities where higher-order activity, that is, components,
become the purpose of the lower order, that is, the subcomponents. This approach will ensure the
tracking of all activities to the developmental objective of the Project. A participatory approach with end
users i s included in the selection of priorities at the regional level, in the selection of subprojects to be co-
financed, the participation in capacity-buildingevents, and becoming an integral part of the Project as co-
financers would ensure broad participation of the communities and the nation as a whole in Project
outcomes. Participatory monitoring tools would be used as needed to evaluate the participatory indexes
in Project and subproject implementation. The oversight committee will assist in keeping the national
interests within the framework of implementation progress.              The Project design includes baseline
determinations and performance (milestones) indicators to monitor the implementation of the plan. Such
monitoring will consist of an intemal evaluation at the component level, and one at the Project level. The
monitoring will be based on periodic reports.
By using a management information system (MIS) and the financial management system, Project
activities will be kept on track and potential problems identified and appropriately addressed. The PMU
will design the MIS for M&E, reporting formats for each component and decentralized office according
to the target annual performance objectives and monitoring indicators shown in Annex 3. The quarterly
reports will cover the progress in implementation, and milestones in the use of Project funds and impact.
Quarterly technical and financial reports will be consolidated by the PMUinto bi-annual progress reports
to be submitted to the Bank within two months of the end of each six-month reporting period. These bi-
annual reports will also include an implementation plan and activity program for the subsequent six
months of the reporting period. Additionally, independent outsourced external reviews and impact
assessments will be done. A midtermreview will assess the overall progress of the Project. The lessons
learned and recommendations for any needed improvements would be used in restructuringor realigning
the Project plans. Post-project impact assessments will be conducted to improve quantitative assessment
of the Project outcome on the final phase of the APL Project planning.




                                                         13

 4.   Sustainability
 In the last three years, as a result of the interventions of Phase Iof the Project, important lessons of
 institutional changes have been generated in the Peruvian NAIS. These institutional innovations would
 be built into Phase I1of the Project to assure the sustainability of the achieved results.
 Leading role within the MOA and GOP support. In this context, the GOP continued to be fully
 committed to the implementation plans of this Project. The establishment of a policy focal point
 (Technological Innovation Policy Focal Point) within the M O A would ensure GOP long-term
 sustainability and support.

.Mainstreaming the competitive grant scheme. The Project would ensure that the competitive grant
 scheme would be an important component of public resourcesfor innovation, and would help to increase
 GOPNOA funds once the Bank-fundedprogram is completed.
 Institutional analysis of the agricultural innovation system done by this Project has documented a large
 number of institutions active in the sector, its problems, and its capacity to participate in the Project,
 which makes it possible to identify and implement remedies and facilitate the long-term viability of
 innovation processes.
 Role of the producer organizations. The producer organizations assisted and strengthened by the Project
 would continue to operate with increased self-sufficiency in terms of funds generated through
 technological interventions that would help sustain them in the longer term.
 Znstitutional plurality in the NAZS. The diversity and the great number of organizations of existent
 producers would allow the Project to be selective and provide direct support to the most capable
 organizations, while investingintraining and promotional activities to strengthen the capacity of others.
 5. Critical Risks andPossible ControversialAspects
 Main Risks
 Given the broad political and institutional commitment to the APL Program and successful
 implementation of Phase I,it is unlikely that major risks would occur to the design of this Project. A
 strengths, weaknesses, opportunities, threats (SWOT) analysis indicatedthat potential risks involved with
 this Project are: (a) the development of a parallel systemof researchand innovation if it is not possible to
 develop and implement a coherent national policy that integrates INIEA, the major public research
 organization, into an increasingly pluralistic innovation system; (b) the weak capacity of newly formed
 regional governments to participate effectively in the decentralized implementation of the Project; and (c)
 a weak procurement system in the project unit. To address these risks the following steps will be taken:
 (i)redefinition of INIEA's strategy to address its role in a pluralistic system; (ii)strengthening the
 capacity of decentralized offices as well as training for local entities; and (iii) development of a plan for
 strengthening procurement processes.
 The Government has committed in the Policy Letter to support an INIEA that i s specialized in the
 execution of research and technology transfer, and which would concentrate its resources in a number of
 strategic programs that are of a strong public good nature. This would also require that the Ministry of
 Agriculture would support these strategic programs. If these conditions are met the Project through its
 Strategic Services Development Fund (FDSE) could co-finance subprojects within these strategic
 programs. Given the importance of smallholder agriculture and its inherent problems, the Project has a
 special interest in financing such a strategic program to address these problems and for which INIEA is
 well positioned to implement, given its presence in various eco-regions of the country.

 Despite the concerns stated above and risk mitigation measures taken (Table 2), it i s evident that the
 institutional capacity of the borrower to handle preparation and implementation of the proposed Phase I1
 Project of the APL program i s very high. The Project Management Unit has three years of experience in



                                                      14

effectively implementing the Phase IProject, and has hireda capable cadre of professionals with excellent
knowledge of the innovation systemand Bank procedures.

No specific risks have been identified through predecessor operations or from poverty, social, and
financial management. A social assessment was carried out during preparation. Moreover, financial
management and procurement specialists were part of the preparation team. A procurement assessment
rated the risk as high and a plan has been agreed upon to remedy the deficiencies that were identified
duringpre-appraisal.
