Possible controversial aspects: Phase I1i s not expected to trigger a threat to stakeholders who might feel
that their interests are threatened by the Project. Phase Ihas supported the development of qualified
private sector providers and, in general, the Project enjoys wide support inthe agricultural communities in
which it works. However, success of such private-led initiatives threatens traditional stakeholders in the
public sector, and this requires continued dialogue to ensure a broad understanding of Project principles.
In addition, the strong support the Project enjoys from other stakeholders should enable it to move
forward.     Phase I1 would also expand activities targeted toward indigenous people, women, and
vulnerable groups.
6. Loadcredit Conditionsand Covenants

As acondition of effectiveness, the Borrower through INCAGRO-PMU,has adopted the Subproject
Operational Manual, in form and substance satisfactory to the Bank.




                                                      15

D. APPRAISAL SUMMARY
1. Economic andFinancialAnalyses
The economic and financial analysis of Phase I1of the APL follows good practices inthe World Bank and
i s based on experiences with similar projects in Latin America and other regions. The analysis i s
structured as follows: (a) a brief summary of general issues for economic analysis of research and
extension projects; (b) a brief review of returns to past investments in agricultural research and extension
projects internationally and in Peru; (c) an estimation of potential internal rate of return (IRR) for the
proposed investment based on experiences with the first project phase; and (d) a fiscal analysis focusing
on the potential consequencesof the proposed investment for the government budget.
The evaluation of subprojects of the Agricultural Technology Fund (ATE) of Phase Iindicated potential
economic benefits of investments inthe nationalresearchand extension system to be financed inPhase 11.
The economic and financial analysis was based on data from the M&E database, subproject progress
reports, and assumptions based on the technical expertise of Project Implementation Unit (PMUin Phase
11) staff. Out of the total of 63 adaptive research and extension projects (excluding Huchuy Ayni
subprojects) of the ATF, 29 subprojects have been selected for the evaluation. The selection was mainly
based on data availability and is therefore not a formally randomized selection of all projects. However,
the sample size can be considered as sufficient, represents the different regions and project types, and is
not biased towards successful projects. Since many subprojects are not completed yet, and the M&Eunit
of the PMU i s still working on data collection and analysis the results have to be considered as
preliminary. The economic analysis i s canied out for a 15-year period. The technology adoption and
benefit flow pattern vary depending on the subproject type and the technology type under consideration.
Adoption data are available for the first years after subproject initiation; adoption pattern beyond this time
period is based on assumptions made by the subproject directors.             The "without project" scenario
accounts for average income increases over time, but does not account for adoption of the technology
generatedand/or promotedthrough project activities.

Financial Analysis:
A financial analysis for adaptive research and extension projects was conducted to demonstrate the
profitability of a project from the direct participants' perspective. Since cost recovery mechanisms would
be implemented under the project in the form of beneficiary contributions, this analysis provides valuable
information on the potential willingness of farmers to pay for adaptive research and advisory services in
the future. The financial analysis of an individual extension project, which focuses on organic sugarcane
production, yields a NPV of US$ 850,000 (at a 12 percent discount rate) and an IRR of 48 percent. The
NPV of the aggregatedprojects amounted to US$6.96 million with a financial IRR of 36.5 percent.

Economic Analysis:
Since price distortions in Peru are limited and affect only a few products (mainly sugar, cotton, corn,
milk, and rice) the differences between the results of the economic and financial analysis are small.
However, in cases of price distortions (inparticular import tariffs) adjustments were made to calculate the
economic values. The aggregated economic analysis for the 29 selected subprojects shows that the
economic IRR of the aggregated projects amounts to 34.1 percent. The high IRR i s not surprising
considering the low levels of technology and productivity of Peruvian farmers. These values are
comparable with research and extension projects in other countries. Sensitivity analysis indicates that a 20
percent reduction inproject benefits would lead to an IRR of 14percent.
The calculation of the overall productivity gain required in the agricultural sector to pay the costs of the
Phase I1Project i s another useful indicator for the economic justification of the investment. The analysis
indicates that a productivity gain of only 0.55 percent of the agricultural gross domestic product (AgGDP)
would lead to an IRR of 12percent. Compared internationally, the requiredlevel i s very low.




                                                      16

However, due to increased emphasis on fiscal discipline in Peru, it is crucial to estimate the fiscal impact
of the proposed investment. If it were not accounted for the planned co-financing and cost-recovery
mechanisms through service providers and clients, public expenditures would have to increase in real
terms by 37 percent annually after the end of the project. This increase would correspond to an increased
expenditure of US$8 million per year. The incremental public expenditures as a percentage of the total
government budget for the agricultural sector would decrease from 3.5 percent to 3.0 percent. However,
in Phase 11, about 20 percent of these costs have to be co-financed by the producers and executing
agencies. Assuming that about the same amount would be provided by these agencies after project
completion, public expenditures for research and extension have to increase by only about US$12 million
annually, or 55 percent. Although these projections mean a significant increase in financial commitment
of the GOP to agricultural research and extension, the public expenditures would still be low compared to
international levels.
Tax revenue can be an important means of recovering public expenditures. An analysis was conducted
which indicates the potential impact of projected AgGDP growth on the recovery of fiscal outlays through
increased tax revenues. The analysis assumes that 50 to 60 percent of the incremental income of
agncultural producers is spent on goods on which the value-added tax (VAT) (18 percent) i s imposed
(mainly non-food items). Assuming that 60 percent of the incremental income i s spent on taxed items,
and that the co-financing mechanisms would be sustained beyond the end of the project, an AgGDP
growth rate of 2 percent would nearly offset government expenditures.            The analysis reveals that
additional tax revenues due to increased economic activity would substantially contribute to the recovery
of fiscal outlays.
2. Technical
All the proposed activities under the Phase I1Project of an APL on strengthening of the market of
services for innovation, strengthening of strategic competence for research and development,
strengthening policies, information exchange, and quality of services of innovation components have been
demonstrated to be technically feasible by the Phase IProject, and by other Bank and various donor-
funded initiatives and operations inPeru. The rationale, approach, and detailed design of each component
can be found inAnnex 4. The first and secondcomponents are building on Project's first phase leadingto
geographical expansion of the program and a scaling up of the already tested design to extension
approach while deepening the adaptive and strategic research component and shifting the focus from
thematic areas based approach to production chain-oriented approach. The monitoring and evaluation
that was embedded in above two components of Phase Iproject is now placed in the third technical
component in Phase 11. The third component on policy, information, and quality that was greatly
modified during preparation of the Phase I1Project requires intense monitoring to ensure that the set
targets are reached as planned, and the anticipated incremental benefits are realized.
The main instrument of investment i s the use of competitive funds with slight variations to address the
different components, target groups, types of results, and time frame that i s fully tested on various
agricultural technology and extension projects, and is proven to be successful in similar projects in Peru
(the Phase IAPL), other Latin American countries, and elsewhere. A long-term strategic vision in
supporting thematic areas will be fulfilled using the same funding window that i s noncompetitive and
targeted, that is, an FDSE instrument. Other design characteristics (a vertical approach using pilot
studies) in the short term, by using competitive funds with support to strengthen the capacity to produce
quality project proposals by indigenous populations and women's organizations and inclusion of other
disadvantaged groups such as indigenous young people in developing innovative market of services, are
expected in the long run to assist in mainstreaming the participation of all population categories of the
Project by the beginning of Phase 111. Knowledge rescue of indigenous knowledge of plants, crop
species, indigenous agricultural systems, and oral traditions through affirmative selection of extension
subproject proposals are a mainstay of this Phase I1Project.




                                                     17

In relation to the process of expansion of the scope of technological interventions and geographic
expansion of the Project during its Phase 11, a set of criteria were considered in order to select the
decentralized macroregions that will be establishedon an annual basis. These criteria include: (a) the key
variables to define the target population of the Project; as relates to the productive efficiency of the
producers and the degree of market articulation including the presence of agro-industrial chains and
demand for products, public service operators with greater capacity of technological innovation, and
better economic context and infrastructure; (b) the potential of farming and forest resources to respond to
the globalization of the market (potential of impact of the services for innovation); and (c) aspects that
harness the success of the intervention strategy of the Project (greater institutional development, leaders
of produce organizations, greater capacity and disposition to payment for services, and greater relative
development of the market of services for the innovation). The expansion processi s detailed inAnnex 6.
The innovative directions of Phase I1Project design that can be emphasized are: market service driven
approach taken in Component 1; applied research framework leading the Component 2 activities rather
than basic research; and modernized forms of knowledge sharing, e.g., web based extension and
establishing a proactive monitoring and evaluation system in Component 3. Marketability and income
generation potential from the intellectual property and development of patentable knowledge is high in a
research and innovation project of this nature; therefore a study will look at the potential use of IPR and
how to strengthen the national and institutional framework for intellectual property, followed by a pilot
study in APL phase 111. Another innovative instrument of Phase I1 designed to recognize technical
quality and best practices of subprojects fundedby the various competitive funds is "Merit Awards".
A central element in the formulation of the technical capacity strengthening of this Project was
constructed inconsultation with and participationof relevant stakeholders, takinginto account the broader
mandate of Phase I1to the whole country, while addressing population cohorts that are disadvantaged.
The inclusion of an oversight ExecutiveCouncil (ExC) provides the additional level of scrutiny to Project
administration, and provides avenues for including good technical practices in the Project while it i s
implemented, and flexibility in the activity plan to address emerging themes. The Project, within its
institutional strengthening mandated through strategic program funding (FDSE), will mobilize the
participation of national public, private, and regional universities and other private entities involved in
agricultural sciences. The Project also provides the PMUthe opportunity to tap quality technical capacity
as consultants (local, regional, and international) on rapidly advancing fields of sciences such as
biotechnology and molecular biology, information technology, and training (virtual learning, distant
learning, and adult education). The Project would mobilize different types of organized community level
organizations using the well-tested models in other Latin American countries where agricultural
technology and extension subprojects have been successful. The social inclusion in the regional context,
though requiring more intense efforts and also having high risks on delivery, would receive particular
attention during Phase 11. In addition, the Project would attempt to maximize opportunities of
collaborations with various donors of NAIS who are service suppliers and are creating additional
demands as identified inAnnex 3 and their respective implementing agencies.
3. Fiduciary
Financial Plan. The estimated total amount of Project Phase I1(Annex 5) is US$43.11 million for a four-
year period, of which US$25.0 million corresponds to the financing provided by the World Bank,
US$12.05 million corresponds to the co-financing of the implementing agencies (suppliers and
demanders), and US$6.06 million corresponds to the contributions of the GOP. The annual average
expenses would be about US$10 million. At the component level, Component 1is estimated to use 48.6
percent on strengthening the Market for Innovation, 32.5 percent on Strengthening of Strategic Research
and Development, and 12.8 percent on Policy Formulation for Innovation. Administration of the Project
cost would be 5.8 percent. Of the budget total, 28 percent will be leveraged directly by the strategic
alliances, which would support the Project through the investment funds and the windows of direct
attention (suppliers and demanders of innovation services). The above-proposed financial plan takes into


                                                    18

account the wide coverage of the Project in terms of the targeted population, and better diffusion of
interventions of the Government to promote technological innovations in agriculture and to improve the
efficiency of resource allocation to the institutionrepresenting this system.
This financial plan, given in US dollars, estimates an international inflationary factor of 1.9 percent per
year, at an exchange rate of 3.5 soles per US dollar. The physical contingency for civil works was
assumed to be at 10 percent and 5 percent for consulting services, 5 percent for consultancies, and 5
percent for capacity building. For the competitive funds, which depend on demand and local operating
expenses, no allowance was made for fiscal contingency.              All estimates were made taking the
implementation schedule into account. This budget includes a few additional expenses related to the
estimated cost of the pre-feasibility study that will help expand Project coverage in terms of target
population, improve the scope of governmental interventions to promote agricultural innovation, and
achieve better resource allocation among institutions within the system. The audit plan will follow
standardBank practices.
The proposed budget is based on a cost structure that is in agreement with the Project design of four
components, while taking into account that the final budget will be subject to the country's borrowing
capacity.

Procurement.      This evaluation concluded that this Project, to be implemented nationwide in a
decentralized mode of operation, has high risks. To ensure that the unit structure i s strengthened and
adequate trained personnel are involved, the following actions are requiredfor the specific objective of
improving its capacity: (a) preparation of a procurement plan, with a complete systematized information
base for follow-up action; (b) a system of filing and archiving at headquarters and at each decentralized
unit; (c) capacity buildingfor members of the ad hoc Evaluation Committee andthe Specialized Standing
Procurement Committee; and (d) implementation of an integrated management information system. The
details of this procurement action plan are given inAnnex 4.
Financial Management. Overall, during Phase Iof the APL, the Project Management Unit (PMU) has
maintained financial management arrangements, systems, and practices that are satisfactory to the Bank,
and are adequate to provide the Bank with accurate and timely information regarding project resources,
expenditures, and activities.
Entity specific and control risks have been assessed as moderate. During appraisal, the major capacity
constraints related to: a) the inability of the current information system to produce Financial Monitoring
Reports (FMRs) to support project management; b) inadequate monitoring mechanism for overseeing the
financial operations of the decentralized units; c) record keeping and filing which fragments the audit trail
of transactions; d) the lack of synchronization between project planning, execution of payments and
authorization (within the Ministry of Finance (MEF) of withdrawal application requests to the Bank, and
(e) capacity to transition into Report-based disbursements mechanism.
Whereas in the Phase I,the impact of these constraints was not material, the Phase I1required a quantum
leap, in order to mitigate the risks posed by the expansion in project complexity and scope. As reflected
in the updated Action Plan, the PMU has (1) upgraded the information systems and produced a
comprehensive User Manual which reflects the internal control processes and procedures; (2) the system
has the capacity to produce Financial Monitoring Reports, which can be used as the basis for
disbursement; (3) a Financial Analyst has been hired and trained and monitoring mechanism have been
instituted to oversee the financial operations of decentralized units; (4) record keeping and filing systems
and procedures have been prepared and implemented in order to ensure an appropriate audit trail; (5) the
Draft Operations Manual has been submitted to the Bank for review and comment; and (6) extensive
training sessions to improve Financial, Disbursement and Procurement capacity have been conducted in
two decentralized regions. Once the scaling up of the integrated financial management system i s
satisfactorily implemented, the residual inherent and control risks will be mitigated and the Bank will
have no objection to approvingthe implementation of the new disbursement mechanism.



                                                      19

4. Social
The preparation of the Project has taken into account the key social development goals on increased rural
employment and decreased rural poverty, and a social assessment was done for the primary purpose of
preparing action plans to mitigate social risks and inequities. The social framework of the Project takes
into account inits main instrument of funding, the Competitive funds, the social impact of the Project as a
whole and at the subproject level, by targeting the end beneficiaries as co-participants in and co-financers
of the Project. A central theme and a decentralized mode of operation of the Project requires community
level participation (that is, in regional priority setting for competitive funds and selection of subprojects
for co-financing), and the active participation of other NGOs is designed to ensure that rural poverty
alleviation i s better addressed as a result of the Project outcomes of individual components. Annex 8
discusses the sociocultural and political impacts of interventions on the market of services and on the
innovation system, and the impact on disadvantaged and excluded groups, such as the indigenous
populations and women.
The Project design treats the gender issue in two ways. First, in the selection of subprojects for co-
financing (in terms of extension, and adaptive and strategic research themes), gender balance in
participationi s inherent, while second, it recognizes the imbalance ineffective participation due to gender
inequities that affect women's organizations, and attempts to overcome this by a special fund. The
proposed plans of action for strengthening indigenous populations and rural women are mentioned in the
safeguard section below, and are also described inAnnex 8.
5. Environment
In accordance with Bank policy, an environmental assessment of the impact of the Project was completed.
The Project was rated at the environmental category B, since the Project components were found to have
relatively limited environmental impact potential. According to the analysis, the Project would integrate
good practices on natural resource management (NRM) and environmental indicators accounting for
environmental issues, and the monitoring and evaluation process will include environmental assessments
to mitigate any potential negative impact, and is thereby expected to have an overall positive impact on
natural resources and the environment. Based on the commissioned study entitled, "The Environmental
Hazards of TechnologicalInnovation," an environmentalmanagement plan (EMP) that takes into account
good practices of ESRP i s attached in Annex 10.2 to streamline the strategy and practices that would
eliminate any adverse environmental and social and human health impacts, or offset them or reduce them
to acceptable levels. All the proposals for subprojects of competitive funds, in addition, would include an
evaluation of the environmental impact, including a list of negative impact verification for the eligibility
of proposals of subprojects for funding. According to the study on potential environmental risks of
technological innovations, the following outcomes would be realized: (a) generation of capacity to
achieve a positive environmental impact by using best practices in the technology generation process and
its final product; (b) establishment of critical steps taking into account both proposed technologies and
their immediate effects and medium- and long-term environmental impacts; and (c) introducing
methodshndicators for environmental economic valuation of the proposed technologies and the
beneficiaries.
6. SafeguardPolicies
The safeguard-screening category of the Project is S2.           Most safeguard policies are not triggered
negatively by the Project, and the safeguard policies that might be of concern are given adequate
attention, as described below. Environmental and pest management might be triggered due to the
introduction of new agricultural innovations.          Following the experience of the Phase 1 Project,
environmental monitoring criteria would continue to be included in the subproject evaluation and
selection and as described in the environmentalplan. Pest management safeguards would be activated in
the Project and its subprojects by: (a) advocating integrated pest management (IPM) practices; (b) training
activities related to promoting biological and environmental control methods over chemical pesticides


                                                        20

where possible; and (c) following the World Health OrganizatiodFood and Agriculture Organization
(WHOFAO) guidelines on the safe use of pesticides which are effective only against the target species,
have minimal effects on non-target species and the environment, and have negligible human health
effects.   A pest management action plan developed for the first time for this type of agricultural
technology and extension projects in the LAC region i s given in Annex 10.3. An action plan for the
dissemination of all the Safeguards Plans of Actions was developed during appraisal and will be
implemented. During Project implementation, appropriate IPM and other technical experts, as needed,
would be included in the supervision missions.
The Indigenous People's Development Plan developed during the Phase 1 Project was revised and
updated, taking into account lessons of the pilot studies. Inthe last decade, several social changes have
taken place among the indigenous peoples. Localcommunities have been strengthened, giving rise to the
emergence of territorial and ethnic organizations and national indigenous fronts, which are the focal point
in the proposed interventions to enhance social inclusion and improve overall productivity to alleviate
pervasive poverty. The Project would also strive to ensure that indigenous people would benefit directly
from the Project through a special fundthat will specifically cater to the needs of disadvantaged groups.
The Project has mainstreamed development assistance activities targeted rural women while addressing
the specific needs of this disadvantaged group through a competitive grant scheme for rural women
organizations.

                       Annex 1: Country and Sector or Program Background
                   PERU: Agricultural Research and ExtensionAPL PhaseI1
Sector Issues
This Annex summarizes the principal characteristics of the Peruvian economy and the evolution of its
agricultural sector since the political crisis of 2000, as background to Project Phase I1plans. In the last
decade, limited changes occurred in the agricultural sector and free market operations, with positive
outcomes, such as a positive economic growth and an expanded internal demand for services. The
agricultural sector increased significantly, from 7.3 percent to 9 percent from 1991 to 2002. Note that
during the same period fertilizer supply increasedby 330 percent, while global market fertilizer imports
increasedfrom 43 to 98 percent (Escobal and Valdivia 2004). Duringthe same period, export volume of
agricultural products tripled, reaching US$934 million in 2002, while importation of agricultural products
increased since 1998. The leading agricultural product was coffee, contributing to 31 percent of total
exports (2000-02). Other agricultural products of note were processed or fresh asparagus (13 percent and
10 percent, respectively), wood (9 percent), and mango (4 percent) (MINAG 2003). The main export
destinations were the UnitedStates (31 percent), Spain (11percent), Germany (11percent), and Mexico
(5 percent).
Improvements in the sector occurred in the following areas: (a) an increase in export crops such as fruits
(mango, for example) and horticultural crops (asparagus, for example) cultivated by medium-to-large-
scale producers in the coastal zone, and with better infrastructural connectivity with the international
market; and (b) an increase in artesian products of an agro-tourist nature for the export market. The food
production index of Peru, at 173, (using a base of 100 in 1989-91, World Bank 2004b), i s higher than the
Latin America and Caribbean (LAC) average of 142, and of the lower-middle-income country average of
158. Fertilizer consumption (100 grams per hectare of arable land), however, remains lower at 813 than
the LAC average of 846, and of the lower-middle-income country average of 1,060. The production
increase is mostly due to increased area of production (that is, for rice, maize, and potato) and to new
crop-basedproducts (asparagus and grapevine). The productivity changes for cereal (for example, yellow
maize), coffee, and potato crops, though positive, were minimal. A large disparity in agricultural
productivity continued between the coastal and sierra regions. The need to catalyze rural growth by
improving access to markets, and by increasing the dynamism and competitiveness of all actors (buyers,
sellers, and other service providers), with sufficient attention given to disadvantaged groups (women and
other minorities), and to improve productivity of small- and medium-scale producers, reduce the
transaction costs of market forces, and improve strategic alliances to facilitate agribusiness opportunities
that are more efficient, are factors to be addressedwithin the scope of this Phase I1Project.
About 26.5 percent of the Peruvian population (26.7 millions) is rural (World Bank 2004b), and the rural
economy is characterized by slow productivity growth, low employment-generation capacity, and high
rural poverty associated with severe social problems. The rural agricultural sector continues to be
associated with poverty, despite the presence of an extensive and potentially productive agricultural land
area (about 5.5 million hectares), that is, about 24 percent of the total area, which i s of agro-ecologically
highly diverse (selva, sierra, and coast), and recent improvements in competitiveness, development of
innovation capacity, and other developmental indexes. Agricultural competitiveness and growth are
constrained by: (a) farm size, characterized by a majority of small farms; 24.3 percent of farm units are
smaller than 1 hectare, and 55.4 percent less are smaller than 3 hectares, with the national average farm
size of 3.1 hectares; (b) water scarcities (irrigable land area in Peru i s about 28.4 percent of the arable
area); (c) low and limited access to technologies; (d) scarcity or lack of relevant information; and (e)
public sector institutional problems.
The Government of Peru (GOP) has pursued a comprehensive strategy to address economic growth and
poverty alleviation by increased social spending. The challenges and cause and effect paradigms of the
Peruvian agriculture and livestock sector illustrates the current situation that needs solutions (MOA


                                                      23

2004a, 2004b). The GOP continues to be committed to an open economy, that is, to contributing to
private-sector-led growth, and to sound economic management leading to positive growth in agncultural
gross domestic product (AgGDP) and poverty reduction. An analysis of sector concerns from the onset of
the APL Program in 1997-99 and now (covering 2000-04) indicates that most, if not all, issues raised at
that time are relevant today.

  0 Rural poverty levels and wealth inequities continue to remain high, presenting a d$ficult
    environmentfor sector growth. In 1997, 64.7 percent of rural households were in poverty, and 24.5
    percent were in severe poverty, compared to 40.4 percent of urban households in poverty, and 9.3
    percent in severe poverty. The situation has improved, but the poverty level, malnutrition, and social
    exclusion of disadvantaged groups persist. According to 2002 data, the less educated, older, and
    Quechua or Aymara native speakers dominate the rural poor, irrespective of the region. Peru i s on
    track to meet the MillenniumDevelopment Goal target of halving the undernourishment rate by 2015.
    At the same time, income, inequalities and social exclusion in the labor market are higher among
    indigenous and Afro-descendants.
  0 Lack of or weak technological innovation. During 1997-99, the lack of technological innovations
    available to farmers constrained the ability to increase productivity and improve post-harvest
    processing and to market value-adding functions.        Moreover, the level of public investment in
    agricultural research was extremely low, at 0.2 percent of agricultural gross domestic product
    (AgGDP). There have been improvements in the availability of and access to new innovations in the
    agricultural sector due to Bank and other donor interventions (Annex 2), but much of the technology
    transfer to endusers remains undelivered in the regions and to the mainstreampopulation.
  0 Natural resources degradation. During 1997-99, natural resource degradation and lack of attention
    to appropriate natural resource management (NRM) strategies were a serious threat to the
    sustainability of some agricultural production systems. These included salinization in the coastal
    soils due to poor imgation practices, erosion of susceptible soils in the selva and sierra due to poor
    land cultivation practices and overgrazing in the sierra, poor management of forest resources and
    deforestation, and overuse of pesticides. Awareness on the need to address the above-discussed
    issues has been raised, but success stories are few, and are an area that must be addressedwithin the
    National Agricultural Innovation System to mitigate adverse impacts of the use of inappropriate
    agricultural practices.
    Plant and animal health services. During 1997-99, the plant and animal health services of Peru
    were constrained by an outdated legal and regulatory system, limited capacity, and limited funding.
    These factors constituted a major constraint on the country's ability to access the international
    markets, restricting access of farmers to improved planting materials, and exposing the country to
    dangerous pests and diseases. Despite the efforts of the GOP and other donor-funded project
    activities (that is, the Inter-American Development Bank; Annex 2), there are large gaps in the public
    sector for addressingthese issues.
    Rural financial services. During 1997-99, rural financial services were limited, subsidized rural
    credit was less available, and there was widespread rural terrorism. At the time, only 30 percent of
    rural families had access to credit (Alverdo and Ugay 1998). It was deemed that an effective rural
    financial system was necessary to support a vigorous rural economy. Despite establishment of
    various social safety nets, the rural financial limitations continue to have negative impacts on
    development activities inthe agricultural sector, particularly among disadvantaged groups.
    Agricultural market development. During 1997-99, agricultural market development improved,
    with the dismantling of government monopolies and price controls, though the GOP continued to
    intervene in several small programs, which adversely impacted market development. Improved
    market information systems and a new wholesale market in Lima remained priorities for an efficient
    market system that would have spillover effects on the rest of the country. Currently, a decentralized
    marketing infrastructure led by the private sector remains the key to development of the agricultural
    sector and to an efficient and web-enabled market information system for the sector.


                                                     24

ProgramDetails
The three-phase Adaptable Program Loan (APL), a program for the development of a National
Agricultural Innovation System (NAIS, for research, extension, and knowledge and information sharing)
in Peru financed by the Bank is now in its second Phase. The focus of this Program is based on the
followingprincipals:
0   Institutional pluralism to draw on the comparative advantage and capacities of multiple institutions to
    provide research andextension services;
0   Competitive allocation of funding for research and extension services, with separation of the
    functions of financing and execution of services;
0   Co-financingcosts of research and extension with the providers and users of technology services;
0   Demand-driven provision of services with participation of users in the identification, financing, and
    implementation of such services.
The program purpose was to increase the productivity and competitiveness of the agricultural sector
through the adoption of environmentally sound technologies generatedand disseminated by a modernized
technology innovation system.
Phase Z Details. The Phase IProject development objective was to establish a private-sector-led,
decentralized technology innovation system to increase the adoption of environmentally sound
technologies. The Project, organized around three components (program and policy coordination, an
Agricultural Technology Fund (ATF), and institutional strengthening), achieved the following, measured
in terms of result indicators: (a) the establishment of three functional macro-regional programs of
extension and adaptive research, with private sector and farmer participation (Macro-regions I:Tumbes,
Piura, and Lambayeque; V: Junin, Hudnuco, Huancavelica, and Pasco; and VII: Amazonas, Sun Martin,
and the high jungle of Cajamarca). ATF activities were subsequently extended to two other macro-
regions (Macro-regions 11:Ancash, Lima, and Zca; and N:La Libertad andthe mountains of Cajamarca);
(b) more than 100 operational entities conducted the subprojects with 137 producer organizations
receiving services, with support from 175 collaborating institutions; (c) implementing agencies of the
subprojects include public and private institutions organized into strategic alliances leveragingabout 50.5
percent of subproject co-financing; (d) direct private sector co-financing (monetary) of about 28 percent
of subproject costs; (e) about 32 institutions are implementing the strategic research programs; (f) about
9,000 farmers/producers are directly involved in business plans of extension and adaptive research
subprojects, while indirect participation i s estimated at 27,000 farmers; and (g) limitedprogress was made
in the strategic researchprograms (that is, genetic resources), and some preliminary advances were made
in other strategic research programs such as biotechnology, animal and plant protection, and agricultural
economics.
Phase ZZ Details. The Phase I1Project would be organized around four components, three of which are
technology related, and one of which i s on Project implementation. (See Annex 4 for a detailed
description of the components.) The Program framework described and planned as described above (see
PAD 1999 for details) remains the same, but the instruments to achieve the goals and activities have
evolved based on Phase IProject experiences, and are reflected in the Phase I1Action Plan (Annex 4).
Several groups of institutions have been identified as potential innovation service providers for producer
demands (Table A.1.3).         Productive alliances among private entrepreneurs, nongovernmental
organizations, and government agencies would be strengthened in Phase 11, not only for the market
chains, but also to provide catalytic technical services to jump-start the adoption of market-led
innovations in agricultural ventures. The Project would continue to operate as the promoter of the NAIS
framework of the Ministry of Agriculture (MOA) within a broader public agricultural sector that involves
other institutions such as the National Agricultural Research and Extension Institute (INIEA) and
publicly funded agricultural universities.

Phase IZZ Details. Phase I11of the Program would consolidate systems for policy, regulation, operation,
and financing of a pluralistic agricultural technology system, leading to increased farmer adoption of
economically and environmentally sound technology. The system would be based on private-sector-led
institutionalpluralism in both implementation and financing. Farmers and other stakeholders would have
an increased participation in the governance of the system. Strategic national research programs would be
generating information and technologies to support adaptive research and extension programs.
Agricultural training institutions and information systems would effectively support the sector technology
programs.    Competitive allocation of resources would be central to the financing strategies. The
involvement of the NAIS thus would be a result of investment by both the public and private sectors,
which are expected to be more dynamic and sustainable. At the end of this Program, a permanent Focal
Point within the MOA would be responsible for policy, regulation, public financing, and international
cooperation of the NAIS in Peru, thereby ensuring continuity of activities and policy direction
commenced duringthe life cycles of the three Projects (APLPhases I,    11,and111).
In terms of individual outputs of the Phase 111Project, it would continue to serve farmers and farmer
organizations throughout the country, and would fund about 1,040 adaptive research and extension
subprojects. An additional 20 grants would strengthen strategic research and extension support programs.
Participating farmers would have demonstrable increases in production and income, and significant areas
would benefit from improved NRM systemsusing new technologies.

                                Annex 4: DetailedProjectDescription

                   PERU: AgriculturalResearchandExtensionAPL PhaseI1
Project Description and Development Objective
This Project (of four years' duration, 2005-09) is the second phase of a three-phase Adaptable Program
Loan (APL) (expected overall project costs: 41.98 US$ million; Bank's share: 25.0 million, GOP: 6.39
million, others: 10.59 million) that aims to increase the productivity and competitiveness of the Peruvian
agricultural sector through the generation and adoption of environmentally sound technologies
disseminated by a private-sector-led and decentralized innovation system. Phase I1would contribute to
the overall Project goal by expanding and strengthening a demand-driven rural agricultural innovation
system in Peru that i s pluralistic, decentralized, and led by the private sector. During this phase the
project will be extended nationwide from the five regions covered in the first Phase and will mainly assist
in developing a modernized service market. Phase 11of the project will also establish Technological
Innovation Policy Focal Point within the Ministry of Agriculture (MOA) that will be responsible for
policy formulation and coordination of public investments, which will contribute to the sustainability of
the national agricultural innovation system. The primary beneficiaries of the Project would be the rural
poor and vulnerable populations (including indigenous and women) nationwide. The project will
mainstream support to rural women by funding a specialized funding window for rural women
organizations. Two other important innovations with regard to vulnerable groups and sub-sectors are: (a)
higher ratings to proposals of research subprojects designed to rescue indigenous knowledge of plants
and/or indigenous agricultural systems; and (b) informal training of local indigenous young people on
basic skills of veterinary or agricultural practices so as to create improved service market.

The Loan amount for Phase I1of the Project has been reduced to US$25m from an initial amount of
US$SOm proposed at the beginning of the first phase. The reasons for this reduction are the limitedfiscal
space available inthe nationalbudget and the level of indebtednessallotted to the Ministry of Agriculture.
This reduction in the Loan amount in Phase I1will be reflected in a reduced overall Loan amount of
US$9lmfor the combined three phases of the APL compared to the amount of US$116mproposed at the
beginningof the Program.
The Phase I1 Project would continue to be implemented by the MOA (INCAGRO-PMU) using the
ongoing Phase IProject's institutional framework, with some modifications (see Annex 6, Figure A.6.1).
Other key actors in this framework include the M O A and other scientific institutions such as publicly and
privately funded universities, Regional Government representatives, and several alliance partner
organizations representing nongovernmental organizations (NGOs) and local authorities (municipal,
district, and departmental).
The Project will build on the lessons and experiences of Phase I,such as: (a) the competitive funding
model of agricultural research and extension that worked because of transparency, inclusion, quality,
efficiency, and legitimacy in the eyes of the beneficiaries and financing agencies; (b) investment in
demand-driven research alone is insufficient to address long-term problems where the allocation of funds
to strategic research issues are essential for the viability of a comprehensive program; (c) adequate
attention must be given to ensure dissemination of subprojects that are inherently dispersed, and to avoid
duplication unless it i s warranted due to agro-ecological adaptation and inherent social differences (that
is, preferences of social groups); and (d) continuous monitoring and evaluation (M&E) i s critical to
ensure that this complex, demand-driven, decentralized process produces and transfers high-quality
researchresults to the beneficiaries.
Phase I1follows the premises that: (a) the long-term strategic vision of investments in both adaptive
research and extension capacity and the strategic research capacity of the National Agricultural
Innovation System (NAIS) would yield increased employment generation, and income, and would



                                                       36

improve the well-being of the population; (b) direct involvement and leverage of funds through private
sector participation increases the quality and quantity of the market of agricultural services and products;
(c) broader application of the implementation experiencesand research outputs of Phase Iin three macro-
regions has direct application in all other macro-regions of the country and would reduce the risks of
investment, taking into account the positive and negative lessons; and (d) increased attention i s required
to stimulate or integrate the other scientific players of the NAIS in Project activities, that is, the National
Agricultural Research and Extension Institute (INIEA), the national and regional university system, other
donors that fund agricultural research (Annex 2), international organizations active in Peru, and large
multinationals that are deeply involved in the productionchain.
The components are briefly described below. The technical details and supporting study reports of each
component and the implementation plan are in the Project file. Annex 12 gives a list of all working
papers, reports of commissioned studies, and other documents used during appraisal.

Description of Project Components
The Project would be comprised of four components to be implementedover four years: (a) strengthening
the market for innovation services (Component 1); (b) strengthening the national agricultural researchand
development system (Component 2); (c) policy formulation, information, quality, and monitoring and
evaluation (M&E) (Component 3); and (d) ProgramProject implementation (Component 4). The overall
Phase I1APL Project takes a three-pronged approach to expanding and strengthening the rural agricultural
innovation system in Peruthrough three technical components and a Project implementationcomponent.
Component 1. Strengthening the Marketfor Znnovation Services
Expected cost: US$20.94 (Bank share: US$14.49 million; GOP: US$0.72 million; Others: US$5.72
million).
The aim of this component is to advance the development of a decentralized market for professional
services for agricultural innovation.     It will strengthen producer organizations as clients of quality
services, and improve entrepreneurial capacity of private service providers. The result will be a more
efficient market for services. Special emphasis will be given to the development of instruments that
would ensure equitable participation opportunities to vulnerable groups such as organizations of
indigenous and women's groups. To realize this objective, Component 1will: (a) finance a Competitive
Fund that will co-finance subprojects in adaptive research and extension based on business plans that
reflect producer demands, including the vulnerable groups such as indigenous populations and women's
organizations; (b) co-finance local participants forums, internships of pre-professionals, and training
workshops, to improve the administrative capacity of organizations and agribusiness producers, and to
strengthen the capacity of the suppliers with a managerial orientation to provision of services. The
participation of technical representatives of Regional Governments of the macro-regions would be
ensured in the evaluation, priority setting, and negotiation of subproject proposals for the competitive
fund. Implementationof the competitive fund will be decentralized at the macro-region level. The local
capacity to formulate and implement innovative subprojects that contribute to regional agricultural
competitiveness will be strengthened.
Investment Approach.         The strategy of intervention of this component postulates that, through
innovations and increased participation of producers in the market of services and the innovation system,
solutions would be provided for market-related production problems, that is, improved yield and quality,
and agricultural competitiveness, thereby reducing poverty, and making better use of natural resources.
Promotion of development of the market of services specialized in innovation is therefore crucial. On the
demand side, the following problems have been identified: (a) low capacity to pay for services by a
majority of small producers; (b) a tradition of free provision on the part of the public sector; (c) low
valuation of the services of innovation for improvement of competitiveness; and (d) limited information
available on critical aspects of service provision and its advantages. The supply side can be described as:

only source of income. Given the limited array of resources, a structural limitation exists to improve their
well-being through the adoption of innovations that improve competitiveness, thereby reducing their need
to participate in the competitivefunds.
Group II (high productive ejjiciency and low articulation with the market): These are small producers
located in communities and which, over the years, have learned to handle diversified complex production
systems, These producers operate in a framework of risk and uncertainty in production and market, the
restrictions of which limit serious adoption of profitable technologies through the fund. This producer
group will adopt a technology that gives higher yield, but at greater risk. These poor but efficient
producers are continuously looking to improve their production and increase their incomes by improving
their negotiating position with buyers. The lack of working capital, transport costs; reduced scales of
production, absence of a storage infrastructure, and lack of information are major constraints in getting
their productionto the market.
Group III (low productive ejjiciency and high articulation with the market): These producers have
adequate resources to generate full use of the economy, but have low levels of production efficiency.
These systems are well articulated to the market. A major constraint i s lack of knowledge to adopt
technological innovationsthat allow them to improve resource use efficiency and, therefore, its income.
Group IV (high productive ejjiciency and high articulation with the market): These producers have
introduced important technological changes in production through the adoption of technological
innovations, with elevated levels of efficiency in the handling of its production systems and improved
yields and income, obtaining the benefits of modernization. These technological changes include: (a)
production structure (introduction of new cultivars with improved quality with a better cost-benefit ratio);
(b) technology (adoption of new processes, for which the provision of services of extension of quality
played a decisive role); (c) management (development of managerial capacity allowing entry to new
markets); and (d) improved access to limited resources (that is, credit). An important change i s in
forming new gremial organizations and new behaviors in their managementusingan enterprise approach
that allows producer associations to demand better prices differentiated by quality of the product, and to
pay higher prices to members of the producer association.
Agricultural Technology Fund (ATF)for Strengtheningthe Marketfor Innovation Services
To realize the above-stated objectives and reach the targeted beneficiaries, the main instrument of
Component 1 will be a competitive fund that will co-finance the following subprojects, based on a
business plan that reflects producer demands, including the vulnerable groups: (a) Extension services
subprojects; (b) Adaptive Research services subprojects; (c) Capacity-building and technical assistance
(extension) subprojects for the improvement of agribusinesses led by organizations of Indigenous
Populations; and, (d) Capacity-building and technical assistance (extension) subprojects for the
improvement of agribusinesses ledby organizations of Rural Women.
Implementation of the competitive fund would be decentralized by macro-region, region, and local level.
The participation of technical representatives of Regional Governments of the macro-regions would be
ensured in the evaluation, priority setting, and negotiation of subproject proposals for the competitive
fund, The local capacity to formulate and implement innovative subprojects that contribute to regional
agriculturalcompetitiveness would be strengthened.
Key design points of the Component regarding the approach and strategy of intervention are the
following: (a) competitive allocation of resources; (b) co-financing as payment for services on the part of
organizations of producer clients of services. The degree of co-financing will be differentiated depending
on the degree of development of the market for services in the zone, and the type of market and improved
goods; (c) decentralization of the implementationof the fund, which will include the participation of local
actors in the decision-making; (d) allocation of resources will be differentiated according to the
characteristics of the target population to promote equitable participation (special instruments for



                                                    39

populations with particular limitations such as indigenous populations and women's organizations); and
(e) emphasis on strengthening the managerial capacity of agribusiness and organizations of clients and
suppliers.
The competitive fund will have the following support activities:

  0 Participatory local forums for diffusion of the Component; facilitating interactions at the local level of
     service clients and providers, and with regional and local authorities; and promoting diagnostic
     studies on competitiveness at the macro-region level, and the formulation in alliance with regional
     govemments o f action plans inwhich the innovations will play an important role.
  0 Promotional and training workshops in agribusiness methods, such as business plans, service plans of
    extension and adaptive research, logistical frameworks, balanced score card, and so forth. The
    participation of technical representatives of the Regional and Local Governments will be encouraged
    to strengthentheir capacity in technological innovations.
  0 Pre-professional training programs on: (a) practices for the improvement of the formulation and
    execution of subprojects; and (b) strengthening of producer organizations in subjects related to
    handling of design tools and management of agribusinesses such as business plans.
  0 Panels for technical evaluation of subprojects at different stages (profiles, proposals), which will
     include participation of technical representativesof each macro-Regional Government.
(a) ExtensionSubpr0.iect.sbased on business plans that address producer demands and provide solutions
to production and market problems. Subprojects of extension services will consist of the provision of
professional and specialized services oriented to addressing the demand by technical expertise, i.e.,
production, post-harvest processing, and commercialization of farming and forest products; and oriented
to improving administration and management of agribusiness and producer organizations. The priorities
will be based on demand for services as a derivative demand of the market for goods, and in agreement
with the strategic programs financed by the Component 2. Participating institutions will be the different
operators that provide innovation services, from the public and private sectors, including producer
organizations that provide services. On the demand side, the project will support producer groups as
clients of the service that are organized and have the ability to present proposals.           Collaborating
institutions, public and private, can also participate. Participation in the competitive fund must be
organized through the constitution of strategic alliances between these partners, where producer
organizations play the most important role.
ImplementationProtocol. The processof awarding subprojects would have several steps:
Stage 1: Presentation and selection of profiles. The profile of extension service subprojects constitutes
the first element of selection for the awarding of resources, and must contain a clear and concise idea in
terms of the strategy to eliminate or control the identified problem within the context of the Project
objectives. Profiles of subprojects will be selectedbasedon the following criteria: (a) identificationof the
central problem, (b) outputs and sustainability, (c) technical and scientific merit, (d) contribution to the
development of market of services, and (e) strengths of the strategic alliance.
Stage 2: Presentation and evaluation of proposals. The proposal must contain a sustainable business
plan, a service plan (supported by a logical framework and a balanced score card) and a budget for service
costs. The Technical Evaluation Panel (TEP) will conduct the technical evaluation of the proposals. The
TEP will be made up of national and international technical specialists of recognized expertise and
experience in evaluation of Extension Services subprojects.           Structuring the TEP will be done in
coordination with the technical representatives of the Regional Governments in each macro-region. With
the purpose of assuring a rigorous evaluation, a two-step approach will be taken: (a) the first stage is
eliminatory, and consists of the evaluation of criteria of output and sustainability, and structure and
content using a business plan and logical framework; (b) in the second stage of evaluation, the following
criteria will be used: technical and scientific merit, contribution to development of the market of services,



                                                       40

strength of the strategic alliance, and social and environmental aspects and agreement with the Project's
high-priority subjects.
Stage 3: Negotiation of the winning proposals with the Special Award Committee. The financial and
technical negotiation will be done with the Special Committee for awarding resources with the purpose of
determining what subprojects will become recipients of the fund. The Special Committee will negotiate
with the representatives of the proposal and interested organizations using the following criteria: (a)
incorporation of recommendations formulated by members of the TEP; (b) incorporation of
recommendations formulated by the Project team; and (c) justification of the Financial Proposal.
Stage 4: Awarding of resources of the Competitive Fund. Grants will be awarded to proponents who
fulfill the requirements established in the Agreement subscribed to, in negotiation with the Special
Committee. Final results of awarding of resources will be witnessed by a notary public and publishedin
the Project web page.
Stage 5: The agreement will be in effect when all parties have signed it.
Amounts and percentage of co-financing: The maximum Project amount will be US$62,500 for
Extension Services proposals. The annual percentage of Project co-financing will be, in average 70%,
depending on criteria indicating the degree of development of the market of services. Around 30% of co-
financing will be by the project partners (strategic alliances), mainly producer organizations. If other
institutions that are part of alliances collaborate with co-financing, producer organizations that are the
clients of the service must obligatorily co-finance at least half of the percentage corresponding to the
alliance (i.e., 15% in average).
Eligible expenditures from the fund are: (a) honorariums of the Technical Coordinator, extension
agents, and legal advisers; (b) consulting services for training and subject matter specialists; (c) training
(cost of short courses, visits, assistantships) covering such areas as travel allowances and transportation;
(d) recurrent expenses (travel allowances, passages, fuel, maintenance of equipment and vehicles,
telephone communication, access to the Internet); (e) goods (material for the activities of extension and
diffusion of results, inputs for demonstration and diffusion of results, and the purchase and renovation of
smaller equipment for extension); (f) expenses of Subproject Administration ("overhead", which is
expenses related to administration of the subproject, such as honorariums of administrators and/or
accountants, and office equipment); and (i)M&Eexpenses (consultancies for activities related to M&E).
(b) Subprojects in Adaptive Research based on business plans that address producer demands and
provide solutions to production and market problems. Subprojects of adaptive research services will
consist of implementing adaptive research experiments to solve problems that limit productivity and
competitiveness of agricultural producers using existing technologies that were successfully verified in
other situations and circumstances, but the results of which have to be adapted for use in specific regions
and locations. In summary, the adaptive research subprojects will allow obtaining solutions that are not
available to the specific problems of a given region. Other conditions of strategic alliances participation,
subproject proposal content, the process of awarding subprojects, amounts and percentage of co-
financing, and eligible expenditures from the fund are similar to the extension subprojects scheme,
described previously.
(c) Capacity-building and technical assistance subprsiects for the improvement of arrribusinesses
led bv organizations of Indigenous Populations (Andean and Amazonian). The objective i s to ensure
that indigenous populations will have equitable access to the market of innovation services, as a mean of
improving their agribusiness, respecting their economic, social, and cultural particular characteristics.
This special fund also attempts to contribute to the improvement of the determinants of social and ethnic
exclusion that indigenous populations face.
"Capacity building services" refers to activities aimed at fostering the development of a body of
knowledge and skills in the producers contractingthe service in question. "Technical assistance services"



                                                      41

are aimed at solving a specific problem, and the provider of the service is not required to transmit their
knowledgeto the producers contracting the service inquestion. Capacity buildingand technical assistance
services come under the heading of extension services. The subproject implementation period will not
exceeda maximum of (12) months.
On the side of the service recipients, groups of producers-men andor women-can participate, provided
they are active members of peasant or native (respectively Andean or Amazonian indigenous)
communities, as the case may be. Producers may be organized as communal businesses, producers'
association or other organization.      On the side of service providers, these can be individuals or
institutions-whether    public or private, national or intemational-engaged in providing capacity building
and technical assistance services. They can also be service providers, indigenous producer organization
leaders, or the organizations themselves, provided they supply this type of services. Individuals or
institutions supporting the organization of producers ("partners") will help formulate and execute the
subprojects. Any other public or private entities with an interest in contributing to the success of the
subprojects are also free to collaborate. Organized indigenous producers and their partners, and any other
interested collaborating entity will make up the Strategic Alliance in charge of presenting subproject
profiles and proposals, and, if they win the award, of executing the subprojects. The Strategic Alliances
are led by the organization of indigenous producers. A beneficiary producer organization must contribute
cash funds to co-finance payment of the services received. Collaborating entities may also contribute
monetary contributions towards the co-financing effort.

Component 2. Strengthening the National Agricultural Research and Development System
Expected cost: US$14.00 million (Bank share: US$7.27 million; GOP: US$0.40 million; others: US$6.33
million).
The aim of Component 2 i s to strengthen the agricultural research system in strategic areas of national
importance of the NAIS. The component would support "centers of excellence" in emerging areas of


                                                 45

science and technology that are critical to increasing competitiveness of the agricultural sector. This
component will specifically address issues on: (a) increasing the capacity to generate scientific and
technological information for innovation, and (b) developing competitiveness and capacities for the
management of innovation. A Competitive Fund will be the main instrument used in achieving these
goals.
Justification.  The new opportunities and challenges for agricultural competitiveness are based on
scientific technological developments that in turn contribute more to the added value that comes from the
knowledge. Evidence exists that as markets are opened and subsidies are reduced for producers, the
technical change plays a more critical role in the improvement of agrarian competitiveness. Equally,
because of the signing of the proposed Free Trade Agreements with the United States, the European
Commission, and MERCOSUR, an increase is projected in the demand for cattle products, and for high-
value fruits, vegetables, and other products of agricultural biodiversity. This demand for high-value foods
has immediate implications for research and development (R&D) policies, including the magnitude of
public financing, researchpriorities, the intellectualproperty regulations that affect the incentives derived
from innovation, and the trade regulationdecisions that affect the use of modem technologies.
It has been verified that the agrarian economy of Peru is basedon goods and servicesthat come from their
biodiversity. Currently, 65 percent of agriculture depends on native genetic resources, 95 percent of the
cattle ranches are sustainedon natural grass, and 99 percent of the forest industry uses the natural forests
and native species. In that sense, their characterization, conservation, and use are at the forefront of the
value-added knowledge chain of modem technologies, and to transformtheir comparative advantagesinto
market opportunities. As an example, biotechnology fulfills a key role in the incorporation of value
without neglecting the standardization and quality of end products, especially with reference to: (a)
development and application of friendly technologies of vegetal and animal protection in fragile
ecosystems and of new post-harvest technology; and (b) new emergent R&D areas, associatedwith new
demand for agricultural products and services tied to the development of techniques to handle renewable
natural resources (ground and water), in which farming and forest production is sustained. This implies
initial high-priority interventions in strategic programs of genetic resources and pre-breeding,
biotechnology, and vegetal and animal protection (under a concept of handling integrated pest
management [PM], diseases, and weeds), to be gradually reinforced with other programs like post-
harvest processing,handling of renewable natural resources, and organic agriculture.
During Phase I,  the Project demonstrated the demand for this type of assistance with the response to calls
for proposals on strategic research subprojects where a large number of proposals were received on
subjects related to genetic resources, biotechnology, and integrated pest management. It also illustrated
weaknesses inthe NAIS by the small number of proposals received in thematic areas such as post-harvest
technology. The opportunities and challenges generated by the new investment failed in areas where
R&D was weak, because of a lack of professionals (below critical mass), poorly equipped laboratories,
and a shortage of financial resources. This situation reflects the limited action of the State in relation to
R&D, and the resultant unfulfilled demands for innovation in the sector. These constraints are further
compounded by a lack of vision as regards research programs, and the lack of fundingpriorities.
Strategic Services Development Fund (FDSE)for Strengthening Strategic Research Capacity
Component 2 will administer a competitive fund to co-finance: (a) the development of research
subprojects in high-priority strategic research programs; (b) development of the capacity of public
research institutes to co-finance strategic research activities targeting public funds for the smallholder
agriculture; and (c) the regional implementation of competitive training curricular programs for extension
agents; and (d) the degrees and sabbaticals for specialization in the high-priority strategic programs for
researchersinemerging national and regional "centers of excellence."
(a) Strategic research subproiects. This fund will finance the generation of scientific and technological
information and technological development of new products and processes as a result of R&D in strategic



                                                      46

programs of three selectedhigh-priority thematic areas. At the beginningof the Project, priority strategic
programs will be: (a) Genetic Resources and Pre-Breeding, (b) Agro-Industrial Biotechnology, and (c)
Integrated Plant and Animal Protection (with emphasis in IPM). From the second year, and gradually,
three new priority programs will be integrated on: (d) post-harvest technology, (e) Renewable Integrated
Natural Resource Management (INRM including land, water and agro-forestry), and (0 Conservation
Agriculture.
This Fundwould also partially finance the development of the public sector capacity to generatescientific
and technological information, to develop new products and processes as a result of research and
development (R&D) in a program of strategic research for smallholder agriculture in Peru, in a manner
where a public research institute concentrate its efforts, technical capacity and financial resources in the
implementation of a number of priority and strategic subprojects of value and delivery of public goods.
Given the importance of smallholder agnculture and its inherent problems, the Project has a special
interest in financing a strategic program to address these problems and for which INIEA i s well
positioned to implement given its presence in various eco-regions of the country. This would require
certain conditions for the reform of INIEA as outlined in the Policy Letter, which accompanies this
operation, and if met this strategic program could be financed in INIEA.
Priority Setting: Workshops would be convened at the national level to prioritize target areas of strategic
research programs, accreditation programs for pre-professionals, and regional priorities, which would be
approved by the Executive Council (ExC). The Policy Focal Point under the guidance of the Science
Council (SC) will identify these priorities based on the NAIS agenda, to address researchable issues and
their impact.
Participating organizations: The institutions involved at the national and regional level will be
universities; research institutes; governmental laboratories; experimental stations; NGOs, and agri-
businesses and producer organizations with demonstrated capacity, institutional commitment, and
availability to maintain and finance, through networks and regional or national strategic programs, in a
sustainable manner. The participants' potential to form strategic alliances, will be representedby: (a) a
local institution leader; (b) integrating a critical number of national or regional and international
institutions; (c) complementing specialties; (d), resources and equipment; and (e) the comparative
advantages of each partner. The competitive fund will finance the research subprojects, as well as
strengthening human capacity and infrastructure, with a vision to form "centers of excellence." The
potential clients of the fund will be about 600 research groups in plant genetic resources; about 100
groups in zoo-genetic resources; more than 210 groups in different areas of the agro-industrial, nutrition,
and ecological biotechnology; and about 50 groups in phyto- and zoo-sanitary research. A large majority
of these groups have weaknesses in quality, resources, and access to updated scientific information and
sources of financing.
Presentation of strategic research subproject profiles:       The Project Management Unit (PMU) will
organize a call for profile proposals (pe@les) in the priority areas.         This call would include the
publication of the rules and regulations that govern the Fund and the adjudication of the proposals. This
would be in accordance with the procedures statedin the OperationalManual (OM), and the various steps
followed would be similar to those described in Component 1. The P M U would also publish workshop
events that would orient the client in project formulation and the requirements neededfor participation in
the fund.
Presentation of strategic research subproject proposals: The PMU will make the second call for the
Technical and Financial Proposals from institutions the profiles of which were selected in the first stage,
giving them the standard electronic formats per the OM. The PMU will also ratify the programming of
capacity-buildingworkshops for this stage.
Revision of profiles and strategic research subproject proposals: The PMU will check the profiles and
proposals for their fulfillment of and conformity with the legal conditions demanded, with emphasis on

requirements, and the criteria of selection, evaluation, and qualification, accompanied by a standard
electronic format of the standard profile and groups of the legal requirements, per OM guidelines. The
PMUwill also call for the programming workshop, targeting directors, specialists, and planners of the
interested institutions to implement training in the formulation of profiles and proposals of curricular
programs, strategy development, and intellectual property issues.
Presentation of proposals of curricular programs of training of extension agents: The PMUwill make a

profiles of which were selected in the first stage. Standard electronic formats will be given for the
presentation of the technical and financial proposal (OM guidelines).             The PMU will ratify the
programmingof training workshops inthis second stage.

Revision of profiles and proposals of curricular programs of extension agents:              The profiles and
proposals will be revised by the PMU, which will verify the fulfillment and the conformity of the legal
conditions neededto participate in the program, with emphasis on strength and pertinence of the strategic
alliance and specialists team, the institution, the alliance, hiringof professionals, and their regionalorigin.
In each stage, the PMU will contract, according to OM guidelines, individual consultants for the PET,
with a minimumof six members, by region, including: an expert each in training competence evaluation,
adult education methods, and education technology, and at least three experts in specific thematic areas.
The criteria for selection of profiles will include: (a) the coherence of the competency to be reached with
the identified training needs, (b) the technical merit of the methodology and proposed instruments of
training, (c) the institutional capacity developed or to be developed to ensure sustainability of the
proposed curricular program, (d) its contribution to the improvement of quality and competitiveness of
extension services, (e) its contribution to regional agrarian development, and (0 its potential for
replicability and accessibility to the target population.
