objective of providing national or international experience. The identification of prestigious academic
centers and the selection of candidates, and the management of handling of postgraduate programs will be
according to the priorities indicatedabove.
Priority setting: The thematic areas of training and specialization will be selected from the priorities of
each region, and at the national level as generatedby each Strategic Research Program, having considered
the necessary professional critical mass for the efficient management, generation, and use of scientific
information, technological innovation, and regional and national agrarian competitiveness.
Postgraduate programs with potential for being selected: The entities contracted for the management of
research scholarships will identify the postgraduate programs in the prioritized thematic areas, and in
national or international universities, and will produce a database based on the criteria of quality and
pertinence. The selection of the postgraduateprograms will take into account the historical prestige of the
academic center and its postgraduate program, the educational platform of the university, national and
international educational visitors, and research themes within the program to be developed by the
scholarship holders. The entities contracted for the management of research scholarships will present a
potential program to the PMU. The PMU will review the strict fulfillment of the requirements and will
ask for the specialized opinion of the SC, and report their recommendations for approval by the ExC.

priorities in the innovation system. The researchers will have to demonstrate their bond with the
institution, and to guarantee their return to the same after completing their studies, and also present a
high-quality research proposal within their center of excellence.           The entity contracted for the

Component 3. Monitoring and Evaluation (M&E), Information Sharing, and Policy for Innovation
Expected cost: US$5.53 million (Bank share: US$2.25 million; GOP: US$3.28 million).
The aim of Component 3 is to develop the institutional capacity of the public sector to formulate and
implement agricultural innovation policy in conjunction with the private sector, and regulate the quality
of the NAIS. The outcome would be the establishment of a Technological Innovation Policy Focal Point
in a modernized MOA that is responsible for the definition and implementation of policies that promote
and provide incentives on activities and investments in science, technology and innovations inthe sector.
The outputs would be: (a) institutionalization of processes, priorities, and policies to promote and protect
innovations with consensus within the system and policy definition in critical areas such as promotion of
public investments in science and agricultural technology, intellectual property rights, and public-private
partnerships; (b) validation of an M&E system to generate performance measurement standards to
measure the results of modemization of the NAIS services; and (c) a modernized and systematized
information system composed of virtual networks that will increase the sector's capacity to generate and
use systematizedand updated scientific and technological information.
Investment approach. DuringPhase 1of the Project, the decentralized technological program was begun
in three macro-regions I,V, and VI1 (see Annex 6, Table A.6.1). These three macro-regions were
selected based on their potential for increase in production, present relative deficiency of technological
services, and the existence of producer organizational capacity and interest. InPhase 11, the Project will
continue in those three macro-regions, and then gradually expand to the other regions, installingtwo new
units in the second year, and three more in the third year. This component responds to the need to
emphasize the intention of the Project to institutionalize, within the public agrarian sector (MOA), a focal
point for policies, standards, and promotion of an NAIS. Most of the institutions supported by Phase Iare
expected to compete for funds in Phase 11, which will assist in creating a critical mass of interventions.
The Phase Iset of subprojects also provides useful information to establish quality standards at the
process level, and for impact analysis. The identification of these implementation standards permits
establishment of emergent best practices more precisely in Phase 11, and to disseminate these best
practices for public and private resource allocation. This will have a positive effect on proposals of
quality standards and the generation of an agricultural information platform, and for better definition of
public sector policies.    The subprojects completed in Phase Iand in progress are the "seeds" or
gravitational centers that attract the next set of actors in the system, to establish physical and virtual
networks.
Responsibilities. This component would have the following responsibilities:




                                                     52

        to set up an efficient M&E systemto coordinate and provide feedback on all components and co-
        financed subprojects, and to propose best-practice standards for the benefit of research and
        extension services;
        to develop a sustainable information platform on science and technology, quality standards, and
        NAIS researchers (research and extension agents of the system) profiles, that is continuously
        updated;
        to propose definition and periodically review strategic programs of science, technology, and
        innovation with the private sector, and set priorities and agendas for these programs.
Activities of Component 3: The activities to be financed would be the following (Tables A.4.9 and
A.4.10): (a) studies and consultations on prospective technologies and technological forecasting,
elaboration of a concerted agenda of the innovation system, identification of the constraints on the
development, adoption, and dissemination of innovations, and definingpriorities and formulating policies
in potential strategic areas of innovations;       (b) an M&E system for subprojects using measuring
instruments that permit comparison of designs, identify best practices of quality, submit information to
the public scrutiny, reward the best subprojects, and ensure the achievement of results and desired
impacts.   The M&E system will therefore collate lessons based on the total Project, individual
subprojects, and related other projects, and maintain a system of continuous learning and feedback
leading to formulation of "sector quality standards" which, as best practices, will be diffused and also
recognized by means of appropriate awards; and (c) an information systemlplatform that is in the public
domain and enriched with data generated initially by subprojects, operational plans, and programs in
place, andlater by other actors of the sector and will be made available to all stakeholders of NAIS.

This unit will be composed o f (a) a unit head and four technical specialists in the areas of general,
environmental, agricultural, socioscientific, and information systems; and (b) an executive assistant. The
main functions of the unit will be to: (1) establish an M&E systemfor NAIS in the agricultural sector; (2)
establish an information technology base for the NAIS; and (3) establish a Focal Point of policies, and
standards for and the promotion of the NAIS in the MOA. The technical specialists must have Masters
Titles and excellent field experience in their area of expertise.         The professional disciplines and
responsibilities will be: (i)an agricultural technology specialist to supervise studies on production and
agricultural productivity, experimental designs, and plans for strategic and adaptive researchand business
plans; (ii)an extension services specialist for planning and monitoring of the progress of technical
agrarian production activities, institutional development, training, and studies of financing of agricultural
technology programs; (iii)an environmental specialist to develop procedures for the control of
environmental and agricultural impacts at the national, regional, and community level; to select
subproject activities with negative environmental impacts and incorporate mitigation measures; to plan
activities with beneficial environmental impacts, and to control Program activities for environmental
impacts; (iv) a socio-economist to support the ex ante and ex post economic impact analyses, and
agricultural income and market studies; supervision of the fulfillment of business plans, supervision of
impact studies of technical programs on different vulnerable groups (indigenous, poor, and women
population segments); coordination of training activities of qualification and planningto improve fairness
in the participationof vulnerable groups, and supervision of studies on extension approaches, technology
needs, and strengthening of producer organization; and (e) an information systems specialist.
The outputs of this component (Table A.4.10) would be: (a) validation of a monitoring and evaluation
system to measure the results of modernization of the agricultural innovation system; (b) a modernized
and systematized web-based information system to assist the generation and sharing of knowledge; and
(c) policy definition on critical areas such as the promotion of public investments in science and
agricultural technology, intellectual property rights, and public-private            partnerships; and (d)
Technological Innovation Policy Focal Point in the MOA.

Component 4. Program/Project Implementation
Expectedcost: US$2.51 million (Bank share: US$0.86 million; GOP: US$1.65 million).
The aim of Component 4 is to finance the present Project Management Unit (PMU), which would
continue to be the implementing agency for Phase I1 of this Program to strengthen the national
agricultural science, technology, and innovation system.        The PMU would provide for project
coordination and administration staff support in procurement, financial management, reporting, M&E,
andknowledge management facilities.
The main responsibilities of the PMU would be: (a) the efficient administration of the organizational
structure of the Project and implementation of its work plan, including annual budgets, and monitoring
and evaluation of the Project at the national level; (b) to contract competent personnel for the
administration of all the Components of the Project, and to evaluate it periodically; (c) administer the
Competitive Funds for the allocation of resources of Components 1, 2, and 3 of the Project; (d) ensure
that the payments of the subproject implementing organizations are efficiently carried out; (e) be the
principal contact with the World Bank in project supervision and financial management of the Bank's
loan and other public resources; (f) operate a special account of the loan and convene annual program
audits; (8) ensure quality assessments of Project implementation in terms of social impacts and
environmental safeguards; (h) maintain the O M up to date; and (i)guide the evolution of the PMU as
outlined in the Policy Letter toward a permanent entity in the MOA that would have responsibility for the
implementation of public programs in innovation of the rural agriculture sector. The Executive Council
would oversee overall Project implementation, with technical advice provided by the Scientific Council.
This component will also oversee the activities shown inTable A.4.12 andthe respective budget inTable
A.4.13.
The Project organizational structure and different functions of each staff member, implementation
arrangements and responsibilities are outlined inFigure A.6.1 and described in detail inAnnex 6.

                              Annex 6: ImplementationArrangements

                   PERU: Agricultural Researchand ExtensionAPL PhaseI1
Implementation of the Project
Overview. The institutional framework of the Proyecto de Investigacidn y Extensio'n Agricdla para la
Innovacidn y Competitividad del Agro Peruano (INCAGRO) established by the Government under the
Ministry of Agriculture (MOA) during Phase Iof the APL Program that was proven effective would
continue to be used to implement the Phase I1Project. The Project Management Unit (PMU) would carry
out the overall coordination of the Project, with the oversight of the Executive Council (ExC) at the
national level, assisted by Macro Regions Consultative Councils (MRCC) in each region.                  The
composition of the PMU and implementing responsibilities of the subunits for each component are
described in detail in Annex 4 (also refer to Figure A.6.1). The PMU that is placed within the MOA
(operational line ministry) but outside the established institutional framework for agriculture or science
and technology provides the desired neutrality to address crosscutting issues entailed by the Project's
action plans, and to interact with various ministries on different issues. With regard to the flow of funds,
the M O A will conclude a loan agreement, and the Bank loan will be disbursed by direct payments to a
special account, and the fund disbursements will be channeled through the INCAGRO-PMU. A detailed
flow of funds for each component, and for funds targeting subprojects and decentralized units (DUs) is
described inAnnex 7.
Project Oversight and Advisory Functions
The Executive Council (ExC) i s the highest oversight body of the Project, which will be made up of high-
caliber people with appropriate technical knowledge and experience. The ExC, constituted by Ministerial
Resolution, will be presided over by the Minister of Agriculture that will provide policy direction for the
Project would include specialized committees for, inter alia, science, finance, and programs. The ExC
will have nine members, including the Chair of the Committee. The other members will be selected and
designated based on the merit of their outstanding work in the following sectors: (1) public and private
agricultural research; (2) public and private agricultural extension; (3) science, technology, and
innovation; (4) natural resources and the environment; (5) financing; (6) agricultural-based industry and
agro-export; and (7) vulnerable groups (organizations of rural women and indigenous communities). The
Executive Director of the Project will act as the Secretary of the ExC, being an ex officio member. The
Operational Manual (OM) of the Project that was approved by Ministerial Resolution will establish the
operational procedures of the ExC. The functions of the ExC will include: (a) selection of the Executive
Director; (b) approval of the OM; (c) approval of ExC regulations; (d) evaluation of the Project and
program implementation; (e) approval of the Annual Operational Plans; (f) revision and approval of the
periodic and annual reports; (g) approval of the annual budgets; (h) general evaluation of outputs; and (i)
providing information and analysis to the Minister of Agriculture on technological policies and proposals
of changes in programs and national policies on science, technology, and innovation. The members of the
ExC will be periodically changed without affecting the continuity of Project activities or losing the
strategic approachas statedin the OM.

Project Staffing Structure
The PMU staff composition and workflow, and decentralized Project implementation structure,
workflows, and staffing details are given in Figure A.6.1. The PMU would provide support for Project
coordination and administrative staff, procurement, financial management, reporting, and M&E for all
components. The Executive Project Director would report to the Deputy Minister of the MOA. The
PMUwill, among other things, act as the overall Project signatory in all agreements with subproject co-
financing and implementingentities.




                                                     57

The Executive Management of the PMU will be composed of four staff (1) the Executive Project
Director, who would be responsible for facilitating the interactions among different operational
components and Project units, and promoting development of activities of high technical and scientific
quality. Additional responsibilities will be to: assure the efficient administration of resources and M&E
activities of the Project, offer technical and scientific leadership to the Project, implement the decisions of
the ExC, and represent the Project at the national and internationallevel; (2) the Legal Advisor, who will
function as an Adviser to the Project Director and the Project on all legal aspects and on the application of
the relevant regulations; (3) the Planning and Budget Specialist, who will provide support to the Project
Director and the Project related to the following subjects: formulation and sustainability of institutional
instruments such as the Strategic Plans, Annual Operational Plans, and other instruments of Project
planning, in coordination with the Project Director and the Project Unit; formulation and presentation to
the corresponding superior levels of the periodic reports about Project progress on the basis of outputs
reported by the corresponding units; coordination of relevant mechanisms for the permanent flow of
budgetary information and the fulfillment of goals to the pertinent entities such as the MOA, the Ministry
of Finance, the World Bank, and others that request such information; and active coordination with other
Project units providing useful information for the preparation of information, such as developing other
tasks that are designated to or delegatedby the ProjectDirector; and (4) an Executive Assistant.
The Project will have three Technical Units:the Strengthening of Market of Services for the Innovation
Unit; the Strengthening of Strategic Competence for Research Unit; and the Policies, Information
Exchange, and Quality of Services of Innovation Unit.
Strengthening the Market for Innovation Services. This Unit will be responsible for implementing
Component 1 of the Project, in accordance with the objectives, strategies, and goals that are presented in
Annex 4. This Unit will include: the Unit Head, two Technical Specialists including an Agricultural
Specialist, and a Technical Assistant. The main responsibilities of the Unit will be to: (a) implement
strategies to contribute with the development and strengthening of the market for innovation services; (b)
manage the process of the competitive fund; (c) propose profiles of the members of the Profiles
Evaluation Panels (PEPS)and Technical Evaluation Panels (PETS)based on technical qualification; (d)
identify demands for extension and adaptive research services, through participatory local forums and
other related activities; (e) conduce actions for the execution of promotion and capacity-building
workshops; (f) formulate and coordinate the implementation of the Action Plan for Indigenous Peoples
and the Action Plan for Rural Women Organizations; (g) promote the Project and the Component in each
region; and (h) inform the Project Director and Unit Heads of Components 2 and 3 of the completed
activities and outputs within respective mandates.
Strengthening of the National Research and Development (R&D) System. This Unit will have the
responsibility of implementing Component 2 of the Project, in accordance with the objectives, strategies,
and goals as presentedin Annex 4. The main responsibility of the Unit will be to lead a competitive fund
directed to: (a) strengthen strategic national and international alliances, to co-finance the development of
capacities and research activities; (b) regional programs of training of extensionists and to co-finance
curricular programs for institutional development; and (c) co-finance scholarships for researchers of
national and regional centers in specialties of high-priority strategic research. Other responsibilities of
this Unit will be to: (i) prepare, propose, and direct the necessary operations for financing of analytical
studies for planning in high-priority research areas, training, and information exchange; (ii)         prepare,
propose, and direct the necessary financial operations for institutional strengthening of the technical
program; and (iii)  elaborate and implement the competitive fund. The Unit will be composed of the Unit
Head, and one Technical Specialist, preferably with a specialization in agronomy.
Policies, Znformation Exchange, and Znnovation Policy Unit.                This Unit will be responsible for
implementing the Component 3 work plan and all decentralized units (DUs). The staff composition and
responsibilities of M&Eactivity and DUs are described in the following sections.

The process of expansion of the Project during its lifetime will be staggered annually as indicated in
Table A.6.2. A set of criteria that have been considered to select the decentralized macro-regions on an
annual basis will be accompanied with administrative offices established in each macro-region to
facilitate the administration of Project interventions. These criteria were: a) the key variables usedfor the
definition of the target population of the Project, as related to the productive efficiency and degree of
market definition and relationships with the market (presence of agro-industrial and product chains in
relation to the demand, public service operators and their capacity for technological innovation),
economic and infrastructure context; b) the potential for farming and forest resources to respond to the
globalization of the market (potential of impact of services for innovation); and c) aspectsthat harness the
success of the intervention strategy of the Project (greater institutional development - leading producer
organizations, greater capacity and disposition to the payment for services and greater relative
development of the market of services for innovation). Based on these criteria, the following sequence of
entry into decentralized macro-regions has been defined (Table A.6.3). The shaded boxes correspond to
the opening of new decentralized offices in the various DU's.


Table A.6.3. Project ExpansionPlanand Establishment of Decentralized Offices inMacro-Regions




Project Monitoring and Evaluation (M&E)Scheme
The M&E process has two objectives: (a) to attain efficient administration of resources and deliver the
planned Project deliverables, and (b) to ensure collaboration among diverse actors in order to multiply
benefits for the NAIS.
Implementation Mechanisms. Under the leadership of INCAGRO-PMU, the M&Eteam will produce the
Annual Operation Plans, Regional Annual Operation Plans, Semi-Annual Monitoring and Evaluation
Reports, External Evaluation by an independent entity, and Operational and Subproject Manuals, as
required. In addition to the subprojects evaluations using set indicator targets, the M&Eteam would also
consolidate information on the rural agricultural sector for the benefit of the Project. The quality of
delivery would be ensured by M&E analyses of the co-financed subprojects in comparison to market,
sector, and global standards.

Subproject Monitoring and Evaluation
Subproject Implementation at the Local Level.         The subproject cycle would include three stages:
formulation, implementation, and evaluation. The responsibility for formulation and quality of design
will reside with the respective promoters of the subproject proposals. In each cycle of the calls for
proposals, the selection of subprojects for funding and their implementation would be done in a public
and open manner using an appropriate tool kit expressing the current NAIS standards, and to orient the
operations to include recent advances in innovations. The monitoring process of subprojects duringtheir
implementation stage would ensure meeting set standards by adjusting as needed and by improving the
efficiency of resource allocation and use within the agreed activity plans. Since the final Project
beneficiaries are the rural small producers, they would play a key and active role in assessingthe delivery
of results of subprojects and the quality of services. Special attention would be given to the new
subprojects and to new alliances and partner institutions to ensure that the agreedrules are followed. The
main contribution of the Project is to follow a stringent and effective selection process, thereby assigning
the scarce resources to the most promising proposals, and to provide incentives to the implementing
agencies to make efficient use of such resources and achieve the most outputs as possible.
The PMU will enter into agreements with subproject implementing agencies (producer organizations,
community-based organizations, nongovemmental organizations (NGOs), universities, and other
organizations) to transfer the project funds as grants of the relevant Competitive Fund in order to execute
the subprojects at the local level. The Operations Manual will detail the ground rules for approving and
implementingthe subprojects.
Flow of Funds. In accordance with the institutional and implementation arrangement described above,
funds will flow from the PMU to the respective subprojects. The intermediary beneficiaries would be
public institutions, public-private partnerships in research, other alliances such as NGOs, producer
organizations, agribusiness consortiums, and so forth. The final targeted beneficiaries of this Project
would be the rural farmers, producers, agro-entrepreneurs, and consumers or end users, including
vulnerable groups such as indigenous populations and women.
Responsibilitiesof the M&EPlan
Planning. A Planning Specialist would be contracted by the ExC to: (a) document and guide the
directives on sector planning, orientation of the ExC, coordination of the administration of units, the
Annual Operational Plan (OP) that serves as the base for Project implementation, and the calendar of
agreements; (b) elaborate the continuing modifications and updates of the OP, and report advances,
modifications, and adjustments of the OP as needed; (c) evaluate the implementation progress of the OP
and prepare a Final Executive Report; (d) on behalf of the Project Director, using periodic progress
information provided by individual units, prepare Project information sheets for the use of various
national organizations and the Bank; and (e) prepare terms of references and monitor the work of Project
consultants.
Technical Team. A team of five Specialists would provide the necessary multidisciplinary expertise
requiredby the subprojects. The profiles of these Specialists are: (1) Unit Head; (2) Policy Specialist, to
conduct studies on agricultural productivity, ex ante and ex post analysis of economic impact, agricultural
income, and market studies, institutional development and investments in technological programs and
evaluation of the analytical framework of the NAIS; (3) an Agricultural Specialist, to conduct studies on
the impact of proposed technologies and their reliability, research and experimental design, and studies on
new farming systems and opportunities; (4) an Environmental Specialist, to conduct studies that aid in
developing procedures to mitigate adverse impact on the environment at the agricultural, community,
national, and regional level; identify negative impact potential of subprojects and recommend alternatives
to reduce damage; plan activities with beneficial environmental impacts and advise the Project on
environmental safeguards and give appropriate advice on ESRP; and (5) an Information Specialist, to
prepare and administer an up-to-date digitized database (management information system [MIS]) that



                                                     62

would serve subproject M&E and all other NAID stakeholders. It would also house all metadata related
to production systems, actors, technologies, and innovationsystems.

Provincial Level.     The Provincial level DUs would have two Subject Matter Specialists and an
Administrative Assistant to follow the Project and subproject M&E plans at the local level.
Informution Flow. The Project is expected to generate information at two levels: at the subproject and at
the component level. The subproject-generatedinformation would be kept up to date in the web-based
information system, and would ensure transparency of Project management. The information generated
from each component would be used for overall Project evaluation, and for monitoring of individual
activities and to keep all interested parties informed. This i s the responsibility of the Planning and Budget
Specialist adjunct to the Executive Project Director.
The key monitoringtools for M&Eand for information sharing will be composed of five elements:
(a) Management Information System (MIS). The MIS would be based on quarterly reports generated by
    each component units. All subproject proposals would include baseline data, performance and impact
    indicators, and targets and M&E plans. Project-implementing agencies would be responsible for
    reporting, and the Regional unit heads and the M&E unit would be responsible for assuring
    compliance with the reportingrequirements. The M&Eunit would maintain the consolidated Project
    database that would contain performance data and impact indicators, and would provide quarterly
    reports to the PMU and Executive Committee outlining the progress and problem areas. Regional
    unit heads and the M&E unit would evaluate the subproject implementation semiannually using a
    highly satisfactory (HS), satisfactory (S), moderately satisfactory (MS), moderately unsatisfactory
    (MU), unsatisfactory (U), and highly unsatisfactory (HU) rating scale, presented in the semiannual
    report and usedas a guide for corrective measures;
(b) Impact Case Studieswould be conducted to measureprogramoutcomes;
(c) Special studies would provide guidance on program impacts and ways of improving program
    efficiencies and effectiveness;
(d) Strategic Program Peer Reviews. These would assess strategic program development subprojects
    with respect to technical direction of the programs, their scientific quality, program performance,
    institutional linkages, and potentialimpacts; and
(e) Annual Program Pe$ormance Reviews. These would assess the program operations, procedures, and
    functioning of the PMU. The third program review would be a special review to assess triggers and
    plans for Phase I11of the APL program, which would be undertaken in the third year of the Project by
    a team of four external consultants (two internationaland two local) over a period of three weeks.
Capacity for maintaining the quality standards. To ensure the meetingof quality standards of M&E, the
DU personnel would receive training throughout the life of the Project to strengthen their capacity to
evaluate the subprojects and provide assistance to extension service providers. The Project will actively
participate in various technology networks and other sector and donor-funded projects and donor
coordination forums to upgrade knowledge in new methodologies and techniques of M&E, and to update
the awareness of the Government, donor community, and civil society on the Project outputs. Project
specialists would also participate in knowledge-building training. A training workshop mode would be
usedfor this purpose.




                                                       63

               Annex 7: Financial Management and Disbursement Arrangements

                   PERU: Agricultural Researchand ExtensionAPL PhaseI1

A Joint Financial Management and Disbursement Assessment were performed by the regional Senior
Financial Management Specialist (FMS) and LOA Senior Finance Officer, in accordance with OP/BP
10.02 and the Guidelines for the Assessment of Financial Management Arrangements in the World Bank
Financed Projects. The evaluation was performed on site during the pre-evaluation mission from August
25 to 27, 2004 and updated during the appraisal mission on October 26 to 28, 2004. During the period
leading up to negotiations, technical guidance was provided to the INCAGRO team with respect to the
actions detailed in the Action Plan preparedduringappraisal.
On February 21, 2005 and before negotiations, the Senior Financial Management Specialist (FMS) visited
the project to review the documents and evidence in support of the completion of the Action Plan which
was agreed during appraisal.
Executive Summary and Overall Conclusion
Overall, during Phase Iof the APL, the Project Management Unit (PMU) has maintained financial
management arrangements, systems, and practices that are satisfactory to the Bank, and are adequate to
provide the Bank with accurate and timely information regarding project resources, expenditures, and
activities.
Entity specific and control risks have been assessed as moderate. During appraisal, the major capacity
constraints related to: a) the inability of the current information system to produce Financial Monitoring
Reports (FMRs) to support project management; b) inadequate monitoring mechanism for overseeing the
financial operations of the decentralized units; c) record keeping and filing which fragments the audit trail
of transactions; d) the lack of synchronization between project planning, execution of payments and
authorization (within the Ministry of Finance (MEF) of withdrawal application requests to the Bank, and
(e) capacity to transition into Report-baseddisbursements mechanism.
Whereas in the Phase I,   the impact of these constraints was not material, the Phase I1requireda quantum
leap, in order to mitigate the risks posed by the expansion in project complexity and scope. As reflected
in the updated Action Plan, the PMU has (1) upgraded the information systems and produced a
comprehensive User Manual which reflects the internal control processes and procedures; (2) the system
has the capacity to produce Financial Monitoring Reports, which can be used as the basis for
disbursement; (3) a Financial Analyst has been hired and trained and monitoring mechanism have been
instituted to oversee the financial operations of decentralized units; (4) record keeping and filing systems
and procedures have been prepared and implemented in order to ensure an appropriate audit trail; (5) the
Draft Operations Manual has been submitted to the Bank for review and comment; and (6) extensive
training sessions to improve Financial, Disbursement and Procurement capacity have been conducted in
two decentralized regions. Once the scaling up of the integrated financial management system i s
satisfactorily implemented, the residual inherent and control risks will be mitigated and the Bank will
have no objection to approvingthe implementationof the new disbursement mechanism.

Organization and Staffing
Details of the project implementationstructure, workflows and staffing have already been describedinthe
Annex 6, and overall, there i s adequate capacity (numbers, experience, and qualifications) in the Finance
and Administration Unit of the PMU, to handle budgeting, procurement, accounting, internal controls,
and reporting functions. In order to scale up and respond to the increased scope and operation of the
Phase 11, recommendations have been made to further strengthen the organizational capacity, and a new
position of the Financial Analyst has been created and filled, to provide financial supervision of the
decentralized unitsin each macro-region (see the map).



                                                      64

AccountingPoliciesandProcedures
The project will be implemented in accordance with the: 1) National Control System, which is regulated
by the Supreme Audit Institution; and 2) the National Accounting System, regulated by the Accountant
General. The chart of accounts used in the first phase (based on the Plan Contable Gubemamental del
Sistema Nacional de Contabilidad) has been adequately adapted to reflect the new project design, i.e.,
components, disbursement categories and financing sources and accounts will continue to be prepared on
a cash basis. The PMU has prepared a draft Operational Manual that had been reviewed by the Bank
before negotiations. Bank's comments will be included inthe final version of the Operational Manual.
BudgetingProceduresandImpactonFlow ofFunds
Planning and Budgeting will be in line with general government procedures regulated by the Annual
Budget Law operatedunder the Sistema Integrado de Administracion Financiera (SIAF).The Procedures
in effect under APL Iwill continue inforce and budgetary control will consist o f (1) timely preparation
and approval of annual programs and budgets (at the three levels, Le., Central INCAGRO-PMU,
Decentralized Units (DUs) and Beneficiary Institutions); and (2) verification that budget information is
entered and available in the accounting systemto allow timely recording of commitments, payments and
accruals. Since the ability to make projections of cash flow requirements will be a key to successful
implementation of report-based disbursements, in the Phase 11, the Project will need to successfully
remedy the lack of synchronization among project planning and execution of payments and authorization
within the MEF of withdrawal application requests to the Bank, which was a major contributor to bottle
necks to disbursements, under the Phase I.To mitigate this risk, the Project will need to prepare and
analyse on a quarterly basis budget execution variances.


DisbursementArrangements
DuringPhase 1of the APL, disbursement arrangements were based on the traditional transaction-based
mechanism. Given the nature of this project and that the bulk of disbursements for the subprojects (which
represents more than 80% of total project) are processed in tranches in accordance with the financing
agreements with beneficiaries resulting from each competitive fund, and to facilitate alignment of the
project request for disbursements with the allocated budget for this project for each year of
implementation, the Bank team evaluated the possibility of transitioning this project from the current
disbursement arrangements (transaction-based) for the Phase Iinto Report-based for Phase 11. As
described previously, a time-bound Action Plan including scaling up of the integrated financial
management systemwas agreedupon with the PMUto be completed before negotiations of the loan.
At appraisal, the Bank team reviewed with the project staff the progress made and followed up on the
Action Plan, taking stock of items which were still in progress or pending to be addressed or completed,
with particular emphasis in evaluating the readiness of preparingFinancial Monitoring Reports (FMRs)
(including reports required to support Report-based disbursements, and the adequacy of system to
generate such reports). Due to delays experienced by the Project with preliminary activities for the
systemenhancementsfor the integrated financial management system, the Project at the time did not have
the capacity to transition into Report-based Disbursements. As described in the Executive Summary
section of this Annex, the Bank team evaluated the completion of the actions completed to scale up the
integrated financial management system before negotiations, and the project has the capacity to prepare
FMRs in support of disbursements. However, given that any system enhancements requires a period of
testing and implementation, the continuation of current disbursement arrangements (transaction-based)
for the Phase Iis kept as a choice for the Phase IIin case there i s any need to switch to transaction-based
mechanism during implementation. Thus, the following sections include provisions to cater for each
option.

SpecialAccount,FundsFlow and ReportingRequirements
Irrespective of the disbursement mechanism to be implemented for this project, and to facilitate project
implementation, the project will have access to funds advanced by the Bank to a Special Account in US
dollars for processing disbursements to beneficiaries, for those eligible expenditures under project
activities. The Special Account (SA) will be opened by the borrower inBanco de la Nacidn (Government
commercial bank) and will be managed by the central INCAGRO-PMU established within Ministry of
Agriculture responsible for implementing the Project (no sub-accounts for decentralized regional offices




                                                     66

have been considered or approved as the central PMU is responsible for financial management and
disbursements of project funds).
Funds deposited into the Special Account as advances (depending on the disbursement mechanism) will
follow the Bank's disbursement operating policies and procedures established for each mechanism as
described in the Legal Agreement and in the DisbursementLetter as the case may be.
The specific detailed funds flow for the subprojects (each competitive fund may entail different funds
flow arrangements to the ultimate beneficiary) will be describedin the Operational Manual, and the Sub-
Project Operational Manual, but in general terms, a community-driven development (CDD) approach will
be used to the extent possible and provided the central INCAGRO-PMU will exercise its fiduciary and
oversight role with the decentralized regional units and executing entities (beneficiaries) in each region.
Funding to executing entities will be made based on lump-sum amounts approved in the Annual
Operating Plan for each executing entity at beginning of the year and under the basis of the financing
agreement signed among INCAGRO and each beneficiary. The financing agreement i s described in the
contract signed between INCAGRO and each beneficiary executing entity after awarding the contract.
The initial tranche payment is considered as an advance until progress is reviewed by INCAGRO and
documented in the first Technical and Financial Report of each executing entity. As subsequentprogress
payments are made to executing entities, INCAGRO-PMU should maintain appropriate internal control
mechanism and supervision through out the subproject implementation so as to ensure that subproject
activities for which financing was provided through transfers of loan funds made to executing entities are
completed before the loan closing date.
Transaction-based disbursements: In this case, an Authorized Allocation (AA) for advances made into
the Special Account will be established. This AA would be set to a maximum of $2,000,000 and the
project could request such advance as neededonce the loan i s declared effective.
Once the initial deposit(s) has been provided, the PMU will submit subsequent withdrawal applications
requests for replenishments to the Special Account on a monthly basis. All withdrawal applications will
be fully supported by appropriate supporting documentation (Le. invoices, receipts, and any other
evidence of payment) except for those expenditures for contracts not subject to prior review and for which
the Bank has approved the use of Statement of Expenditures (SOEs), as referred to in the Legal
Agreement and the Disbursement Letter. The Central PMU in INCAGRO will be responsible for
preparing and submitting withdrawal applications to the Bank. All supporting documentation of
withdrawal applications (including those for which SOEs are used) should be retained by the PMU at its
central location and be available for review by the Bank supervision missions and independent auditors.
Withdrawal from the loan account could be made on the basis of SOEs for the following expenditures:
(a) contracts for goods costing less than US$200,000 equivalent, (b) contracts for consulting firms costing
less than US$50,000, (c) contracts for individual consultants costing less than US$25,000, (d) works, (e)
training, (f) all subprojects, and (g) operating costs (Table A.7.2).

Report-based Disbursements: In the case that withdrawals of loan proceeds are made using Report-
based disbursements, the Bank will deposit into the Special Account an amount which the Bank has
determined, based of the reports submitted (FMRs and additional reports for disbursement purposes) that
i s requiredin order to finance eligible expenditures during the next two period (no more than 6 months)
following the reportingperiod of such FMRs.
The core FMRS will include:
a) A financial section specifying sources and applications of project resources and a statement of
     investment by project components/activities reporting the current quarter and the accumulated
     investment against ongoing plans;
b) An output monitoring section that: (i)     describes physical progress in the implementation of the
     project, both cumulative and for the period covered by the report; and (ii)
                                                                               explains variances between
     the actual and previously forecast implementationtargets; and
c) A procurement section setting forth the status of procurement under the project and expenditures
     under contracts financed out of the proceeds of the loan, as of the end of the period covered by the
     report.
To support report-based disbursements, the following additional reports have been discussed and review
by the Bank. The format and content of such reports has been agreedupon before negotiations:
     a) Special Account Activity Statement;
     b) Summary Statement of Special Account Expenditures (for those expenditures subject to prior
         review and below prior review other than subprojects);
     c) Summary Statement of Special Account Expenditures for subprojects (which are not subject to
         Bank prior review).
The format and content for reporting the Forecast of expenditures for next two reporting period (i.e. 6
months) has not been reviewed before negotiations as the PMU will prepare this report in line with the
budget submission to MEF using the Budget module in SIAF. Given the nature of the subprojects
(demand-driven through competitive funds) and the timing of disbursing the funds through the
implementation cycle (maximum 3 years) per subproject, particular attention was given to discuss the




                                                    68

design (format and content) of the disbursements under this component based on the new disbursement
mechanism and the experience so far with the phase Iof the APL.
Incase of Report-based Disbursements,the borrower should submit withdrawal applications as follows:
    (a) Initial Withdrawal Application: A request for withdrawal of loan funds using the appropriate
        form (1903) provided by the Bank, along with the project's expenditures forecast for the next two
        reportingperiods; and
    (b) Subsequent Withdrawal Applications:         A request for withdrawal of loan funds using the
        appropriate form (1903) provided by the Bank, along with the FMRs for the period ended, the
        Special Account Activity Statement (including Bank statement), and any other applicable
        Summary Statement of Special Account Expenditures, and forecast for next two reporting
        periods.
Under this mechanism, only the reports listed above are submitted to the Bank with the withdrawal
applications. All documents supporting the reported expenditures should be maintained by INCAGRO-
PMUand madethem available for review by Bank supervision missions and external auditors.
As with the case of transaction-based disbursements, direct payments to consultants and service
providers, and reimbursement to the Government for pre-financed Bank's share of project expenditures
will be available to the borrower and set out inthe Disbursement Letter.

RetroactiveFinancing
During negotiations, the borrower requested and the Bank agreed to include $400,000 equivalent for
retroactive financing with respect of payments made for eligible expenditures under categories (1)
through (4) and after March 1, 2005.

ExternalAudit
The auditors have issued unqualified opinions of the project financial statements over the years of its
implementation and, overall, the Project has consistently taken timely and complete corrective action to
address weaknesses identified by the auditors. In addition, the Project has consistently delivered audit
reports when due. The Project therefore is expected to continue to provide the Bank annually with
financial statements for the P M U audited by an independent auditing firm acceptable to the Bank and
underterms and conditions satisfactory to the Bank which will include an opinion on the project financial
statements and a management letter on the internal control structure. Annual audit reports with the
related statements will be submitted to the Bank within six months of the end of the Borrower's fiscal
year. All supporting records would be maintained at the Project site for at least one year after the
completion of the Project. The Bank's audit guidelines will be used by the Borrower in determiningthe
format and content of the annual financial statements and in preparing the audit TORS. The costs of the
Project audits are subject to financing from loan proceeds.

FinancialManagementSupervisionduring implementation
Given the expanded scope of the Project, supervision activities will be expanded to include a visit to
/review of the financial operations of at least one new decentralized unit, in addition to review of the
FMRS, annual review of external audit.




                                                     69

                                Annex 8: ProcurementArrangements

                    PERU: Agricultural ResearchandExtensionAPL PhaseI1
A. General
Procurement for the proposed Project would be carried out in accordance with the World Bank's
"Guidelines: Procurement under IBRD Loans and IDA Credits," dated May 2004; and "Guidelines:
Selection and Employment of Consultants by World Bank Borrowers," dated May 2004, and the
provisions stipulated in the Loan Agreement. The general description of various items under different
expenditure categories is indicated below. For each contract to be financed by the Loan, the different
procurement methods or consultant selection methods, the need for prequalification, estimated costs, prior
review requirements, and time frame are agreed between the Borrower and the Bank Project team in the
Procurement Plan. The Procurement Plan will be updated twice yearly or as requiredto reflect the actual
Project implementationneeds and improvements in institutional capacity.

ProcurementMethods
The procurement methods and the contract values for the use of each procurementhelection method are
presentedin Table A.8.1.
Procurement of Works. Due to very limited requirements identified at the Project design stage, no
major civil works contracts would be financed at Project implementation. Minor works to be procured
under this Project will consist of the rehabilitation of Project offices and network installation of computer
equipment in the Lima Project office, three existing regional offices, and eight new regional offices at
different points in time as Project implementation progresses. Use of International Competitive Bidding
(ICB) and National Competitive Bidding (NCB) procedures for procurement of works i s not expected.
All contracts for works estimated to cost US$250,000 equivalent or less per contract will be procured
with the use of price comparison from at least three price quotes obtained from local qualified contractors
under lump-sum, fixed-price contracts. Total amount of works above is expected not to exceed, in the
aggregate, US$O.11million.
Procurement of Goods. Goods to be financed under the Project will consist of supply of vehicles,
computer, and office equipment for new regional offices in eight different regions to be procured at
different times duringProject implementation. They will be grouped and sized in packages to meet the
needs of each group of regional offices planned to start operations in different years of the Project. Due
to the expectedpackagesizes, there will be no contracts to awardunder ICB procedures.
Vehicles for the Proyecto de Investigacidn y Extensidn Agricdla para la Innovacidn y Competitividad del
Agro Peruano's (INCAGRO's) new Project offices at the regional level will be grouped to form packages
to be procured under NCB procedures. Whenever feasible, they will be grouped in packages estimated to
cost US$50,000 equivalent or more per contract, but less than US$200,000 equivalent.
Procurement of computer equipment will provide for PCs, laptops, servers, laser printers, and software,
while office equipment will include copy machines, air-conditioning, scanners, digital cameras, and office
furniture for start-up of new regional offices. Except for a first-year package of computer equipment to
be procured with the use of NCB procedures, all goods will be procuredwith the use of Price Comparison
(shopping) procedures from quotations obtained from at least three local qualified suppliers. Goods
grouped under packages estimated to cost US$50,000 equivalent or more but less than US$200,000
equivalent per contract, will be procured with the use of NCB procedures. Contracts with an estimated
value of less than US$50,000 equivalent per contract will be purchased through Price Comparison. Total
amount of goods contracts to be financed under this Project i s estimated at US$0.6 million.
Procurementof Non-ConsultingServices (includingtraining-relatedexpenses). The Project would
finance contracts for the provision of services other than consulting services for such training-related
activities as workshops, seminars, consultation meetings, study tours for participating agency staff


                                                      70

working in Project activities, and so forth. Expensesto be financed will consist of travel, subsistence, and
per diems for trainers and trainees, registration fees, and training materials and the logistics for organizing
training events. Total amount of non-consulting services to be financed under the Project would not
exceedUS$1.9 million.
Selection of Consultants.      Contracts for employment of firms and individuals will be procured for
evaluation of subproject profiles and for proposals for several different studies and training, supervision
of subprojects, monitoring and evaluation, project management services, baseline studies for indigenous
groups, project midterm evaluation, impact evaluation, policy-related studies, technological perspective,
development of management information systems, and project financial and procurement audits. Total
cost of consultant contracts to be financed underthe Project are expected not to exceedUS$2.4 million.
Contracts for employment of firms will be procured through use of Quality-Cost Based Selection
(QCBS), Least-Cost Selection (LCS), and Consultants' Qualification Selection (CQS) and Single-Source
Selection (SSS) methods.
In addition to the services noted above, consulting services for employment of individual consultants
under subprojects will be procured by participating communities with the use of Commercial Practices,
acceptable to the Bank, and described in the Project Operational Manual, based on the comparison of
qualifications and criteria such as academic background, experience, knowledge of local conditions and
language, and overall suitability for the assignment. The comparison of qualifications will be carried out
with due consideration of at least three candidates who shall meet minimumrelevant requirements. The
consultants will be selected on the basis of the best qualified among the candidates considered for a
particular assignment.
Short lists of consultants for services estimated to cost less than US$350,000 equivalent per contract may
be comprised entirely of national consultants in accordance with the provisions of paragraph 2.7 of the
Consultant Guidelines..
Operating Costs.       Expenses on account of Project management and monitoring, including office
supplies; utilities; rent; communication expenses, includingthe telephone, the Internet and fax; vehicles;
office  equipment and computer operation and maintenance; transport; travel; subsistence; per diems;
supervision costs; and Project staff salaries would be financed as operating costs and procured using
comparison procedures, whenever possible, and the implementing agency's administrative procedures
which were reviewed and found acceptable to the Bank. Under this category, the Project will finance
dissemination and advertisement services on spot radio, the print media, and television for promotion of
competitive matching grants for execution of subprojects. Non-consulting services would be procured
with the use of either direct contracting or price comparison (shopping) procedures and the use of sample
documents acceptableto the Bank for the latter. Expected total costs would not exceed US$5.3 million.
Subprojects (Matching Grant Fund). The Project would finance demand-driven research and
agricultural extension subprojects proposed by agricultural communities or associations of communities
using matching grants mechanisms. Subprojects would be evaluated and selected in accordance with
specific evaluation criteria to be described in the Project Operational Manual. Subproject maximum size
will not exceed US$lOO,OOO equivalent, of which about 15 percent will be made available by
participating communities as counterpart funds, and 20 percent as government counterpart funds.
Subproject may encompass consultant services, training expenses, small goods, very small works, and
seed capital. Although no procurement of goods above $50,000 equivalent per contract is expected,
Bank prior review would be performed whenever packages of such value or more were feasible.
Procurement of goods and works funded under subprojects would be carried out with the use of
Community Participation procedures following to the extent possible shopping procedures, as described
in the Operations Manual. Total expected amount of grants for the purposes noted above would not
exceedUS$32.6 million.




                                                       71

    B. Assessment of the Agency's Capacityto Implement Procurement
Procurement activities will be carried out by INCAGRO, the Government entity responsible for
implementationof the first Agricultural Researchand Extension Project. At the central level, the Project
Management Unit (PMU) i s staffed by 17 officials, including Project administrative staff and one
Procurement Officer. In addition, as new regional offices are incorporated into Project implementation,
INCAGRO will need to gradually hire additional Project and administrative staff to enable a decentralized
Project implementation approach. Eventually, hiring of staff in regional offices is estimated to reach 43
staffers. Gradual incorporation of staff at these offices i s foreseen to take place within the first 14months
after implementationbegins.
The Operational Manual (OM) will describe both institutional and implementation arrangements; the
organizational structure and functions proposedfor Project implementation and coordination; and detailed
procedures for procurement of goods and works, and selection of consultants for mandatory use of
INCAGRO offices at all levels, and participating communities.               Standard Project documents for
procurement of goods and works, request for proposals from consultant firms, and standard letters of
invitation and forms of agreement for selection and contracting of individual consultants agreed for the
Project will also be included inthe OM.
Capacity assessment exercises of INCAGRO as Project agency to implement procurement actions were
performed by Keisgner D. Alfaro, Senior Procurement Specialist, in April and June 2004.                   The
assessment was largely based on the review of agency past performance, the current organizational
structure for Project implementation, and arrangements and procedures used for management of
procurement and contract administration, including internal procedures for functioning of the Evaluation
Committee and interactionwith the Procurement Officer.
Issues identified as potentially affecting Project implementationinclude: (a) some degree of uncertainty in
respect of the PMU's organization to be adopted for implementation of the Second INCAGRO Project
and the extent to which the organizational and implementation arrangements will remain the same as
currently in place. The latter will be largely contingent on the outcomes of a study contracted out to
specialized consultants that will incorporate a proposal for this agency to operate under a decentralized
implementation approach in approximately 11 regional offices, out of which 8 will be created; (b) poor
performance in procurement by Evaluation Committee members, particularly for the selection and
employment of consultants, due to insufficient knowledge of Bank procurement and selection guidelines
and inadequate and inefficient work arrangements; (c) the lack of an integrated project management
information system for monitoring, control, and reporting purposes that would otherwise enable decision
malung and increaseproject coordination; and (d) the needto reinforce INCAGRO's project filing system
at the central level and provide existing and new regional offices with a similar and coherent system.
Corrective actions agreed for the mitigation of these shortcomings are: (1) submission of a proposal for
implementation of decentralized arrangements to be agreed at loan negotiations or earlier; and (2)
implementation of revised procurement arrangements discussed and agreed during the appraisal mission
that include the participation of a Procurement Officer in the Evaluation Committee for ensuring proper
use of Bank procurement procedures and for provision of increased coordination and expediting; and
provision of Bank procurement training to Project agency staff, including the Committee's members, not
later than the Project launching mission; hiring of a specialized consultant for design and implementation
of a new project filing system at the central and regional levels, and implementation of an integrated
project management information system through the development of a procurement module within the
financial management system and their interconnection through inter-phase software. These initiatives
are all included in the Procurement Improvement Action Plan included in the Agency capacity
Assessment Report and agreed to duringthe appraisal mission.
The overall project risk for procurement is high.

                              Annex 9: Economic and Financial Analysis
                    PERU: Agricultural Researchand ExtensionAPL PhaseI1
Economic andFinancial Analysis
The economic and financial analysis of Phase I1 of the Agricultural Research and Extension (R&E)
Project (APL 11) follows good practices in the World Bank, and i s based on experiences with similar
projects in Latin America and other regions. The analysis i s structured as follows: (a) summary of
general issues for economic analysis of R&E projects; (b) review of returns to past investments in
agricultural R&E projects internationally and in Peru; (c) an estimation of potential internal rate of return
(IRR) for the proposed investment based on experiences with the Project Phase I;and (d) a fiscal analysis
focusing on the potential consequences of the proposed investment of the government budget.
General Issues for Economic Analysis of R&E projects
The Good Practice Note, "Ex-Ante Economic Analysis in AKIS Projects-Methods                  and Guidelines,"
(Horstkotte-Wesseler and others 2000) summarizes issues for economic analysis of World Bank-funded
R&E projects. The Note emphasizesthat the calculation of a single summary measure should not be the
major objective of the economic analysis of R&E because it i s usually subject to a wide range of
assumptions. Rather, the priority should be mainstreaming economic analysis into a process of evaluating
costs and benefits for more efficient resource allocation within the R&E system. Therefore, general
support and capacity building within the national research and extension system (NAIS) should be
undertaken to conduct ongoing economic analysis for effective evaluation, priority setting, and impact
assessment. Since the larger share of resources in R&E projects i s allocated to institutional and human
capacity building, the economic analysis of a whole project i s not very useful, and economic analysis of
individual investments within the project may be more appropriate and warranted. The recommended
model for evaluation i s the economic surplus method (or a simplification thereof). The exact model type
i s determined by the activities to be financed by the project, analytical capacity, and data availability.
Returns to Past Investments for Agricultural R&EProjects
Many ex post economic analyses have shown high rates of return to investments in agricultural R&E.
Alston and others (1999) conducted the most comprehensive review of literature on this topic. The study
reviewed 294 studies (including extension) reporting 1,858 estimates of returns on investments in
Agricultural R&E. After eliminating extremes, the remaining 1,760 estimates indicatedan average return
of 73 percent per year. These results confirm the conventional view that returns on investments in
Agricultural R&E i s relatively high. Returns averaged 88 percent on investments in research alone, 79
percent on extension alone, and 45 percent on R&E combined. The report argues that the lower estimates
for R&E combined might be because the corresponding studies captured more of the total costs of the
technology innovation process. No evidence was found of declining rates of return in recent years, and
little consistent difference among regions. Rates of returns were similar across all research categories
except for naturalresource research, where lower rates of return were mainly due to the longer production
cycle. There is no reason to believe that economic rates of return in Peru would be substantially below
that level. Alarcon (1999) and Ganoza and others (1990) analyzed potential rates of return to investment
in researchand extension in the country, and showed comparable levels of IRR. Fano and others (1999)
conducted a study on expected impacts of 12 technologies developed by the National Agricultural
Researchand ExtensionInstitute (INIEA), and estimated an average net present value of more than US$3
million, and an average rate of return of 24 percent (ranging from 11 percent for quinoa varietal
development to 39 percent for cotton technology development).
Estimate of IRR for Investments inthe Agricultural R&E Project
A share of resources within the Agricultural R&E Project is allocated to institutional development or
capacity building of the NAIS system in Peru. As discussed, economic analysis for the whole Project is

EconomicAnalysisfor Overall Project: An estimate of the IRR for the overall Project was not attempted
for the reasons stated above. However, the calculation of the overall productivity gain required in the
agricultural sector to pay the costs of Project Phase I1i s a useful indicator for the economic justification
of the investment.      The analysis includes the total annual Project costs, including institutional
development, and assumes a time lag for initiation of benefits of four years. It i s projected that the
maximumbenefits will be attained after eight years from the start of Phase 11. The analysis indicates that
a productivity gain of only 0.55 percent of the agricultural gross domestic product (AgGDP) would lead
to an IRR of 12 percent. Compared internationally, the required level is very low. In many countries,
expected productivity gains from agricultural researchof between 0.5 and 1.5 percent per year of AgGDP
have been reported.
FiscalAnalysis
Peru's significant achievements in terms of economic growth, poverty reduction, and improvements in
social indicators up to 1997 have been partially set back lately by ensuing persistent slowdowns in
economic activity, political uncertainty, institutional weaknesses, poor governance and, in particular,
fiscal indiscipline. Since 1999, Peru's fiscal stance has been fragile due to expansive fiscal policies and
other unfavorable domestic and external developments. As a result, the overall fiscal deficit significantly
increased from 0.4 percent of GDP in 1997 to 3.2 percent of GDP in 2000, only to be slightly reduced to
about 2.5 percent of GDP in 2001 (World Bank 2003). The success of the envisioned economic reform
programin Agricultural R&E will depend to a great extent on how quickly the current administrationwill
be able to restore fiscal discipline, reorient the budget toward pro-poor expenditure, and improve the
efficiency of public expenditure.



                                                      77

In this context of scarce financial public resources and increased focus on pro-poor expenditures,
Agricultural R&D has the potential to play an important role. The preliminary results of the economic
and financial analysis indicate that the investments in R&E would be economically and financially
justified. These results are supportedby highreturns on past investments inPeru and other countries, and
the conventional view and empirical evidence that investments in agricultural research have major
positive impacts on poverty reduction (Fan, Zhang, and Zhang 2000; Fan, Hazell, and Thorat 1999;
Thirtle and others 2003). Due to the economic importance and potential of agricultural production in
Peru, and the need of the agricultural sector to become more competitive to fully exploit the opportunities
provided through the prospect of new trade agreements with the UnitedStates, the European Union (EU),
and MERCOSUR, a significant increase in public funding for agricultural research and extension is
essential. The Agricultural R&E Project i s an important step inthis direction. However, due to increased
emphasis on fiscal discipline, it i s important to estimate the fiscal impacts of the proposed investment.
Base Analysis: The fiscal analysis aims at assessing the likelihood that sustainable local sources of funds
will be available at the end of the Project to take over from funds provided under the Project. The current
public expenditures for R&E are compared with the projected demands on public expenditures for
sustaining Project activities after the completion of the Project Phase I1in 2009. Table A.9.3 illustrates
the scenario where the planned cost recovery and co-financing mechanisms are not in place after the end
of the Project, which in turn would result in an increase of public expenditures for agricultural R&E in
real terms of 44 percent annually. This increasewould correspond to an increased expenditure of US$9.8
million per year, which is less than 0.1 percent of the total government budget in 2002, and 3.5 percent of
the budget for the agricultural sector in 2002.

Co-Financing and Cost Recovery: The fiscal impacts of the planned investments into the national R&E
system depend significantly on implemented co-financing and cost recovery mechanisms. The analysis
above i s conservative by assuming that total investment costs of the competitive fund have to be covered
by public expenditures after the Project i s phased out (Phase I1of APL, in 2009). In Phase 11, about 20
percent of these costs have to be co-financed by the producers and executing agencies. Assuming that
about the same amount would be providedby these agencies after project completion, public expenditures
for researchand extension have to increaseby only about US$8 million annually, or 37 percent (see Table
A.9.4).    The incremental public expenditures as a percentage of the total government budget for the
agricultural sector would decrease from 3.5 percent to 3.0 percent. Inaddition, the researchinstitutes that
would function as training and information centers for extension service providers are expected to move
to a charge-back system under which they would recover the costs partially for the training courses.
Moreover, it is planned that the producers would be increasingly charged for receiving advice from the
extension agencies. Preliminaryanalysis suggests that use of levies would be financially feasible for most
commodities (Alarcon 1999). These measures would further decrease the amount to be provided by the
government.

                                       Annex 10: Safeguard Policy Issues

                     PERU: Agricultural ResearchandExtensionAPL PhaseI1
Safeguard Category: S2 (Limited impact)

                                    Annex 10.16: IndigenousPeoplesActionPlan

    1. StrategicContextandRationale:theEquityPrinciple
The Project's target population i s defined by the minimum levels of productive efficiency and market
articulation required to expect effective and beneficial participation by small producers inthe market for
innovation services and the innovation system in general. Given this framework, during Phase Iof the
Project, studies aimed at better identifying the characteristics of the target population; and establishing
whether the target population might contain segments participating under conditions of inequity in the
activities developed by the Project to promote access to the service market were carried out. The
emphasis laid on the equity principle is related to two fundamental aspects: (a) it i s important to
understand equity as the manner in which the overall organization of a society-not              only of its poor or of
any particular group--enables or impedes access to tangible and intangible resources to develop its
capacities; (b) the implications of applying this principle to the way a Government (and, therefore, the
Project) act regarding its responsibility to increase opportunities for its citizens, especially those in the
most vulnerable segments".
The initialstudies carried out" revealed that producers inpeasantcommunities (indigenous populations in
the high Andes) were engaged in a variety of agribusiness activities as a way to increase their income.
Furthermore, to improve their market articulation they had an unsatisfieddemand which was importantly
associatedto access to specialist professional services enabling them to improve both the productivity and
product quality of their businesses and the management of their marketing channels, negotiation power,
their share of the net marginof commercialization, andprofitability. Inaddition, it was possible to verify
the limitations faced by this type of producers in accessing the said specialized service market, which
presentedextremely low levels of development inthe study area (Huancavelica),as well as in other areas
with similar characteristics. This was due not only to limitations and poor dynamism of markets of goods
and services, poor socioeconomic infrastructure, absence of information at all levels, but also to poor
institutional development of supply of services-a           state of affairs that aggravated the uncertainty and
ignorance of producers as well as of the potential providers of services. Other socioeconomic limitations
related to the cultural, social and economic characteristics of this type of indigenous producers-
indigenous mother tongue, phenotypic features, geographical origin, clothes, traditional know-how
applied to the management of their biodiversity resources, low holdings of such private assets as formal
education, low access to public goods and services, etc.) determined exclusion processes that created the
need to have a differentiated strategy to promote the fair participation by these producers in the market of
services and in the NAIS. Finally, these initial studies warned that women producers pertaining to these
indigenous populations had even more limited access to the service market as demanders of services due
to existing gender inequities'*. In responseto these initial findings, between August 2004 and June 2004,
the ATF implemented a pilot fundingwindow designated "Huchuy Ayni", in the Huancavelica region for
the co-financing of capacity building and technical assistancefor the improvement of agribusinessesrun
by producers in peasant communities. The lessons learned from, and the findings of, this pilot are
presentedinthe next section.
