For Phase 11, the Project decided to continue implementing the Indigenous Peoples Action Plan that had
been prepared as a part of Phase          II3. To that end, improvements were introduced in the intervention
strategy for this segment of the target population, including specific strategies for work with indigenous
peoples in the high Andes and the Amazon region. The Plan will make it possible to foster equitable
access by producers in indigenous communities to the innovation services market as a way to improve
their agribusinesses and attend to the demand for better market articulation. Working with this population
group into an action strategy i s consistent with the national policies of promotion of segments of the
populationthat have been or are affected by exclusion and discrimination processes.In fact, documents of
such major national policy (including agricultural policy) relevance as Peru's National Agreement,
explicitly state this ~bjective'~:Of the various groups facing the situations of discrimination and social
inequity emphasized by the Agreement, the ones most relevant to the Project's intervention strategy are
"women" and "the members of ethnic communities" (indigenous populations among them), who have a
large presenceinrural areas ingeneral and inthe agricultural sector inparticular.
As regards the Agricultural Policy in particular, the document prepared by MINAG's Agricultural
Planning Office in 2004, entitled "Bases para una Poli'tica de Estado en la Agricultura del Peru"',
specifically states that ... the role and the logic of public intervention should go beyond the mere
`administration' of the market" and ... public rationality should not be associated only to the idea of
                            "

                                            "
competitiveness and efficiency", but also to the constant search for equity. In this sense, it points out that
"the State should bear a responsibility for improving the quality of life and opportunities of its citizens,
especially those in the most vulnerable segments, such as the rural poor. This strategic objective is
associated to the need to articulate agricultural producers in poor and extremely poor areas ... to the
markets ..."     15.  For its part, the document entitled Lineamientos de la Poli'tica Agraria del Perh
highlightsthe importance of supporting "low-income agricultural producers organized and presenting a
market articulation potential ...". The need i s stressed to "provide small producers in traditional and
subsistence agriculture ... with equitable and sustained market articulation". It also states that
"competitiveness and equity mechanisms need to be developed in order for traditional and subsistence
agriculture groups to achieve better market integration."'6.
The indigenous peoples, who have a significant presence in Peru's rural areas and agricultural sector, are
characterized by higher levels of poverty than the non-indigenous segment of the population. In fact,
according to a recent study describing the situation of Peru's indigenous population based on the
ENAHO 2001 survey, it i s estimated that, whilst 46,8% of the total population i s below the poverty
threshold, in the indigenous segment the poverty incidence i s as high as 63.8% (in the non-indigenous
segment of the population, the poverty incidence stands at 42.2%). One half of the indigenous people
below the poverty threshold are in a situation of extreme p~verty'~.              This is the rationale of the Project's
intervention strategy which emphasizes actions specifically focused on the indigenous segment of the
population within the context of its general policy of promoting equitable access to the innovation
services market in order to improve agribusiness competitiveness.
As a part of the preparation of Phase I1 Project and the present Action Plan, a study was carried out for
the purpose of updatingthe Plan that had been used for Phase I,             which had placed special emphasis on the
proposed guidelines for work with Amazonian indigenous peoples". This study was complemented by

one on the design of the instruments to be implemented to achieve the objectives proposed in said Action
Plan. This second study has been entitled "Design of aFundingWindow for the Co-financing of Capacity
Building and Technical Assistance Subprojects for the Improvement of Agribusinesses Led by
Amazonian Indigenous Peoples". The study will make it possible to define the guidelines for the funding
windows to be implemented for this type of producers, as well as to help improve the design of the
competitive fund for Andean indigenous peoples. Moreover, the inputs obtained as a result of the
implementation of Phase I,as well as those derived from studies and field work carried out within the
framework of the preparation of Phase 1119,will provide valuable input for a general strategy for action
with Andean and Amazonian indigenous peoples, to be made a part of this Action Plan, as well as the
instruments best suitedfor the achievement of the expected results. The general guidelines of the Action
Plan and its implementationtools are described inthe following sections. Finally, it shouldbe mentioned
that, although high Andes and Amazonian indigenous populations tend to be concentrated in the poorest
segments of the rural area, they also present more dynamic segments in terms of technology and market
insertion. The explicitly differentiated work to be performed with these groups during Phase I1will
amount to an effort aimed at identifying their contribution to innovation and technological development
as much as at promoting equitable participation in the service market by these segments of the target
population.
    2. Indigenouspeoples'demandfor innovationservicesasrevealedbythe"HzxhuyAyn?' pilot

Between August 2004 and June 2004, as a part of Phase Iof the Project, the ATF implemented a pilot
funding window in the Huancavelica region for the co-financing of capacity building and technical
assistance for the improvement of agribusinesses run by producers in peasant communities. The peasant
communities were taken as the organizational referent of Andean indigenous populations present in the
area. The funding window was given the Quechua name of "Huchuy Ayni" (small mutual help). Its
principal objective was to empower and strengthen producers' capacities for managingtheir organizations
and their agribusinesses, as well as their service contracting capacity. The underlying aim was to use these
services to improve the agribusinessesdeveloped by these indigenous producers, and to foster attention to
their newly-founddemandfor better market articulation.
As a result of the implementation of the Huchuy Ayni funding window, resources were awarded to a total
of 16 subprojects. The funding window covered 5 out of the 7 provinces in the Huancavelica region
(Acobamba, Castrovirreyna, Huancavelica,Angaraes and Huaytard). It elicited much interest, as shown
by the fact that a very significant number of communities submitted project profiles. Their number (50
peasant communities) represented 22% of the total number of communities in the 5 provinces (427).
Information dissemination, promotion and training could not be brought to all communities. However, in
nearly 100% of those contacted the response was positive, which indicates that there remains untapped
demand in this segment of the population. In the five province covered by the call for proposals, the
population registered in the communities"               numbered 55 thousand families.           Of these, 1,169 were
included in the applications submitted through the project profiles presented. Ultimately, 320 families
representing 0.6% of the total received funds.
Some of the lessonslearnedfrom the Huchuy Ayni experience follow:
a) The aim of improving market articulation through a strategy designed to improve competitiveness via
     specialized innovation services i s indeed viable for producers with these characteristics, although it
     also requires the implementation of strategies responding to their peculiarities, and the limitations
     inherent to their ethnicity (indigenous mother tongue, geographical origin, discrimination, dearth of
     rightsof access to, anduse of, resources, etc.);


l9 In2004, the ATF team carried out extensive field work in the departments of Puno and Loreto (Reserva Pacaya Samiria), in
order to complement the field work envisaged in the studies for the design of the funding windows for Amazonian indigenous
peoplesand women's organizations.
2o No more than 70% of the families registeredlive inthe community.


                                                             82

    In areas, such as this, featuring a predominance of indigenous population, the Project can benefit and
    achieve greater impact by introducing the cultural dimension, and incorporating these peoples'
    ancestral and traditional practices, uses and customs;
    These economies hold significant agribusinesses potential associated to a great variety of products,
    many of which linkedto the sustainabledevelopment of biodiversity;
     There i s little presence of public institutions and private operators able and willing to support
    producers' activities with a sustainable market approach. Most of those present show serious
    deficiencies in the use of the instruments available for designing and managing agribusiness projects,
    which makes it all the more difficult for them to meet producers' demands. Most institutions still
    follow a food security-based philosophy combined with welfare-oriented practices. Nevertheless,
    there exist a group of service providers interested in acquiring greater strength in order to make their
    undertakings more businesslike by deepening their reconversion process and acquiring the capacities
    that may enable them to meet producers' demands associated to the need to improve their
    agribusinessesby offering them quality services at market prices;
    The Project had to invest considerable effort and resources in order to provide producers and their
    partners with the professional consultancy services necessary on an ongoing basis to enable them to
    meet the established technical and institutional accreditation requirements and compete for the funds.
    Given that an outlay of funds of this size i s unlikely to be repeated, it is necessary to implement
    strategiescomplementary to the competitive fundas such;
    There exist new producer organizations-many          of them consisting of young producers-with       a
    strong interest in developing successful rural agribusinesses. Their potential multiplier effect should
    not be ignored;
    On the other hand, there also exist producer organizations that still display distorted forms of
    behavior, where passivity and opportunism hold sway, with little initiative to lead changes in their
    own communities.         This behavior, unfortunately, is reinforced by some nongovernmental
    organizations (NGOs) and public organizations managing relations in the zone with an authoritarian,
    vertical, political patronage-basedstyle;
    The empowerment potential of the funding window mechanism was made evident as producer
    organizations were able to choose their partners to work with in drawing up and implementing their
    subprojects, to contract service providers, and in general to manage the money for implementingtheir
    subprojects. This will allow beneficiary producers to develop their own agribusiness initiatives by
    solving problems and implementing improvements of their choice without having to establish
    relations of dependence on or commitment vis-&vis local institutions that often impose restrictive
    conditions on their decision-making capacity, in addition to distorting their true demands for better
    market articulation;
    The Huchuy Ayni experiment has made evident thatthose producers in peasant communities are
    willing and able to pay in order to co-finance the costs of capacity building and technical assistance
    when they help them with production and market articulation problems as identified by them. The
    diverse strategiesto make cash co-financing possible should be considered at on a case-by-case basis
    to allow for greater flexibility and successful implementation of the objectives; and
    Finally, the role of local public actors, regional governments, and the competent authority of the
    Ministry of Agriculture (MOA) in the region i s critical to the successful implementation of a funding
    window, for without their support in terms of information dissemination, promotion and training,
    negotiationof the winning proposals would be impossible.
As it will be seen inthe following sections, these conclusions were inputs for the present Action Planwith
Indigenous Peoples, and were used in the development of the specific guidelines for the high Andes
indigenous populations inparticular.




                                                      83

     3. InterventionStrategy ofthe strengtheningthe Marketfor InnovationServices Componentfor Work
         withAndeanandAmazonianIndigenousPeoples
The intervention strategy of the Project for the promotion of the equitable participation by Andean and
Amazonian indigenous peoples in the service market for services and the innovation system i s based on
the development of two levels of action:
  Indirect Project intervention. Aimed at contributing to the improvement of issues relatedto the cultural
  and socioeconomic milieu of the indigenous peoples, which are key for improving the Project's chances
  of success: land use regulations (land titling, etc), access to and sustainable use of resources, ethnic
  discrimination, awareness of duties and rights, among others, and
  Direct Project intervention. Aimed at improving equitable participation by this type of producers inthe
  service market-mainly      as clients, but also as providers-for the strengthening of their agribusinesses.
At the first level, the role of third party public or private stakeholders will be critical, given that the
Project's only role will be that of initiative facilitator and promoter. At the direct intervention level, the
Project will have the lead. This strategy will require the gradual intervention of the Project inthe various
areas where these population segments live, initially through pilot programs. The areas of intervention
will be selected according to criteria designed to increase the chances of success. Later, instruments will
be implemented for more generalized intervention. Both levels of action are envisaged in the Indigenous
Peoples Action Plan. The level of direct action will be developed mainly through the implementation of a
competitive fund to co-finance the capacity building and technical assistance subprojects for improved
agribusiness under the managementof this category of producers.
Finally, it i s important to mention that the treatment of population groups excluded from the service
market, as i s the case with Andean and Amazonian indigenous peoples, i s addressedby all three Projects
of Phase I1of the Program. The Strengthening the Service Market essentially consists of implementing
the Action Plan designed for the work to be performed with this segment of the target population. This
involves not only organizing the calls for proposals and performing whatever support activities are
required, but also attending to issues related to the formalization of producer organizations and dealing
with-albeit    indirectly-with   other issues relating to the cultural and socioeconomic milieu in which the
indigenous peoples live. The Strengthening Strategic Research and Development Capacities component
will help develop a supply of services tailored to the specific needs of these population segments. Finally,
the Policies, Information and Innovation Services Quality component will generate ongoing information
about the supply and demand of services, in addition to providing quality incentives for those services in
the form of awards.
     4. MainGuidelinesoftheActionPlanwithIndigenousPeoples
4.1 Main Objective and Specific Objectives of the Action Plan
The central objective of the Action Plan with Indigenous Peoples is to promote equitable participation by
these producers in the service market and the innovation system as a way to improve their agribusinesses
while respecting their socioeconomic and cultural peculiarities, as well as helping reduce the causes of
their social and ethnic exclusion. The Action Plan's three specific objectives-two       on the side of service
demand, one on the side of service supply-are:        (a) Empower and strengthen the capacities of indigenous
producers as customers requiring services enabling them to improve their agribusinesses; (b) Strengthen
the organizational and entrepreneurial capacities of indigenous producers; and (c) Promote a supply of
services appropriate for working with indigenous producers, including indigenous producer organization
leaders as suppliers of services.
By empowering and strengthening indigenous producers as demanders of specialized innovation services,
it i s hoped to increase their organizational and entrepreneurial capacities as a way to improve their
agribusinesses, their income, and, ultimately, their well being. For this to be possible, it i s necessary to
foster the supply of quality services using methodologies appropriate for this target population, ensuring


                                                         84

that technologies are adopted, information i s handled and management i s improved as required by the
producers as beneficiaries. Indigenous producer leaders may assume the role of quality service suppliers
themselves, provided they have the necessary capacities. What follows i s a brief presentation of the
guidelines of the Action Plan with Indigenous Peoples. The work to be done with indigenous populations
in the high Andes (mostly located in the central and southern highlands (sierra), and comprised of
Quechua and Aymara ethnic groups), and the work to be done with Amazonian indigenous populations
(mostly located in Amazonian Peru, zones of high and low forests where there i s a great diversity of
ethnic groups, including Ashaninka, Bora, Cocama,Huitoto, Jibaro, Ocaina, Omagua, Quichua, Yagua,
and Yanesha).
4.2 IndigenousPeoples inthe HighAndes
Based on the experience of the Huchuy Ayni pilot carried out with the communities of the Huancavelica
region, the following issues will be addressedby the Action Plan with indigenous populations in the high
Andes:
    Duringthe first year, and gradually duringthe following years, the Project will expand the scope of
    its calls for proposals for peasant communities beyond the Huancavelica region, to include other
    areas where communal organization predominates and where the prevailing conditions allow greater
    possibilities of success in improving agribusinesses through the provision of services, in terms of
    stronger producer organizations, and in relation to the supply of services. In these areas competitive
    funding will be used to co-finance capacity building and technical assistance for agribusiness
    improvement subprojects conducted by producer organizations inpeasant communities;
    In areas in which promising experiences of agribusinesses are being developed and expressing the
    existing demand, communities will be selected that have identified opportunities for businesses to
    arise from the sustainable local natural resources. Also, areas will be selected in which livestock
    (cattle) activity i s most important for generating income. This choice i s justified by the greater
    relative importance of the market for private professional services meeting the demand from livestock
    breedersinpeasantcommunities;
    The selected areas must have access to a first-order (primary) market, to ensure greater agribusiness
    viability. Also, those areas where there is a significant presence of local capacities for agribusiness
    managementwill be preferred;
    The potential for future economic development will be targeted by only admitting for consideration
    proposals envisaging the sustainable development of local biodiversity by producing, processing, and
    marketing specific natural resources. Proposals for ecotourism or other rural agribusinesses will also
    be admitted for considerations that combine the sustainable resource development with traditional
    practices of the indigenous peoples, so that their identity i s also strengthened;
    The level of institutional development of the indigenous peoples will be taken into account in the
    selection of beneficiaries, by giving preference to zones that have well developed communal
    organizations. These organizations must be important allies of the Project, involved from the start by
    disseminating information on the Project's objectives, planning local actions, organizing events and
    capacity building activities, disseminating information to support their members' entrepreneurial
    activities, and providing social control to ensure the successful implementation of the subprojects to
    be co-financed;
    Throughout the implementation of the competitive fund for peasant communities, the Project will
    develop actions to help mitigate critical negative factors and ensure the success of agribusinesses led
    by communal producers. Special efforts will be made to build local capacities for the formulation,
    implementation, and management of business plans, both for service providers and among leaders of
    producer organizations. To that end, special care will be taken to improve capacities in the handling
    of such tools as businessplans, balanced score cards, logical frameworks and budgets; and
    Actions will be developed to empower producers as demanders of services and implementers of the
    subprojects to be co-financed by INCAGRO. The structure of the strategic partnership implemented



                                                     85

    for the Huchuy Ayni pilot will remain in place, with the producer organization as its core, supported
    by an individualor institutionpartnering with it in formulating and implementing the subproject.
4.3 Amazonian IndigenousPeoples
The study prepared to develop the guidelines for action with Amazonian indigenous peoples (Smith2004)
proposes as a general strategy that, during an initial period of at least two years and based on work done
in specific areas, a development model be established for entrepreneurial activities among Amazon
indigenous populations. The model will promote the basic conditions and know-how required to improve
competitiveness and achieve a good market position. To that end, proper attention will be paid to such
actions as may contribute to land use regulation, as well as to the provision of training and consultancy
services supporting the sustainable development of natural resources within the framework of viable
business management. As to the instrument to be used to achieve the objectives of the Action Plan, the
Smith study recommends that a program be implemented to support agribusiness proposals through
competitive fundinginthe form of a fundingwindow designed in accordance with the cultural framework
of the given people, and ensuring equal opportunities of access. Emphasis i s placed on the crucial
importance of contacting the indigenous peoples to ensure their active participation in designing the
implementation instrument, and later in its implementation; and of enlisting the knowledgeable advice of
people familiar with the people and the communities in question. The details about the instrument
designed for Amazonian (and Andean) indigenous peoples are set forth in the next section of this
document. The operational plan will consider the following:
    During an initial period of at least two years, the Project will limit its attention to a few areas
    inhabited by indigenous communities inthe Amazon where prevailing conditions offer better chances
    of success in improving agribusinesses through services. Duringthis period, the Project can trial and
    consolidate various methods for supporting the indigenous economy;
    Duringthe first two years of Phase 11,four pilot cases will be selected-two       pilots each in the high
    forest and low forest zones. This will make it possible to experiment with very different ecosystems
    and different natural resources;
    In principle, each pilot case must have access to a first-order (primary) market, to facilitate business.
    This would limit the selection to zones of easy accessibility to Zquitos, Pucallpa, Puerto Maldonado,
    Leticia-Tabatinga, Tarupoto,and to the roadnetwork of the CentralForest;
    Only proposals envisaging the sustainable development of natural resources will be admitted for
    consideration. This includes areas where the existence or proposed creation of communal forest
    reserves or the titling of extensive forest areas open possibilities of future economic development
    based on the sustainable development of such resources in the form of extraction, processing and
    marketing, or ecotourism;
   The level of institutional development of the indigenous peoples will be taken into account in the
    selection of beneficiaries, by giving preference to zones that have better developed communal and
    inter-communal organizations. These organizations must be important allies of the Project, involved
    from the start by disseminating information on the Project's objectives, planning local actions,
    organizing events and capacity building activities, disseminating information to support their
    members' entrepreneurial activities, and providing social control to ensure the successful
    implementationof the subprojects to be co-financed;
    The existence of public or private institutions working in the same area andor on similar issues will
    also be taken in to account given their potential as strategic partners of the Project. The institutions
    that work directly with Amazonian indigenous peoples inPeru are few. Of those few, very few indeed
    focus their work on economic development, production alternatives, and micro-businesses for
    agribusiness development. The institutional offer in the areas selected for implementing the pilots
    will be evaluated with a view to explore the possibilities of establishing strategic partnerships; and
    Duringthe first year at least, the Project will develop actions to help mitigate critical negative factors
    and ensure the success of the intervention. These actions will include land use regulation (land titling,




                                                     86

    etc.) the formulation and implementation of management plans, and the dissemination of knowledge
    on market conditions andhow to prepareabusiness plan.

   7. FinancingtheAndeanandAmazonianIndigenousPeoplesActionPlan

Table A.10.1.A.3 shows how the Andean and Amazonian Indigenous Peoples Action Plan will be financed.
The main sources of financing are the World Bank, Matching Funds from the Peruvian Government, and
Strategic Partnerships (mainly producers receiving the services as beneficiaries). The funds from the first
two sources are equivalent to the resources of the Project for Strengthening the Service Market (ATF)
which i s to implement the Action Plan. The resources that will be contributed by the partnerships are
basically the co-financing of the subprojects to be executed by the producers receiving the services as
beneficiaries.

                      Annex 10.l.B: RuralWomen's OrganizationsActionPlan
1.Strategic ContextandRationale:
The emphasis laid on the equity principle i s related to two fundamental aspects of equity. On one hand, it
i s important to understand equity as the manner in which the overall organization of a society-not               only
of its poor or of any particular group-nables        or impedes access to tangible and intangible resources to
develop its capacities. On the other, the implications of applying this principle to the way a Government
(and, therefore, the Project) acts regarding its responsibility to increase opportunities for its citizens,
especially those in the most vulnerable segments           'l. The initial studies carried outz2revealed that
organized groups of rural women producers were engaged in agribusiness activities involving growing
and breeding a wide variety of crops and livestock, and included production, processing and marketing
activities. These producer women had an unsatisfied demand to improve their market articulation as a
way to increase their income. The strategy for improving that articulation was significantly associated to
access to specialist professional services enabling them to improve both the productivity and product
quality of their businessesand the managementof their marketing channels, their negotiation power, their
share of the net marketing margin [net margin of commercialization] and their profitability.
However, in those areas where women producers engage in production and other business activities, the
service market evinces all the limitations identifiedfor the rural milieu in general. Those limitations are
even more serious in those areas where indigenous peoples live (the initial studies were focused on high
Andean locations with a predominantly indigenous population, such as the Huancavelica region). Market
development in this area is extremely low because of the poor dynamism of the goods and services
markets, the poor social and economic infrastructure, the lack of information at all levels and the low
institutional development of the service supply-a          state of affairs that further aggravates the state of
general uncertainty and lack of knowledge prevailing among producers as much as potential services
providers. In addition to all that, women producers were found to have only limited participation as
demanders of services. This was associatedto exclusion processes brought about by the cultural, social
and economic characteristics of women in that milieu as much as by gender inequities proper. In general
terms, rural women producers are a very vulnerable segment of the population because they suffer not
only gender discrimination but also ethnic discrimination as members of indigenous Andean or

*'MINAG,   Oficina General de Planificaci6nAgraria (OGPA), Bases para una Polftica de Estado en la Agricultura del Per&,
2004, p. 16
22 De La Torre, 2003; Agreda y Mendieta-Documento Intemo,2004


                                                         90

Amazonian ethnic groups.23.On the side of service supply, women's limited participation as service
providers was found along with the absence of a gender approach to service provision in general. Hence
the ,need to implement a differentiated strategy to promote equitable participation by these women
producers inthe service market and the innovation systemi s required.
Inresponseto thesefindings, theATFdecidedto prepareaRuralWomen's Organizations ActionPlanto
be implemented during Phase 11. This Plan will make it possible to adequately promote women
producers' participation in the innovation service market from a gender equity perspective, as a means to
improve women producers' agribusinesses and to attend to this newly found demand for better market
articulation. Working with this population group in an action strategy i s consistent with the national
policies of promotion of segments of the population that have been or are affected by exclusion and
discrimination processes. In fact, documents of such major national policy (including agricultural policy)
relevance as Peru's National Agreement, explicitly state this objective24 "Eleven - National Policy:
Promoting Equality of Opportunitieswithout Discrimination.
Of the various groups facing the situations of discrimination and social inequity emphasized by the
Agreement, the ones most relevant to the Project's intervention strategy are "women'' and "the members
of ethnic communities" (indigenous populations among them), who have a large presence inrural areas in
general and in the agricultural sector in particular. As regards Agricultural Policy in particular, the

de Estado en la Agriculturu del Peru'", specifically states that ... the role and the logic of public
document prepared by MINAG's Agricultural Planning Office in 2004, entitled "Basespara una Politica

intervention should go beyond the mere `administration' of the market" and ". .. public rationality should
                                                                                    "


not be associated only to the idea of competitiveness and efficiency", but also to the constant search for
equity. Inthis sense, it points out that "the State should bear a responsibility for improvingthe quality of
life and opportunities of its citizens, especially those in the most vulnerable segments, such as the rural
poor. This strategic objective is associated to the need to articulate agricultural producers in poor and
extremely poor areas ... to the markets ..."25. As mentioned before, the highest level of poverty is found
in the rural areas in general and among the indigenous population in particular26 and this is even most
critical when the specific situation of women i s analyzed, in particular those who are not family heads.27.
This means that among the target population of the Project, there is a higher level of vulnerability among
the women working in productive activities. This is the rationale of the Project's intervention strategy
which emphasizes actions specifically focused on rural women producers within the context of its general
policy of promoting equitable access to the innovation services market in order to improve agribusiness
competitiveness.
As a partof the preparation of PhaseI1del Project andthe presentAction Plan, a study was carried out for
the purpose of drawing up the main guidelines of the latter, as well as a study on the design of the
instruments to be implemented to achieve the objectives proposed in saidAction Plan. This second study
has been entitled"Design of a FundingWindow for the Co-financing of Capacity Buildingand Technical
Assistance Subprojects for the Improvement of Agribusinesses Led by Organizations of Rural Women".
Also taken into account were the inputs obtained as a result of the implementation of Phase I,as well as

as much as at promoting equitable participationinthe service market by rural women.

2. The ChallengesFacedby RuralWorkingWomen
A study carried out to prepare the guidelines for the Rural Women's Organizations Action Plan made the
following findings inrelation to the condition of women producers::29:
     Rural women perform very important roles infamilybuilding, inincome generation and in communal
     life. There is, however, a lack of perception of these roles by most people in the community-not
     least the women themselves-which          hampers human and economic growth inthe rural milieu. On the
     other hand, women do not live alone. They live in a society with culturally and historically
     established relationship systems that are determinant in every sphere of activity. Our analysis must
     therefore have a comprehensive approach and a gender perspective.
     The limitations and opportunities which women must face for their own development are generated
     by the peculiar and complex relationships they establish with respect to men, which vary on a case by
     case basis according to specific factors, such as the geographic location, the production system and
     the culture. Itis necessaryto consider social differences andproceedaccording to context.
     Any programs, projects or activities addressing women must also consider a strategy for husbands
     and families. Their cooperation i s a necessaryfactor of equitable family development.
     Young women and men and the immensely valuable human capital they represent must receive
     adequate attention. Young women in particular show a sense of responsibility with regard to the tasks
     assumed or the commitments made that makes their active involvement in future activities
     particularly usefuland necessary.
     Poor communities, who are the majority in Peru, are going through a process of deterioration in their
     gender, generational and communal relations. The weakening of social relations within these
     communities further deteriorates the quality of life for community members and hampers their
     projection into the future. No community aspiring to develop can do without the constructive
     contribution of the majority of its members. On the contrary, it must make the most of each member's
     specific skills and capacities and harness them for the common good of the community. To have each
     age group develop a vision and mission for the future would contribute greatly to its development.
     If implementedwithout a duly differentiatedapproach, credit and grant programs will usually widen
     the gap already separating the social groups in a community, because those who are living in better
     conditions take more and better advantage of the support received. This has been seen even in the
     poorest communities. It i s necessary to have those programs implemented equitably and in a
     differentiatedway to avoid risks or power conflicts within the community.
     By focusing their intervention on specific social groups, development support programs can put the
     specific virtues, capacities and skills of men, women, older people, young people and children to
     work for the growth of their community.
     To enhancethe sustainability chances of an investment such as the one the ATF plans to make by co-
     financing services to improve agribusinesses managed by rural women's organizations, it i s
     necessary, in parallel to the competitive allocation of resources, to develop a capacities building

** In2004, the ATF team carriedout extensive field work in the departments of Puno and Loreto (Reserva Pacaya Samiria), in
order to complement the field work envisaged in the studies for the design of the funding windows for Amazonian indigenous
Ytoples andwomen's organizations.
  "Plan de Acci6n conWomen's organizations Rurales". ConsultancyReportfor INCAGRO, Amelia Fort, May 2004


                                                             92

    program which should include gender aspects and credit culture for men and women.
    Given that gender equality is still a goal to be achieved, actions must be promoted that tend to build
    objective equity, thus eliminating the vast gap between theoretical equality under the law and the
    existing very real inequality. Awareness raising regarding access to credit, training and resources
    control must go hand ina hand with a strategy to fight self-exclusion.
    Whatever approach i s chosen for the intervention, it must take into account a holistic view of
    women's lives which encompasses their three main three roles-in         the family, at work (production)
    and within the community. Even though ATF's envisaged intervention only addresses one of these
    roles-production-the     interventionstrategy must take the other two roles into account.
    Women are used to repeat, from one generation to the next, stereotypes and myths that hamper their
    progress and participation in the production systems. A joint review of this challenge would help
    create the appropriate conditions for thinkingabout and identifying the main issues involved.
    Women do not favor their daughters' involvement in the organizations-especially the federations-
    because they hope they will have a different future, free from needs that have to be met through an
    organization. This idea helps neither the emergence of a new leadership nor the dissemination of
    knowledge among girls. In each project prepared on the basis of an organization, it i s necessary to
    include training activities for girls in order to create the conditions for democratic and independent
    leadership.
3. InterventionStrategy of the Strengthening the Market for InnovationServices Component for
RuralWomen's Organizations
The intervention strategy of the Project for the promotion of the equitable participation by rural women
producers in the service market for services and the innovation system i s based on the development of
two levels of action as described in section 10.A.l. At the first level, the role of third party public or
private stakeholders will be critical, given that the Project's essentially only role will be that of facilitator
and promoter. At the direct intervention level, the Project will have the lead. This strategy will require
the gradual intervention of the Project in the various areas where such organized groups of women
producers live, initially through pilot programs. The areas of intervention will be selected according to
criteria designed to increase the chances of success. Later, instruments will be implemented for more
generalized intervention. Both levels of action are envisaged in the Rural Women Organizations Action
Plan. The level of direct action will be developed mainly through the implementation of a competitive
fund to co-finance the capacity building and technical assistance subprojects for improved agribusiness
under the management of this category of women producers. Finally, it i s important to mention that the
treatment of population groups excluded from the service market, as i s the case with rural women
producers, i s addressedby all three Projects of the INCAGRO Program usinga three-pronged approach.
The Project for Strengthening the Service Market essentially consists of implementing the Action Plan
designed for the work to be performed with this segment of the target population. This involves not only
organizing the calls for proposals and performing whatever support activities are required, but also
attending to issues relatedto the formalization of women producer organizations and dealing with-albeit
indirectly-with   other issues relating to the cultural and socioeconomic milieu in which the women
producers live. The Project for Strengthening Strategic Research and Development Capacities will help
develop a supply of services tailored to the specific needs of this population segment. Finally, the Project
for Policies, Information and Innovation Services Quality will generate information on ongoing basis
about the supply and demand of services, in addition to providing quality incentives for those services in
the form of awards.
4. MainGuidelines of the RuralWomen's OrganizationsAction Plan
4.1 CentralObjective andSpecificObjectivesof the ActionPlan
The central objective of the Action Plan with Rural Women's Organizations i s to promote equitable
participation by these women producers in the service market and the innovation system as a way to
improve their agribusinesses. Equal opportunities for women producers to participate in the service


                                                      93

  market will be promoted also as a way to help reduce the causes of their social and ethnic exclusion. Due
  account will be taken of their socioeconomic and cultural peculiarities. The Action Plan's three specific
  objectives-two      of them on the side of service demand, one on the side of service supply-are:        (a)
  Empower and strengthen the capacities of women producers as customers requiring services enabling
  them to improve their agribusinesses; (b) Strengthen the organizational and entrepreneurial capacities of
  women producers; and (c) Promote a supply of services appropriate for working with women producers,
  including women producer leaders as suppliers of services. By empowering and strengthening women
' producers as demanders of specialized innovation services, the project will increase their organizational
  and entrepreneurial capacities as a way to improve their agribusinesses, their income, and, ultimately,
  their well being. For this to be possible, it i s necessary to foster the supply of quality services using
  methodologies appropriate for this target population, ensuring that technologies are adopted, information
  i s handled and management i s improved as required by the women producers as beneficiaries. Women
  producer leaders may assume the role of quality service suppliers themselves, provided they have the
  necessary capacities.
  The empowerment and strengthening of women producer organizations will also help gain for the latter
. members' democratic attention to their needs anddemands, within a context of democratic insertion into
  their respective communities' political life for joint work with their male counterparts. Fulfilling these
  objectives will require of the Project to perform actions from a gender equity perspective. The focus will
  be on strengthening the members of families, groups and organizations in accordance with their specific
  conditions within their gender relations systems, so as to build integrated democratic spaces and solid
  families providing the institutional foundation for rural development. In this sense, support for women
  producer organizations must never lose sight either of its ultimate purpose and temporary status, or of the
  principles of integrationanddemocratic representation.

  4.2 Action PlanPolicy Guidelines
  The implementation of the Rural Women's Organizations Action Plan with a gender equity perspective
  requires takinginto account the followingpolicy guidelines:
  a) Facilitate and support the participation of women-organized       or not-in  the Project's benefits, using
       a gender and human development approach, and an equity perspective;
  b) Recognize that group and individual participation and growth of women's organizations is a process
       to be fostered by implementing ongoing capacity building, accompanying and monitoring actions;
  c) Incorporate men-family            members, authorities and communal leaders-into  the actions supporting
       women;
  d) Develop actions to support women and their organizations3', as well as the young, wherever and
       whenever necessary, always seeking gender integration;
  e) Adopt a comprehensive approach to the life of organized women, based on understanding that their
       role as producers cannot be supported without taking into account their roles within the family and
       the community; and
  f) Facilitate group processesof adoption and reinforcement of value conducive to human development,
       such as solidarity, reciprocity, honesty and hard work, as a basis for strengthening capacities and
       sustainablelocal development.
  4.3 Actions andStrategiesProposed for the Project's Indirect Intervention
  What follows is a description of actions and strategies proposed in the study for the preparation of the
   Rural Women's Organizations Action Plan. The recommendations regarding the Project's contribution at
  the indirect intervention level are complemented with references to the cultural and socioeconomic milieu
  inwhich women producers operate.



   30Also known as positive discrimination or affirmative action.


                                                                 94

Objective 1: Organized women acquire the knowledge required to improve their opportunities to
participate. Actions: (a) Build their capacities in terms of ability to improve their roles and family
relationships; (b) Build their capacities in terms of ability to take care of their own health; (c) Buildtheir
production and marketing capacities and (d) Build their effective organization and democratic leadership
capacities.
Strategy: (a) Identify the various types of organizational progress and classify the organizations for
purposes of support strategy implementation; (b) Make arrangements with women group leaders for the
involvement of the various social groups, with a special focus on the youngest; (c) Make arrangements
with educational institutions of recognized standing; (d) Make arrangements with thematically specialized
institutions; and (e) Strengthen capacity building and technical assistance service providers by issuing
gender quality and human development certifications.
Objective 2: Women's organizations access theproductive resources they need to be competitive in terms
of quality and market position. Actions: (a) Help organized women to formalize their land titles and
water access rights; (b) Systematically provide the organizations with timely information on production,
marketing and other opportunities; (c) Look for innovative ways to facilitate the acquisition of animals,
seeds, tools, equipment and other inputs by women's organizations; and (d) Do research on the current
constraints affecting the various types of women peasant organizations, in order to facilitate members'
access to production and managementresources.
Strategy: (a) Ensure coordination with the competent agencies of the sector and the local authorities, and
make arrangements with them for training their officials on gender and human development issues; and
(b) Implement short applied research actions through agreements with higher education institutions and
professionals from local NGOs.
Objective 3: Women's organizations engage in direct relations with other organizations from the modern
sector. Actions: (a) Supply information on the various types of organizations they may do business with,
including exact contact data; (b) Inform and instruct organized women on an ongoing basis regardingthe
laws and rules governing their production activity; (c) Invite women's organizations to meetings, fairs,
seminars, etc. where they can enter direct relations with organizations from the modem sector; and (d)
create and update on an ongoing basis a database containing information on ruralwomen's organizations,
their products, skills and condition; and make it available to entrepreneurial organizations operating in the
agricultural sector.
Strategy: (a) Make sure local authorities and leaders are aware of the support expected of them for
women's organizations. Inthat context, it will be useful to raise their awareness of gender relations and
human development; (b) Strengthen the democratic leadership of women leaders by means of workshops
designed to promote their personal leadership, and accompany those processes on an ongoing basis; (c)
Make arrangements for exporters' organizations, business associations, financial agencies, etc. within the
sector to put the resources offered by women's organizations to best use; and (d) Stipulate agreements
with specialized local institutions (ONGs, universities) providing for capacity building, information
facilitation, awareness raising of authorities and leaders. Make the gender quality and human
development certification a part of such agreements.
Objective 4: Organized women receive recognition and support from their relations and social milieu.
Actions: (a) Hold periodic workshops on human and family relationships for organized women's family
members; (b) Strengthen organized women's personal and group processes by holding periodic
workshops on human and family relationships and leadership; (c) Involve all family members on
domestic role issues; and (d) Support the community's mixed-gender organizations, as well as women'
organizations, in the production of services supporting the domestic role, such as the supply of basic
information on how to runa home, to look after small children, old people, etc.
Strategy: (a) Establish agreements with local institutions specialized in quality capacity building on this
subject; (b) Consult with women in the organizations and their relations on their domestic problems and




                                                      95

needs; and (c) Discuss the values of human development with communal authorities, and foster activities
on their part supportingwomen's domestic role and organization..
Objective 5: Organized women and their organizations take part in identifying the community's
challenges andpotential strengths, and in communal decision making. Actions: (a) Use capacity building
to enhance the role of communal participation by organized women; (b) Raise the awareness of male
authorities and leaders of the importance of stimulating and supporting women's and women
organizations' participation in communal planning and management; (c) Make arrangements with
communal authorities for the representation of women's organizations in the community's decision
making process; and (d) Foster a close connection between women's organizations and mixed-gender
organizations within the community, and work for the former to build mixed-gender spaces in their
activities.
Strategy: (a) Wherever possible, use specialized organizations and/or professionals certified in gender
quality and human development in the implementation of awareness raising and capacity building
actions-whether    for men or women; (b) Keep communal authorities and leaders informed on what will
be done to facilitate women's representation in decision making processes on issues affecting the
community, and commit them to the process; and (c) Encourage young, single women to participateinthe
processes strengthening women's organizations in communal management. This will involve promoting
their participation in adult, family and organizational spaces, as well as providing them with incentives
(including certificates or other forms of individualrecognition).
As the next section will show, some of these recommendations have been built into the design of the
competitive fund for organized women producers. We are referring, inparticular, to the recommendations
relating to capacity building on market and agribusiness issues for women producers; capacity building
and certification on gender quality for service providers; promotion of young people's involvement. The
remaining recommendations will be implemented as a part of the indirect intervention actions to be
developed by the Project with the support of the relevant public and private institutions.

4.4 Scenarios for inviting women to submit proposals and the cross-cutting promotion of the gender
approach
Inconjunction with the implementationof a gender approach through the RuralWomen's Organizations
Action Plan, a set of scenarios needs to be considered in which differentiated measures will be applied
depending on the soundness of the women's organizations, or women's democratic and equitable
participation in the case of mixed-gender organizations, and the conditions for the implementation of the
fundingwindow. Also, a general strategy is proposed for cases in which the Project is intervening or has
intervened in support of producers' organizations as a result of the implementation of Phase I.Inthis way,
the diversity of situations surrounding the women's organization and the women's conditions for
participating will be taken into account.
a) In those cases in which the Project i s already intervening or has intervened in support of producers'
    organizations in general as a result of the activities carried out inPhase I,
                                                                               the results achieved will need
    to be strengthenedby promoting an equitable participationof women and young adults in the activities
    and benefits of the subprojects;
b) Inthe case of well-run women's organizations, which are already generating income and which seek to
    improve their profitability and growth, these will be invited to participate in the competitive fund
    created in the framework of this Action Plan. Using profile and proposal evaluation criteria, the
    incorporation of young adults in specific subproject activities will be promoted;
c) In the case of mixed-gender organizations that are democratic and strong, the possibility will be
    evaluated of allocating equitable participation quotas per gender in order that such organizations may
    enter the fundingwindow. Inthis case, the mixed-gender organizations will be trained, usingstrategies
    differentiated per gender, in family and community relationships with equity and human development;
    this training will be incorporated into the activities prior to implementation of the subproject.



                                                      96

     Subsequently, during the execution of the subproject, similar activities will be conducted for
     reinforcement purposes. The incorporation of young adults in specific subproject activities will be
     promotedusingprofile and proposal evaluation criteria; and
d) Inother scenarios in which the conditions are still insufficient interms of the degree of development of
     the women's organizations, support will be given to build the capabilities of these organizations,
     seeking to provide follow-up on their processes. One contribution will be the provision of integral
     training services under contracts or agreementsas the case may be. These organizations will participate
     inthe fundingwindow from the secondor thirdyear of Phase 11,butnot before then.
Based on these criteria, areas will be identified for the execution of diverse pilot experiences, depending on
the characteristics of the context in which the women's organizations are functioning. Successful cases
have previously been identified in other interventions, in different parts of the country, such as: i)
Development of the Puno-Cusco Corridor Project; ii)El Centro de Investigacidn, Educacidn y Desarrollo
(CIED) in Cajamarca; iii)SEPAR Seivicios Educativos Promocidn y Apoyo Rural in the Mantaro Valley;
iv) The Carob-tree project Consolidacidn y Validacidn del Manejo Integral de 10s Bosques Secos de la
Costa Norte del Peru` [Consolidation and Validation of the Integral Management of the Dry Forests in the
Northern Coastal Area of Peru]; v) Proyecto de Manejo de Recursos Naturales en la Sierra Sur del pais -
MARENASS [Project for the Management of Natural Resources in the Southern Andean Highland Area of
Peru -     MARENASS]; vi) CEDAP (Centro de Desarrollo Agropecuario) [Agriculture and Livestock
Development Center -CEDAP] inAyacucho; vii) CEDECUM inPuno; viii) Rural Roads Program; ix) The
experience of the Fondo Contravalor Peru'-Canadd.

financing of the subprojects to be executedby the women producer beneficiaries.

8. Planfor Disseminationof the RuralWomen's OrganizationsAction Plan
Inorder to ensurethat the RuralWomen's Organizations Action Planreachesthe mainstakeholders
involved, the following guidelines have been drawn up for its dissemination:
a. Dissemination of the Action Plan through the INCAGRO web site and the MINAG web site,
    ensuring that updated information is posted in these virtual media about the activities and events
    conducted by the ATF inconnection with this plan.
b. Disseminationof the Action Planthrough events introducingINCAGRO and the ATF inthe regions,
    both former ones and new ones, where the Project will be working in Phase 11, in coordination with
    the Regional Government authorities, local authorities, and representatives of the agencies and
    institutions of the public and private sector.
c. Disseminationof the Action Plan at the Local Fora to be conducted in the regions in which Phase I1
    will be operating.




                                                   99

d. Dissemination of the Action Plan through especially trained promoters, sensitized with regard to
    gender equity, conversant in the indigenous languages predominant in the areas where the invitation
    to participate in the funding window will be issued (Quechua, Aymara and other native languages);
    these promoters will travel to the farmer communities, native communities, and other'local groups in
    these areas in order to make public the Plan's objectives, the characteristics of the funding window
    competition, and requirements for participationtherein.
e. Dissemination of the Action Plan through a brochure to be distributed in local newspapers in the
    regions in which the Project i s to promote the Plan, explaining its goals and expected results.
f. Hiringof the services of a social communicator to design a communication strategy in the regions
    where the Project will work in Phase 11,particularly with regard to the execution of activities in the
    framework of the Action Plan.
The approximate total cost of the Dissemination Plan is US$ 56,770, corresponding to the Promotionand
Disseminationitem in the budget contained inTable A.10.1.B.3.



Annex 10.2 The Environmental Impact Analysis and ProposedAction Plan
EA Category: B
1.Strategic Focusof the Project
The APL second phase Project sets out the expansion of a decentralized system of technological
innovation with the final objective to contribute to the generation and dissemination of sustainable and
environmentally safe technologies. This implies the involvement of innovation agents in a sustainable
strategy of natural resources use in all actions involved in technological changes in the improvement of
yield and competitiveness of farmingand forest-basedactivities.
Inthe first stage, the Project has been successful: (a) in introducing the criteria of environmentalimpacts
as a requirement of the qualification of technical quality in the selection of proposals on subprojects; (b)
to give special attention to the search for changes in agricultural practices and development of new
practices that have a significant effect on the natural resources of the system of production in different
agro-ecologies; and (c) to collect basic data on effects and environmental impacts of a critical number of
subprojects of technological innovation, identifying reasons of internalization of practices to mitigate
potentialenvironmentalimpacts duringthe technological adaptation processes.
An Environmental Mitigation Action Plan to mitigate potential adverse effects of the Phase I1Project,
since the latter through its different components, would play an important role in the technological
innovation process such as:       (a) expansion of technological innovation at the national level and
incorporation of new regions where the natural resources could undergo potential positive or negative
effects;  (b) changes in farming practices and the use of technological or consumer goods that are
associatedwith natural resources such as water, land, fauna and flora, and the need for sustainableuse of
fragile mixed farming systems; and (c) changes in livestock management associated with better natural
resource management and biodiversity conservation, implying a need for new adjustments in the
production systems.
Therefore, the Project while co-financing the services associated with technological changes must also
contribute to inducing the sustainable handlingof natural resources at a decentralized level, with strong
emphasis of such a paradigm in the new regions. The Project must also contribute to the strengthening of
the local management capacity in natural resources use, to encourage yield improvement and
competitiveness based on the generation and dissemination of environmentally safe technologies, and on
stimulating the social recognition of environmentally friendly practices.




                                                     100

Planof Actionon EnvironmentalEvaluationandManagement
The objective of the plan i s to incorporate environmental aspects in all relevant activities and technologies
proposed and financed by the Project and determine the potential impact on the environment prior to and
at time of project formulation to continue during implementation and post-impact analysis. The Phase I1
Project will contribute to the development of sustainable and environmentally safe technologies and to
this endthe following actions areproposed:
1) Drawing up a Study on the MinimumPrior Conditions to be met by the subprojects, and establishing
the operational indicators that will be used as a filter for the approval of the subprojects before the
beginning of the secondphase. This means that the following activities needto be carried out:
     1.1) include in the requisites the minimum operational indicators that the subprojects applying to
     participatein the system should contain; and
     1.2) incorporate into the subproject follow-up and assessment system the indicators by which the
     subprojects are to be measuredand/or evaluated.
2) Draw up a "Methodological Validation Study on Effects and Impacts per type of Subproject,
intervention sites, and impact areas". This should permit, first and foremost, the generation of a standard
instrument for the evaluation of potential environmental risks of new technologies to be introduced. This
involves such tasks as:
     2.1) implementing an Environmental Management Plan that will forecast the direct, indirect, and
     cumulative impacts, and induced risks that could be caused to the different environmental
     components;
     2.2) using representative environmental variables to identify impacts and justify the scale, level of
     resolution and volume of data, replicability of the information, definition of impact thresholds, and
     identification of both critical or inadmissible impacts and positive impacts;
     2.3) awareness of the existing regulations and national standards and geographic area of intervention;
     and
     2.4) hiring an expert (using Project Component funds) to carry out the validation study and resulting
     EnvironmentalManagement Plan for use as a managementtool and for subsequentM&E.
3) Promote by means of the calls for grant proposals and by conducting local and regional fora, an
environmental awareness-raising process; requiringthose entities that take part in the system to carry out
a positive environmental management process (total quality) on the contents of their processes and end
products, with emphasis on aspects that have the greatest impact on the environment, and which will be
given greater weight inthe selection process.
This process implies carrying out awareness-raising andtraining activities both within the Project and for
the public, including:
     3.1) training the Project personnel, especially the personnel for the new regions, in the knowledge,
     management and application of the minimum environmental indicators and their use in M&E
     activities;
     3.2) extending training activities to the personnel of the organizations executing subprojects financed
     with Project funds; and
     3.3) preparing the pertinent training and extension materials.
4) Creating award mechanisms whereby the Project will be able to reward the participatingorganizations
whose proposals contribute efficiently to: improving the rational use of natural resources; the
implementation of good farming practices; the implementation of management plans for forests and
natural forest land; improvement of irrigation infrastructure; and the economic valuation of natural
resourcesinterms of their diversity and demand. This activity would include:
     4.1) earmarking about 20% of the Component 3 budget to be spent on prizes for subprojects meeting
     the environmental objectives of the Project strategy.
     4.2) hiringan expert to evaluate the environmentalquality of the subprojects.
5) Entering into agreements with organizations that are competent in forecasting and controlling
environmental risks, in order to help:


                                                      101

    5.1) improve the regulatory framework for technological innovation; and
    5.2) coordinate with the national regulatory institutions assistance for compliance with, or the
    application of, existing environmental regulations.
6) Periodic evaluation of the Environmental Management Plans drawn up by the subprojects, which
should include, among other aspects:
    6.1) the development of capacities of executors and producers;
    6.2) the action plan, basedon the results of minimumindicators, duly scheduled and budgeted; and
    6.3) the drafting of specific reports on the advances of the Environmental Management Plans and
    compliance therewith.
7) Conducting mid-term and final evaluations of the results achieved by applying the technologies
proposed by the Subprojects, in order to assess the environmental benefits obtained, taking into account
the quality of the processes and the end product in connection with the business plans. It will be
necessaryto:
    7.1) hire two impact assessment experts, one for the mid-term phase and the other for the final phase,
    to perform the evaluation of effects and impacts of the proposed technologies, as well as the
    environmental benefits obtained, this evaluation i s to be madeup to 2007; and
    7.2) produce evaluation reports in each phase, the results of which will be systematized and inserted
    into the management information system.
8) Updating and dissemination of current regulations with regard to natural resource management in
terms of quality management, total quality, social impact, biosafety, and others.
9) Subprojects should conduct environmental valuation exercises (costhenefit), and for this purpose it i s
proposed to:
    9.1) encourage the executing organizations to conduct an environmental valuation exercise of the
    benefits they propose to obtain through technologies already implementedor to be implemented; and
    9.2) facilitate the training of experts in environmental economic valuation, so that they will be able to
    assist any subprojects requiring help. This would be cost-shared with the organization(s) conducting
    the exercise.
10) Extracting from the subprojects all possible lessons that may suggest ways of improving the
legislation on environmental issues, interms of both planningand management of natural resources.
The legislation that serves as a support for the proper management of the Subprojects should also be
known and taken into account in the subprojects' M&E process. It should also be taken into account in
the process of evaluation of the Technical Evaluation Panel (TEP) during the funding window
competitions, so that considerations of regulatory aspects may also be used in the scoring and final
qualification.

Financing the Action Plan for Environmental Risk Prevention

World Bank, Matching Funds from the Peruvian Government, and Strategic Partnerships. The funds
from the first two sources are equivalent to the resources of the Project for the generation of policies,
information, and quality of the innovation services which will enable the Action Plan to be executed. The
resources to be contributed by the partnerships are basically the co-financing of the subprojects to be
executed by the producers receiving the services as beneficiaries.

          Planfor the Disseminationof the ActionPlanfor EnvironmentalRiskPrevention

Inorder to ensure that the Action Plan reaches the main stakeholdersinvolved, the following guidelines
have been drawn up for its dissemination:
a). dissemination of the minimumoperational indicators to be contained by the Subprojects applying to
    participateinthe systemthrough the Project INCAGRO web site and the Training Manual;
b). dissemination of the EnvironmentalManagement Planduringthe training process and inthe drawing
    up of proposals for the Subprojects selectedby the ATF andthe FDSE;
c). dissemination of the results in terms of progress made in the Environmental Management Plans, and
    compliance with these Plans, at events to introduce INCAGRO and the Competitive Funding
    mechanism both in the former regions and in the new regions where the ATP and the PROFOSE will
    be working in Phase 11, in coordination with the Regional Government authorities, local authorities,
    and representatives of the agenciesand institutions of the public andprivate sector;
d). dissemination of the Action Plan for Environmental Risk Prevention at the Local and Regional Fora
    to be conducted by the ATF and the FDSEinthe new regions where PhaseI1will be operating;
e). dissemination of the Action Plan through a brochure to be distributed in the Decentralized Units in
    which the ATF and the PROFOSE will promote this plan, explaining their goals and objectives and
    the expected results.
